قسمة
مجانب
مباين
جهة
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's adjacency or separateness can be determined by whether He is pointable by sense.
Correction: This leads to circularity because God's sensible perceptibility is itself what needs to be proven, making the proof depend on the conclusion.

Checkpoint

A) That the determinant is contingency
B) That the determinant is sensible perceptibility
C) That the determinant is existence
D) That the determinant is a shared concept between substance and accident

Key Takeaway
The determinant for dividing into adjacent and separate cannot be sensible perceptibility because that would require proving what is in dispute, resulting in circular reasoning.

The Eighth Objection: Determinant Present but Judgment Absent
The objector argues that the determinant may exist yet the judgment fails due to lack of receptivity or a missing condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a determinant (cause) may exist yet its judgment (effect) may not follow due to the subject's lack of receptivity or a missing condition.

Core Idea
A cause is not enough; the subject must be receptive for the judgment to follow.

The eighth objection challenges the argument from division by pointing out that even if a cause exists, the effect may not occur if the subject is not receptive or a condition is missing. The objector uses the example of God's life without passions to illustrate this point. This lesson unpacks that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the complete Arabic passage and its literal translation. Allow the objection to be heard without filtering.
Notice the structure: general principle, two reasons, example, then application.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage states that judgment may be absent for two reasons. Find these two reasons in the text.
They are: subject not receptive, and condition missing.
3
Examine the example
The objector gives the example of a living being and God. Understand how this example supports the objection.
Life in God does not produce passions because God's essence is not receptive.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a question: 'So why do you say it is not possible here?' Consider how this challenges the previous argument from division.
The objection aims to show that the argument is not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثامن : إنه قد يحصل المقتضى ، ولكن يتخلف عنه الحكم . إما لأن المحل غير قابل ، وإما لأن الشرط فائت، ألا ترى أن كون الحي حيـا يصحح قبول الشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة والجهل والموت . ثم إنه تعالى حي ، مع أن شيئا من هذه الأحكام لا يصح عليه ، إما لأن ذاته تعالى غير قابلة لهذه الأحكام ، أو لأن قبول هذه الأحكام موقوف على شرط ، يمتنع حصوله في حق الله تعالى . فلم قلتم : لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر ههنا كذلك ؟
Eighth question: It may be that the determinant occurs, but the judgment is absent. Either because the subject is not receptive, or because the condition is missing. Do you not see that a living being's being alive makes it receptive to desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, ignorance, and death. Then God is alive, yet none of these judgments are permissible for Him, either because His essence is not receptive to these judgments, or because receptivity to these judgments depends on a condition whose occurrence in the case of God is impossible. So why do you say that it is not possible that the matter here be similar?
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This eighth objection points out a general principle: the cause of a judgment may be present, but the judgment may still be absent due to either the subject's lack of receptivity or a missing condition. The objector gives the example of life: a living being is receptive to passions and death, but God is alive yet not subject to any of those. Therefore, even if the determinant for the division (existence or something) is present in God, the judgment (being divided into adjacent or separate) may not apply because God's essence is not receptive or a condition is missing.

Key Terms

حكم
محل قابل
شرط
حي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the cause (determinant) is present, the effect (judgment) must necessarily occur.
Correction: This objection shows that the effect may not occur if the subject is not receptive or a condition is missing.

Checkpoint
In the eighth question, what example is given to show that the cause can exist without the effect?
A) Substance and accident
B) God's being alive without having passions
C) Contingency and necessity
D) Sensible and intelligible

Key Takeaway
The objection demonstrates that even when a cause is present, the effect may not follow due to lack of receptivity or missing conditions, so the argument from division is not decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Doubt of Raising Hands to Heaven
Sanusi refutes the doubt using the relativity of direction and the nature of heaven as source of blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why raising hands to heaven does not imply that God occupies a spatial direction.

Core Idea
Raising hands to heaven is about blessings, not God's location.

The doubt that raising hands to heaven proves God is in a direction 'above' is a common anthropomorphic argument. Sanusi responds by explaining the reason behind the gesture and showing the relativity of direction. This lesson unpacks his refutation, which upholds divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
The sixth doubt claims that because Muslims raise hands to heaven when supplicating, God must be located in a direction above. Recognize this as an anthropomorphic assumption.
The doubt is about the directionality implied by the gesture.
2
Examine the real reason
Sanusi explains that heaven is the source of blessings – lights, water, and good things descend from it. People naturally turn towards what benefits them.
3
Consider the relativity of 'above'
The direction we call 'above' changes depending on location. What is above for one country is to the right or left for another. This undermines the idea of a fixed divine direction.
Example: the sun at the beginning of Cancer shines straight down wells in Mecca and Medina but not in northern countries.
4
See the absurd consequence
If God were literally above one country, He would be to the side of another country, contradicting the belief that He is above all.
5
Recognize the final absurdity
Claiming God is above all countries would force Him to be a sphere encompassing the earth – an absurd conclusion that no rational person accepts.
Thus, raising hands to heaven does not imply divine location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السادسة وهي رفع الأيدي إلى السماء . فتقول : إن رفع الأيدي إلى السماء إنما كان لأجل أن السماء منزل البركات والخيرات . فإن الأنوار إنما تنزل منها والماء إنما ينزل منها وإذا ألف الإنسان حصول الخيرات من جانب ، مال طبعه إليه ، فهذا المعنى هو الذي أوجب رفع الأيدي إلى السماء وتوجه الأبصار إليها . والذي يدل عليه : أن الموضع الذي من السماء : يكون فوقا لأهل بلد ، يكون يمينا لأهل بلد آخر ، ويسارا لأهل بلدة ثالثة . والدليل عليه : أن الشمس إذا كانت في أول السرطان ، فإن الضوء ينزل إلى أعماق الآبار في مكة والمدينة ويضيء جميع جوانب البشر ، وليس الأمر كذلك في البلاد الشمالية ، وهذا مما يعرفه جميع العوام الذين سافروا في البلاد . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن أهل كل بلد ، يرفعون أيديهم إلى الجانب الذي هو فوقهم ، فإن كان إله العالم كائنا في الموضع الذي هو فوق هذه البلدة ، لزم أن يكون [؟] لا فوق البلدة الثانية ، بل إما على يمينها ، أو على يسارها ، أو جهة أخرى وحينئذ لا يلزم من رفع الأيدي إلى السماء كون الإله فوق ذلك الموضع . وأما إن قلنا : إن إله العالم كائن فوق جميع البلاد فهذا القول إنما يتم لو كان إله العالم كرة محيطة بجميع الأرض ، وحينئذ يرجع حاصل هذا القول إلى أن إله العالم فلك من الأفلاك المحيطة بالأرض ، ومعلوم أن العاقل لا يقول ذلك
And the answer to the sixth doubt, which is raising hands to heaven. So we say: Raising hands to heaven was only because heaven is the place of descent of blessings and good things. For lights descend from it, and water descends from it. And when a person becomes accustomed to receiving good things from a direction, his nature inclines towards it. This meaning is what necessitated raising hands to heaven and directing sight towards it. What indicates this is that the direction which is from heaven: it is above for the people of one country, it becomes right for the people of another country, and left for the people of a third country. Evidence for this is that when the sun is at the beginning of Cancer, light descends to the depths of wells in Mecca and Medina and illuminates all sides of the well [or 'human'], and it is not so in northern countries. This is known by all common people who have traveled. When this is established, we say: The people of each country raise their hands to the direction that is above them. If the God of the world were located in the direction that is above this country, then it would be necessary that He is not above the second country, but rather to its right, or to its left, or another direction. And then raising hands to heaven does not necessitate that God is above that position. And if we say: The God of the world is above all countries, this statement is only complete if the God of the world were a sphere encompassing all the earth. And then the conclusion of this statement is that God is one of the spheres surrounding the earth, and it is known that a rational person does not say that.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage refutes the doubt that raising hands to heaven implies God is in a particular direction above. The answer explains that heaven is the source of blessings, so people naturally turn to it. Moreover, the concept of 'above' is relative; different countries have different directions as above. If God were literally above one country, He would be to the side of another. To claim God is above all countries would require Him to be a sphere surrounding the earth, which is absurd. Thus, raising hands to heaven does not imply anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
رفع الأيدي إلى السماء
جهة
فوق
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Raising hands to heaven proves that God is literally above us in a physical direction.
Correction: The gesture is due to heaven being the source of blessings, and the concept of 'above' is relative; thus no physical direction for God is implied.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the response to the sixth doubt?
A) Heaven is the direction of God
B) Raising hands to heaven is a cultural habit unrelated to God
C) The concept of 'above' is relative and does not prove God's location
D) God is a sphere surrounding the earth

Key Takeaway
Raising hands to heaven reflects the human inclination toward the source of blessings, not God's location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in dua, do you ever catch yourself thinking that Allah is physically 'up there'? How does Sanusi's argument about the relativity of 'above' challenge that assumption? What will you do to correct that thought in your next prayer?
?
Sanusi points out that human nature inclines toward the source of blessings. Where in your life do you mistakenly direct your heart toward the material source (e.g., money, people, status) rather than the true Giver? What specific step can you take today to redirect that inclination?
?
The absurd conclusion that God would have to be a sphere if He were literally above all—does this make you truly feel the transcendence of Allah? How can you train yourself to avoid imagining Allah in any form or place?

Answer to the Sixth Objection: Raising Hands to Heaven
The author refutes the argument that raising hands to heaven proves God's location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why raising hands to heaven does not imply God's physical location, using the relative nature of direction and the natural explanation of seeking blessings.

Core Idea
Above is a relative direction, not a fixed abode for God.

Objectors sometimes point to the practice of raising hands in supplication as evidence that God is located in the direction of heaven. The author provides a twofold rebuttal: first, raising hands is a natural response to heaven being the source of blessings; second, the concept of 'above' is relative and cannot be pinned to an absolute location for God. This lesson unpacks that logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the author's structure: an alternative explanation followed by a relative direction argument.
2
Understand the alternative explanation
The author argues that raising hands is due to heaven being the source of blessings (rain, light). This psychological inclination does not require God to be spatially present there.
This refutes the objection by offering a non-spatial reason for the practice.
3
Grasp the relativity of 'above'
The author uses the example of the sun at the beginning of Cancer to show that 'above' is relative to location. The same celestial point is above one country but to the side of another, so raising hands cannot fix God's location.
The final absurdity: if God were above all countries, He would be a sphere encompassing the earth, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السادسة وهي رفع الأيدي إلى السماء . فتقول : إن رفع الأيدي إلى السماء إنما كان لأجل أن السماء منزل البركات والخيرات . فإن الأنوار إنما تنزل منها والماء إنما ينزل منها وإذا ألف الإنسان حصول الخيرات من جانب ، مال طبعه إليه ، فهذا المعنى هو الذي أوجب رفع الأيدي إلى السماء وتوجه الأبصار إليها . والذي يدل عليه : أن الموضع الذي من السماء : يكون فوقا لأهل بلد ، يكون يمينا لأهل بلد آخر ، ويسارا لأهل بلدة ثالثة . والدليل عليه : أن الشمس إذا كانت في أول السرطان ، فإن الضوء ينزل إلى أعماق الآبار في مكة والمدينة ويضيء جميع جوانب البشر ، وليس الأمر كذلك في البلاد الشمالية ، وهذا مما يعرفه جميع العوام الذين سافروا في البلاد . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن أهل كل بلد ، يرفعون أيديهم إلى الجانب الذي هو فوقهم ، فإن كان إله العالم كائنا في الموضع الذي هو فوق هذه البلدة ، لزم أن يكون إله العالم كائنا لا فوق البلدة الثانية ، بل إما على يمينها ، أو على يسارها ، أو جهة أخرى . وحينئذ لا يلزم من رفع الأيدي إلى السماء كون الإله فوق ذلك الموضع . وأما إن قلنا : إن إله العالم كائن فوق جميع البلاد فهذا القول إنما يتم لو كان إله العالم كرة محيطة بجميع الأرض ، وحينئذ يرجع حاصل هذا القول إلى أن إله العالم فلك من الأفلاك المحيطة بالأرض ، ومعلوم أن العاقل لا يقول ذلك
The answer to the sixth objection, which is the raising of hands towards heaven, is as follows: The raising of hands towards heaven was only because heaven is the place of descent of blessings and good things. For lights descend from it, and water descends from it. When a person becomes accustomed to obtaining good things from a direction, his nature inclines towards it. This meaning is what necessitated raising hands to heaven and directing eyes towards it. What indicates this is that the location that is heaven is above for the people of one country, to the right for the people of another country, and to the left for the people of a third country. The proof of this is that when the sun is at the beginning of Cancer, light descends to the depths of wells in Mecca and Medina and illuminates all sides of the well, but this is not the case in northern countries. This is known by all common people who have traveled in countries. Once this is established, we say: The people of every country raise their hands towards the direction that is above them. If the God of the world existed in the direction that is above that particular country, then it would be necessary that the God of the world would not be above the second country, but rather to its right, left, or another direction. In that case, raising hands to heaven does not necessitate that God is above that location. If we say that the God of the world is above all countries, this statement would only be valid if the God of the world were a sphere encompassing all the earth. Then the outcome of this statement reduces to the claim that the God of the world is one of the spheres surrounding the earth, and it is known that a rational person does not say that.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage answers the sixth objection, which argues that since Muslims raise their hands towards heaven in supplication, God must be located in that direction (above). The author refutes this by providing an alternative explanation: heaven is the source of blessings (rain, light), so people naturally turn towards it. He then uses physical evidence that 'above' is relative: the same point in the sky is above one country but to the side of another. He gives the example of the sun at the beginning of Cancer; its light reaches deep wells in Mecca and Medina from directly overhead, but not in northern countries. Thus, if God were literally above one country, He would not be above another. The only way to be above all countries is to be a sphere encompassing the earth, which is absurd because it would make God a celestial sphere (falak). Therefore, raising hands does not prove that God is in a direction.

البركات
الجهة
الفلك
رفع الأيدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Raising hands to heaven in supplication proves that God is located in a spatial direction (above).
Correction: Raising hands is a natural response to heaven being the source of blessings, and 'above' is a relative direction that cannot pin God to a location.

Checkpoint

A) That the sun is a sign of God's power.
B) That the same direction can be above one place and not above another.
C) That wells in Mecca are deeper.

Key Takeaway
The practice of raising hands does not prove God's spatial location because the direction 'above' is relative and the practice has a natural explanation rooted in the search for blessings.

The Wisdom of Gradual Instruction in Tawhid
The author explains why scriptural language sometimes uses anthropomorphic expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scripture sometimes uses anthropomorphic expressions and how this reflects a wise pedagogical method.

Core Idea
Scripture uses anthropomorphic language to gradually guide common minds to divine transcendence.

The seventh objection asks why the Quran contains verses that seem to describe God in human terms. The author answers by highlighting the prophetic method of gradual instruction, tailored to the capacity of the audience. This passage reveals the wisdom behind such language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: da'wa, tanzih, tashbih.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the meanings of الدعوة (call), التصريح بالتنزيه (explicit affirmation of transcendence), التشبيه (anthropomorphism, resemblance), العوام (common people).
3
Understand the reasoning
Notice how the author argues that explicit tanzih is not accepted by common minds; thus, it was preferable to include words that suggest tashbih while also hinting at absolute transcendence.
This is a pedagogical strategy, not a theological compromise.
4
Reflect on the balance
Consider how this principle applies to specific Quranic verses about God's attributes.
The passage does not give examples, but you can think of verses about 'hand' or 'face' of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السابعة : هو أن الدعوة للخلق إلى الحق يجب أن تكون واقعة على أحسن الوجوه وأقربها إلى الصلاح ، ولما كان التصريح بالتنزيه مما لا تقبله عقول العوام ، لا جرم كان الأولى اشتمال الدعوة على ألفاظ توهم التشبيه مع التنبيه على كلمات تدل على التنزيه المطلق
The answer to the seventh objection is that the call (da'wa) of creation to the truth must occur in the best manner and the one closest to what is beneficial. Since explicit affirmation of transcendence (al-tanzih) is not received by the minds of the common people, it was inevitably preferable that the call includes words that suggest anthropomorphism (tashbih) while simultaneously directing attention to words that indicate absolute transcendence.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage answers the seventh objection, which likely challenged that the Quran contains anthropomorphic verses (like God's hand, face, etc.) which seem to imply God is in a direction or has a body. The author argues that the prophetic method (da'wa) must be gradual and appropriate to the audience. Common people cannot immediately grasp pure transcendence (tanzih), so it was wise for scripture to use expressions that carry a hint of anthropomorphism (tashbih, i.e., resemblance), but these are always accompanied by clarifications that point to absolute transcendence. This balances the need to communicate with the masses and preserve God's incomparability.

Key Terms

التصريح بالتنزيه
التشبيه
التنزيه المطلق
العوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anthropomorphic verses prove God has a body or human features.
Correction: The author explains these verses are pedagogical tools for gradual instruction, not literal descriptions of God.

Checkpoint

C) Because tanzih contradicts the Quran.
D) Because the Quran only uses anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
The prophetic method of da'wa utilizes anthropomorphic language only as a stepping stone to lead people to absolute divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you now approach those passages with the trust that they are perfectly suited to guide hearts to absolute transcendence? Commit to a specific practice for reading such verses.

Answer to the Eighth Objection: The Limits of Imagination in Theology
The author refutes an objection against seeing God by showing it relies on imagination, not proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a parity argument to show that rejecting seeing God based on imagination is inconsistent with accepting God's existence beyond imagination.

Core Idea
If imagination can't disprove God's existence, it can't disprove seeing Him.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that denies the possibility of seeing God because it seems impossible to the mind. He cleverly turns the tables: if mere imaginative impossibility were decisive, we would have to reject the very existence of God as described by rational theology. This lesson walks through his argument and its implications for theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Focus on the author's two conditional statements and the parallel he draws.
2
Identify the two conditional arguments
The author presents two possibilities: if mere isti'bad (rational estimation) is sufficient to reject something, then we must also reject God's existence as described by theologians. If it is not sufficient, then the opponents must provide a real proof against seeing God, which they haven't.
Notice the logical structure: either way, the opponent's position is weak.
3
Understand the parity argument
The author draws a parallel: just as theologians have proven a being (God) that is neither inside nor outside the world (contrary to imagination), so too have many people affirmed seeing something not in a direction (likewise contrary to imagination). If one accepts the first, consistency demands considering the second possible.
This is a key dialectical move—it shifts the burden of proof to the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثامنة : إن الكلام في أنه هل تجوز رؤية الله تعالى ؟ سيأتي تقريرها. وبتقدير صحتها فإنا نقول : إن كان مجرد الاستبعاد كافيا ، فلا شك أن الوهم والخيال يستبعد وجود موجود معبود لا داخل العالم ولا خارجه ، فوجب أن يكتفى بهذا القدر من غير حاجة إلى التمسك بالرؤية ، وإن كان غير كافي فنقول . كما أثبتنا موجودا لا في العالم ولا في خارج العالم على خلاف حكم الوهم والخيال ، فكذلك جمع عظيم من الناس أثبتوا رؤية شيء ، لا في الجهة ، فلم قلتم : إن ذلك محال ؟ وما الدليل الذي دل على امتناعه ؟ فإن أحداً من المخالفين لم يذكر فيه شيئاً سوى الاستبعاد . وهذا تمام الأجوبة على شبهات من أثبت الحيز والجهة . والله أعلم بحقائق الأمور
The answer to the eighth objection is that the discussion of whether it is permissible to see God (ru'ya Allah) will come later in its proper place. Assuming the validity of that discussion, we say: If mere impossibility according to rational estimation (al-isti'bad) were sufficient, then there is no doubt that imagination and fancy (al-wahm wa al-khayal) consider impossible the existence of an existent worshipped being that is neither inside the world nor outside it. So it would be obligatory to be satisfied with this amount without needing to hold onto the vision (ru'ya). And if it is not sufficient, then we say: Just as we have established an existent that is not in the world nor outside the world, contrary to the judgment of imagination and fancy, similarly, a great multitude of people have established the vision of something that is not in a direction. So why do you say that is impossible? And what proof is there that indicates its impossibility? For none of the opponents have mentioned anything other than rational estimation (al-isti'bad). This completes the answers to the objections of those who affirm spatial location (al-hayyiz) and direction (al-jiha). And God knows best the realities of things.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage answers the eighth objection, which likely stems from those who argue that God cannot be seen because seeing requires direction and location. The author first notes that the detailed proof for the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya) will come later. But here he gives a preliminary argument: those who refuse to accept that God can be seen because it seems impossible to their minds should also reject the very existence of God as affirmed by rational theology (a being without inside or outside), since that also seems impossible to imagination. If they accept that through rational proof, they should similarly accept the possibility of seeing God without direction, since both transcend imagination. He challenges opponents to provide a real proof beyond mere rejection based on apparent impossibility. This concludes the refutations of those who posit spatial and directional attributes for God.

Key Terms
رؤية الله
الاستبعاد
الوهم والخيال
الجهة
الحيز
المخالفون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage proves the possibility of seeing God.
Correction: The author explicitly says the full proof will come later; here he only gives a preliminary rebuttal to the objection, not a full proof.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the opponents rely on to deny the possibility of seeing God?
A) Quranic verses.
B) Rational proofs.
C) Mere impossibility according to imagination.
D) Consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of seeing God cannot be proven by mere imaginative difficulty; it requires a rational proof, which the opponents have not provided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Eighth Objection on Vision of Allah
Author answers the eighth objection on vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the eighth objection against the vision of Allah by exposing the weakness of the opponent's reliance on implausibility.

Core Idea
If implausibility were proof, then Allah's existence would also be impossible.

The eighth objection claims that seeing Allah is impossible because it would require a direction. The author responds by turning the argument on its head: if mere implausibility were a valid reason to reject something, then the very belief in a transcendent God would be impossible. This lesson examines that powerful counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the two-part argument
The author presents two scenarios: if mere implausibility is sufficient, then the same would apply to Allah's existence; if it is not sufficient, then the opponent must provide a real proof.
These two branches cover all possibilities.
3
Examine the parallel
The author draws a parallel between the opponent's rejection of vision and the acceptance of a spaceless God. This exposes the inconsistency.
4
Understand the conclusion
The opponent has offered only implausibility, not a logical proof. Therefore, their objection is invalid.
This completes the refutation of those who affirm spatial location and direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثامنة : إن الكلام في أنه هل تجوز رؤية الله تعالى ؟ سيأتي تقريرها. وبتقدير صحتها فإنا نقول : إن كان مجرد الاستبعاد كافيا ، فلا شك أن الوهم والخيال يستبعد وجود موجود معبود لا داخل العالم ولا خارجه ، فوجب أن يكتفى بهذا القدر من غير حاجة إلى التمسك بالرؤية ، وإن كان غير كافي فنقول . كما أثبتنا موجودا لا في العالم ولا في خارج العالم على خلاف حكم الوهم والخيال ، فكذلك جمع عظيم من الناس أثبتوا رؤية شيء ، لا في الجهة ، فلم قلتم : إن ذلك محال ؟ وما الدليل الذي دل على امتناعه ؟ فإن أحداً من المخالفين لم يذكر فيه شيئاً سوى الاستبعاد . وهذا تمام الأجوبة على شبهات من أثبت الحيز والجهة . [ والله أعلم بحقائق الأمور ] .
And the answer to the eighth objection: The discussion about whether it is permissible to see Allah, may He be exalted, will come later in its proper place. But assuming it is valid, we say: If mere implausibility were sufficient, then there is no doubt that the imagination and fancy would deem implausible the existence of a worshipped being neither inside the world nor outside it. So it would be necessary to be content with this much without need for recourse to vision. And if it is not sufficient, then we say: just as we have established a being not in the world nor outside the world contrary to the judgment of imagination and fancy, likewise a great multitude of people have established the vision of something not in a direction. So why do you say that is impossible? And what proof indicates its impossibility? For none of the opponents has mentioned anything except implausibility. And this completes the answers to the objections of those who affirm spatial location and direction. [And Allah knows best the realities of things].
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the eighth objection concerning the vision of Allah (ru'ya). The objection likely argued that seeing Allah is impossible because it would require direction or location. The author responds with a two-part argument. First, if mere implausibility (istib'ād) were a valid proof of impossibility, then the very existence of a being who is neither inside nor outside the world would also be deemed implausible by the imagination (wahm) and fancy (khayāl), yet the opponent accepts that existence. So they should be satisfied with that acceptance without needing to reject vision. Second, if mere implausibility is not enough, then the opponent must provide a real proof. The author notes that the opponent has only offered implausibility, not a logical proof. The passage concludes that this completes the refutation of those who affirm spatial location (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha) for Allah.

Objection and Answer: The Void Contaminated by Filth?
Author raises and answers the objection that identifying God with the void implies His contamination by filth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the doctrine of the void as God against the objection of contamination by filth, using the void's immutability and analogy with time.

Core Idea
The void's essence is unchanged by its contents, so God is not contaminated.

This lesson addresses a common objection to the view that God is identical with the void: if God is the void, then He is mixed with filth. The author presents a brief but powerful rebuttal based on the immutability of the void's essence. By examining this exchange, you will learn how the argument uses analogy with time to defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening sentence where the opponent states the problem. Notice it is presented as 'قول من يقول' (the statement of one who says).
The objection is that if God is the void, He would be mixed with filth.
2
Understand the dismissal
The author calls this objection weak (كلام ضعيف). Identify the reason: the objector assumes that mixing implies change, but the void does not change.
The core principle is that only things that are affected by contact are contaminated.
3
Examine the analogy
Read the analogy with time: God exists with all events in time without deficiency. The same applies to place.
This shows that presence in space does not entail impurity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the objection is invalid because it relies on a false premise about changeability.
The void's immutability is the key defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول من يقول : إنه على هذا التقدير تكون ذاته مخالطة للقاذورات . فهو كلام ضعيف . لأن هذا إنما يصير إذا تأثر ذلك الشيء بمخالطة غيره (1) والفضاء لا تتغير ذاته ولا حقيقته بحسب ما يحصل فيه البتة ، ألا ترى أنه لا نزاع في أنه تعالى موجود () مع كل الحوادث ، ولم يلزم من كونه تعالى مع هذه الحوادث بسبب الوقت والزمان : نقص في ذاته . فكذلك لا يلزم من كونه تعالى معها بحسب الجهة والمكان : حصول نقص فيه .
As for the statement of one who says: on this supposition, His essence would be mixed with filth. That is weak speech. Because this would only happen if that thing is affected by mixing with something else, and the void's essence and reality do not change at all according to what occurs in it. Do you not see that there is no dispute that He, the Most High, exists with all events, and His being with these events due to time and occasion does not necessitate a deficiency in His essence. Similarly, His being with them according to direction and place does not necessitate the occurrence of deficiency in Him.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage addresses a common objection to the doctrine that the void is God: if God is the void that contains everything, then He would be in contact with filth and impurities, which is unbecoming of divinity. The answer denies that this follows. It argues that a thing is only affected by contact if it is susceptible to change. The void, however, does not change its essence or reality due to what occurs within it. The proponents draw an analogy: God, according to orthodox belief, is present with all events in time without being affected or diminished. Similarly, His being present in all places and directions does not entail any deficiency. Thus, the objection is dismissed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical presence implies physical contamination, so if God is the void, He would be contaminated by filth.
Correction: Contamination only occurs if the entity is affected by its environment; the void's essence is immutable, so it is not contaminated. Moreover, God's presence in time does not cause deficiency, so presence in space does not either.

Checkpoint

A) By saying that filth does not exist in the void.
B) By arguing that the void's essence is immutable and unaffected by its contents.
C) By claiming that God is not actually present in places of filth.
D) By stating that the objection is not worth considering.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God, if identified with the void, would be contaminated by filth fails because the void's essence is immutable and unaffected by its contents, just as God's presence in time does not diminish Him.

Two Meanings of 'Infinite' for God's Essence
Author explains two meanings of 'infinite' and applies them to God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'infinite' in Islamic theology and why the correct interpretation for God's essence is negation (salb).

Core Idea
When we say God is infinite, we mean He is free from all quantity and limits, not that He is endlessly extended.

When theologians say that God is 'infinite,' what exactly do they mean? The term can be confusing because it has two distinct senses: negation of limit or endless extension. The author explains both meanings before applying them to God's essence. This lesson clarifies the proper theological meaning for Ahl al-Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three contexts of 'infinite'
The author notes that 'infinite' is used concerning God's essence, attributes, and duration. Focus on the essence context for this lesson.
We will only cover the essence context here.
2
Distinguish the two meanings: negation vs. privation
The author defines two senses: negation (salb) means total absence of quantity; privation ('udul) means endless quantity. Grasp this distinction.
Negation denies any concept of limit; privation affirms quantity but denies its end.
3
See the application to God's essence
The author states that when we say God's essence is infinite, we mean negation: He is free from quantity, volume, position, and location.
This aligns with the Sunni stance on divine transcendence.
4
Contrast with other interpretations
The author mentions that those who believe God has a body interpret 'infinite' in the privative sense, which is rejected.
This highlights the importance of correct interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع في تفسير قولنا : إن الله تعالى غير متناهي اعلم أن هذا اللفظ يطلق في حق الله تعالى تارة بحسب ذاته ، وتارة بحسب دوام وجوده ، وتارة بحسب صفاته ، وقبل الخوض في هذه التفاصيل نقول : إن قولنا للشيء : إنه لا نهاية له قد يذكر بمعنى السلب وقد يذكر بمعنى العدول . أما بمعنى السلب فهو أن يكون المراد : أن الماهية القابلة لمعنى الحد والنهاية مسلوبة عنه . وذلك لأن النهاية عبارة عن طرف مقدار الشيء ومقطعه وإذا كان خاليا عن المقدار وجب خلوه عن طرف المقدار ، لأن عند انتفاء المقدار، يستحيل حصول طرف المقدار وأما بمعنى العدول ، فهو أن يكون الشيء له حجمية ومقدار، إلا أنه لا ينتهي مقداره إلى مقطع وحد ، بل كلما عددته أو قدرته ، فإنك تجد خارجا عنه شيئا آخر منه من غير حاجة إلى التكرير . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا قلنا : إن ذات الله تعالى غير متناهي فإنا نريد به معنى السلب . نعني : أنه منزه عن المقدار والحجمية والوضع والحيز ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فالمعنى الذي لأجله يصح وصف الشيء بأن له حدا أو طرفا ، مسلوب عن ذات الله تعالى، فكأن ذاته غير متناهية بهذا التفسير ، وهذا عند من يقول إنه تعالى منزه عن المقدار والجسمية والوضع والحيز ، أما عند من يقول بهذه المذاهب ، فإنه نفس كونه تعالى غير متناهي ، إما في جميع الجهات ، أو في بعض الجهات بالتفسير الثاني . وهذا هو الكلام في تفسير كونه تعالى غير متناهي ، بحسب الذات .
The Ninth Chapter: On the interpretation of our statement: 'Indeed, Allah the Exalted is infinite.' Know that this expression is used concerning Allah the Exalted sometimes with respect to His essence, sometimes with respect to the perpetuity of His existence, and sometimes with respect to His attributes. Before delving into these details, we say: Our statement about a thing that it has no end may be mentioned in the sense of negation (salb) or in the sense of privation ('udul). As for the sense of negation, it is that the intended meaning is that the quiddity (mahiyya) capable of the meaning of limit and end is negated from it. That is because the end (nihaya) is an expression for the extremity of a thing's quantity and its termination. And if it is devoid of quantity, then it must be devoid of the extremity of quantity, because when quantity is absent, the occurrence of the extremity of quantity is impossible. As for the sense of privation, it is that the thing has volume and quantity, but its quantity does not reach a termination and a limit; rather, whenever you count it or estimate it, you find outside of it something else of it without needing repetition. If you have understood this, then we say: When we say that the essence of Allah the Exalted is infinite, we intend by it the sense of negation. That is, He is transcendent above quantity, volume, position, and location. And if this is the case, then the meaning by which it is valid to describe a thing as having a limit or extremity is negated from the essence of Allah the Exalted. Thus, His essence is infinite by this interpretation. And this is according to those who say that He the Exalted is transcendent above quantity, corporeality, position, and location. As for those who hold these doctrines, then it is the same as His being infinite, either in all directions or in some directions, according to the second interpretation. And this is the discussion on the interpretation of His being infinite with respect to the essence.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of the statement 'Allah the Exalted is infinite' specifically concerning God's essence. The author first notes that the expression 'infinite' is used in three contexts: essence, perpetuity, and attributes. Before detailing those, he clarifies the two possible meanings of 'infinite': negation (salb) and privation ('udul). Negation means that the thing lacks any concept of limit or end because it is free from quantity entirely; thus no extremity can be attributed to it. Privation means the thing does have quantity but it is endless—you can always add more. The author then applies this to God's essence: when we say God's essence is infinite, we mean it in the negative sense—God is transcendent above quantity, volume, position, and location. Therefore, any basis for attributing a limit is negated from God. This interpretation holds for those who affirm God's transcendence. For those who do not (i.e., those who believe God has a body or direction), they interpret it differently, either as infinite in all directions (endless extension) or in some directions, which is the privative sense. The author thus establishes that the correct interpretation for Ahl al-Sunna is the negative one.

حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'infinite' always implies endless extension in space.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'infinite' can also mean total absence of quantity (negation), which applies to God's essence.

Checkpoint

A) It means God has no end in time (eternal).
B) It means God's essence is free from quantity, volume, position, and location (salb).
C) It means God's essence is infinitely extended in all directions.
D) It means God's attributes are unlimited.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is infinite in the sense of negation (salb), meaning He is absolutely free from any quantity, volume, position, or location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refutation of the Claim that God is the Essence of Infinity
Refutes the philosophical claim that God is the essence of infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the abstract concept of infinity cannot be identical to God, and how the author distinguishes between legitimate attribution of infinity to God and the false claim that God is the essence of infinity.

Core Idea
God is described by infinity, but He is not the concept of infinity itself.

This passage tackles a philosophical claim that God is the reality of infinity. The author has already argued about infinite series; now he turns to a specific ancient statement. He shows that if we understand 'infinity' as an attribute of God, it is acceptable, but if we identify God with the abstract concept of infinity, we fall into error because a mental concept cannot be a necessary existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a recap of a previous argument about infinite series.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two possible meanings of the claim 'the god of the world is the reality of infinity.' Find each one and note how the author evaluates them differently.
The correct interpretation: God is described as infinite. The false interpretation: God is identical to the meaning of infinity.
3
Understand why the second interpretation is false
The author argues that the mere meaning of infinity is a mental consideration, lacking independent existence. Therefore, it cannot be the self-subsistent Necessary Existent.
This connects to the learning objective.

موجودة بالقوة ، تكون متناهية بالقوة . وأما أنها غير متناهية لا بالقوة ولا بالفعل ، فهي بحسب النهاية [ التي لا يوجد بعدها نهاية أخرى ، فإذا كانت هذه النهاية (١) ] ممتنعة الحصول، كان انتهاء الجملة الماضية إلى مثل هذه النهاية لا تكون موجودة لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة . [ وكانت الجملة الماضية غير متناهية إلى مثل هذه النهاية لا بحسب الفعل ولا بحسب القوة (٢) ] وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب ونختم هذا الفصل بذكر ما جاء في الفلسفة القديمة : [ وهو (٣) ] أن إله العالم هو حقيقة اللانهاية. فنقول : إن عنوا به أن إله العالم هو الذي يصدق عليه كونه غير متناهي في الذات، وفي الدوام، وفي الصفات ، على التفاسير المذكورة . فقد صدقوا في هذا القول. وإن عنوا به أن إله العالم هو نفس معنى اللانهاية [ فقد أبعدوا ، لأن مجرد معنى اللانهاية (٤) ] اعتبار ذهني ، ولا استقلال له في الثبوت والوجود ، فيمتنع القول بكونه إلها واجب الوجود لذاته . Existing in potentiality are finite in potentiality. As for that which is infinite neither potentially nor actually, it is with respect to the end [after which there is no other end; so if this end (1)] is impossible to obtain, then the past series ending at such an end does not exist either actually or potentially. [And the past series is infinite to such an end neither actually nor potentially (2)]. This completes the discussion in this chapter, and we conclude this section by mentioning what came in ancient philosophy: [which is (3)] that the god of the world is the reality of infinity. We say: If they mean by it that the god of the world is the one to whom it is true that He is infinite in essence, in perpetuity, and in attributes, according to the aforementioned interpretations, then they have spoken truth in this statement. But if they mean by it that the god of the world is the very meaning of infinity, [then they have gone far astray, because the mere meaning of infinity (4)] is a mental consideration, and it has no independence in substantiality and existence, so it is impossible to say that it is a god, necessary of existence in itself. [And God knows best (5)].
(1) from (w) (2) from (s)
(3) addition (4) from (s)
(5) from (w).

Commentary
This passage refutes a philosophical claim that God is the essence of infinity. The author first recapitulates a preceding argument: something that is infinite neither potentially nor actually cannot have an end after which there is no other end; if such an end is impossible, then a past series terminating at such an end does not exist at all. This concludes the chapter. Then the author mentions an ancient philosophical statement: 'The god of the world is the reality of infinity.' He distinguishes two interpretations. If they mean that God is described as infinite in essence, duration, and attributes according to proper interpretations, then they speak truth. But if they mean that God is identical to the abstract concept of infinity, that is false because a mere mental concept has no independent existence and cannot be a necessary existent God.

The Need for a Separate Proof on Ru'yah
Al-Razi states the impossibility of ru'yah is not known necessarily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of seeing God is not self-evident and requires a separate rational proof.

Core Idea
The intellect cannot judge ru'yah without a separate proof.

Al-Razi begins by establishing that the issue of ru'yah is not a matter of immediate certainty. He argues that the impossibility of seeing God is not known necessarily and therefore needs a separate proof. This sets the stage for the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting how Al-Razi frames the discussion about the necessity of ru'yah.
The passage starts with a reference to direction (al-jiha), which is left incomplete from the previous page.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that the impossibility of ru'yah is not known by necessity.
The phrase 'غير معلوم بالضرورة' is key.
3
Understand the reason
Al-Razi explains that the intellect cannot decide on the possibility or impossibility without a separate proof. Note his analogy about color vision.
He compares ru'yah to a type of disclosure akin to seeing color.
4
Reflect on the learning objective
Consider why this preliminary point is important for the overall debate on ru'yah.
It clears the ground for rational arguments, not mere assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهة؟ ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن امتناع ذلك غير معلوم بالضرورة، لأنا قد ذكرنا أن المراد بالرؤية نوع من الانكشاف، نسبته إلى المرتبة الثانية كنسبة رؤية اللون إلى تصور ماهية اللون ولا شك أن الحكم بجواز حصول هذه الحالة، وبامتناع حصولها، مما يتوقف العقل فيه، ولا يمكنه الجزم به، لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات، إلا بالدليل المنفصل.

والتحقيق فيه: إن محض الاستبعاد إن كان حجة في العدم، كان القول بإثبات موجود لا يكون في الجهة والحيز محالا، وهذا باطل عند المعتزلة والفلاسفة، وإن لم يكن حجة، وجب أن لا يكون محض الاستبعاد ههنا أيضا حجة، فالقول بأنه حجة ههنا وليس حجة هناك: متناقض فاسد.

المقدمة الثالثة: إن رؤية الله تعالى بالتفسير المذكور بتقدير أن تحصل فمحلها هو هذه العين والحدقة أم جوهر النفس؟ والأول كالمستبعد جدا وأما أن محل ذلك الإدراك الشريف هو جوهر النفس الناطقة فهذا أقرب إلى العقل بوجوه.

فهذه مقدمات الخصناها قبل الشروع في ذكر الدلائل.
And the direction? And it is known necessarily that its impossibility is not known necessarily, because we have mentioned that what is meant by vision (ru'yah) is a type of disclosure, its relation to the second rank being like the relation of seeing color to conceiving the quiddity of color. There is no doubt that the judgment about the permissibility of this state and its impossibility is something in which the intellect hesitates, and it cannot decide it, either by negation or affirmation, except by a separate proof.

And the investigation concerning it: If mere improbability (isti'bad) were a proof of non-existence, then the statement asserting the existence of an entity that is not in a direction or location would be impossible, and this is false according to the Mu'tazila and philosophers. And if it is not a proof, then mere improbability must not be a proof here either, so the claim that it is a proof here and not a proof there is a contradictory corruption.

The Third Premise: If the vision of God Most High according to the aforementioned interpretation were to occur, its locus—is it this eye and pupil or the substance of the soul? The first is extremely improbable. As for the locus of that noble perception being the substance of the rational soul (jawhar al-nafs al-natiqa), this is closer to reason in several ways.

These are the preliminaries we have specified before beginning to mention the proofs.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage contains three preliminary points that the author sets up before presenting the proofs against ru'yah. First, he states that the impossibility of seeing God is not known necessarily (bi al-darura); it requires separate proof. Second, he analyzes the argument from improbability (isti'bad): if mere improbability were proof of non-existence, then the existence of a non-spatial being would be impossible (which is false, according to Mu'tazila and philosophers), so it is inconsistent to use it here while rejecting it elsewhere. Third, he proposes a third premise: if ru'yah were to occur, its locus (mahall) would be the soul (jawhar al-nafs) rather than the eye, which is more reasonable. These preliminaries show that the issue is not self-evident and requires reasoned argument.

دليل منفصل (dalil munfasil)

Correction: Al-Razi argues that the impossibility is not known necessarily; the intellect hesitates and needs a separate proof.

D) Because the eye is the locus of vision.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of seeing God is not self-evident; it requires a separate rational proof.

The Inconsistency of the Improbability Argument
Al-Razi refutes the argument from improbability against ru'yah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why using mere improbability as a proof against ru'yah is inconsistent with other theological claims.

Core Idea
Improbability cannot be a proof unless applied consistently.

Al-Razi then turns to a common argument against ru'yah based on improbability. He points out that if mere improbability were sufficient to disprove something, then the existence of a non-spatial God would also be impossible—a claim rejected by many. This reveals an inconsistency in the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic argument
Read the passage focusing on the conditional reasoning about isti'bad (improbability).
Note the structure: 'if ... then ...' and 'if not ... then ...'
2
Identify the two cases
Al-Razi presents two scenarios: isti'bad as a proof and not as a proof.
He then shows the consequence for the existence of a non-spatial being.
3
Understand the contradiction
If isti'bad is accepted here but rejected elsewhere, it's contradictory.
The Mu'tazila and philosophers accept a non-spatial God, so they cannot use isti'bad against ru'yah.
4
Reflect on the learning objective
Consider why this argument is important for the debate on ru'yah.
It undermines a common objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهة؟ ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن امتناع ذلك غير معلوم بالضرورة، لأنا قد ذكرنا أن المراد بالرؤية نوع من الانكشاف، نسبته إلى المرتبة الثانية كنسبة رؤية اللون إلى تصور ماهية اللون ولا شك أن الحكم بجواز حصول هذه الحالة، وبامتناع حصولها، مما يتوقف العقل فيه، ولا يمكنه الجزم به، لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات، إلا بالدليل المنفصل.

والتحقيق فيه: إن محض الاستبعاد إن كان حجة في العدم، كان القول بإثبات موجود لا يكون في الجهة والحيز محالا، وهذا باطل عند المعتزلة والفلاسفة، وإن لم يكن حجة، وجب أن لا يكون محض الاستبعاد ههنا أيضا حجة، فالقول بأنه حجة ههنا وليس حجة هناك: متناقض فاسد.

المقدمة الثالثة: إن رؤية الله تعالى بالتفسير المذكور بتقدير أن تحصل فمحلها هو هذه العين والحدقة أم جوهر النفس؟ والأول كالمستبعد جدا وأما أن محل ذلك الإدراك الشريف هو جوهر النفس الناطقة فهذا أقرب إلى العقل بوجوه.

فهذه مقدمات الخصناها قبل الشروع في ذكر الدلائل.
And the direction? And it is known necessarily that its impossibility is not known necessarily, because we have mentioned that what is meant by vision (ru'yah) is a type of disclosure, its relation to the second rank being like the relation of seeing color to conceiving the quiddity of color. There is no doubt that the judgment about the permissibility of this state and its impossibility is something in which the intellect hesitates, and it cannot decide it, either by negation or affirmation, except by a separate proof.

And the investigation concerning it: If mere improbability (isti'bad) were a proof of non-existence, then the statement asserting the existence of an entity that is not in a direction or location would be impossible, and this is false according to the Mu'tazila and philosophers. And if it is not a proof, then mere improbability must not be a proof here either, so the claim that it is a proof here and not a proof there is a contradictory corruption.

The Third Premise: If the vision of God Most High according to the aforementioned interpretation were to occur, its locus—is it this eye and pupil or the substance of the soul? The first is extremely improbable. As for the locus of that noble perception being the substance of the rational soul (jawhar al-nafs al-natiqa), this is closer to reason in several ways.

These are the preliminaries we have specified before beginning to mention the proofs.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage contains three preliminary points that the author sets up before presenting the proofs against ru'yah. First, he states that the impossibility of seeing God is not known necessarily (bi al-darura); it requires separate proof. Second, he analyzes the argument from improbability (isti'bad): if mere improbability were proof of non-existence, then the existence of a non-spatial being would be impossible (which is false, according to Mu'tazila and philosophers), so it is inconsistent to use it here while rejecting it elsewhere. Third, he proposes a third premise: if ru'yah were to occur, its locus (mahall) would be the soul (jawhar al-nafs) rather than the eye, which is more reasonable. These preliminaries show that the issue is not self-evident and requires reasoned argument.

Key Terms
استبعاد (isti'bad)
جهة (jiha)
حيز (hayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that improbability is a valid proof of non-existence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if it were, it would lead to unacceptable consequences, and thus it cannot be used selectively.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Razi argue about the use of mere improbability (isti'bad) in the context of ru'yah?
A) It is a reliable proof accepted by all.
B) It is inconsistent because it is not applied uniformly.
C) It is the strongest proof against ru'yah.
D) It is only used by the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
The argument from improbability is inconsistent and cannot serve as a proof against ru'yah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to examine your own beliefs for similar inconsistencies—are there arguments you accept for some theological issues but reject for others because they are uncomfortable?
?
When you encounter a claim that seems 'improbable' to your mind, do you immediately dismiss it, or do you examine whether your criteria for probability are applied consistently across all your beliefs?
?
This lesson shows that even respected thinkers can fall into contradiction. Where in your own understanding of God's attributes might you be accepting a premise that, if applied consistently, would force you to change your position? What will you do about that?

The Locus of Ru'yah: Eye or Soul?
Al-Razi proposes the soul as the locus of ru'yah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the more suitable locus for the vision of God is the rational soul, not the physical eye.

Core Idea
The soul is a more fitting locus for perceiving God than the eye.

In his third preliminary, Al-Razi discusses where the vision of God would occur if it were to happen. He considers two possibilities: the physical eye or the rational soul. He argues that the soul is a more reasonable locus, which sets the stage for philosophical arguments about ru'yah.

Guided Path
1
Read the third premise
Read the Arabic passage about the locus (mahall) of ru'yah.
Note the two options: 'al-'ayn wa al-hadaqa' (the eye and pupil) and 'jawhar al-nafs' (substance of the soul).
2
Identify the two possibilities
Al-Razi presents the eye as extremely improbable (al-mustab'id jiddan) and the soul as closer to reason.
He uses 'kalamustab'id jiddan' for the eye.
3
Understand the reasoning
Consider why the soul might be a more appropriate locus for a non-physical perception.
The soul is immaterial, so it can receive a non-physical disclosure.
4
Connect to the overall debate
Reflect on how this premise influences the arguments for or against ru'yah.
If the locus is the soul, then physical sight is not required.

The Eight Conditions for Necessary Sight
Establishment of the principle that eight conditions necessitate sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight conditions that are claimed to necessitate vision, and why their absence would lead to sophistry.

Core Idea
When eight conditions are met, sight must occur; otherwise, our perceptions could be doubted.

This lesson presents the first part of the rational argument against the possibility of seeing God. The arguer first establishes a general principle: whenever eight specific conditions are present, vision necessarily occurs. If this were not the case, our sensory knowledge would be unreliable, leading to sophistry. Understanding this principle is crucial before applying it to the case of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the eight conditions
List each condition as mentioned. Notice that they are physical and relate to the observer, the object, and the environment.
3
Understand the necessity argument
The arguer claims that when all eight conditions are present, sight must occur; otherwise we could doubt obvious perceptions like hills and trumpets, leading to sophistry.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the structure
Note that this passage sets up a premise to be used in the main argument against ru'yah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى: قالوا: لو صحت رؤيته، لوجب أن نراه الآن، والثاني باطل. فالمقدم باطل. بيان الملازمة إنما يظهر عند تقديم مقدمة نقول: إن عند حصول ثمانية أنواع من الشرائط فإنه يجب حصول الرؤية أولها: كون الحاسة سليمة. والثانية: كون الشيء بحيث تصح رؤيته. وثالثها: أن لا يحصل القرب القريب. ورابعها: أن لا يحصل البعد البعيد. وخامسها: أن لا يكون المرئي في غاية الصغر. وسادسها: أن لا يكون في غاية اللطافة مثل الهواء. وسابعها: أن لا يتوسط بين الرائي وبين المرئي شيء من الحجب. وثامنها: أن يكون المرئي مقابلا للرائي أو في حكم المقابل. 
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: عند حصول هذه الشرائط الثمانية يجب الإبصار، إذ لو لم يجب لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا تلال وبوقات ونحن لا نراها ولا نسمعها. وذلك يفضي إلى السفسطة. فيثبت أن عند حصول هذه الشرائط الثمانية يجب الإبصار.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: لو كانت رؤية الله جائزة لما اعتبر في حصولها إلا الشرطان الأولان، أعني كون الحاسة سليمة، وكون المرئي بحيث تصح رؤيته. فأما الشرائط الستة الباقية فإنه يمتنع اعتبارها في حق الله تعالى، لأنه إنما يمكن اعتبارها بالنسبة إلى الجسم، وإلى الشيء الذي يكون حاصلا في الحيز والجهة، والباري تعالى مقدس عن هذه الحالة، فيثبت أنه بتقدير أن تجوز رؤية الله تعالى، فإن الشرائط الست لا يمكن اعتبارها في حق الله تعالى، فلم يبق في حصول رؤية الله إلا سلامة الحواس، وكون المرئي بحيث تصح رؤيته. وهذا أن الشرطان حاصلان في الحال، فكان يجب أن نراه الآن، فيثبت بما ذكرناه: أنه لو صحت رؤيته لوجب أن نراه الآن، ومعلوم أنا لا نراه الآن، فوجب أن يقال: إنما لا نراه الآن لكونه في نفسه، بحيث تمتنع رؤيته، وذلك هو المطلوب.
The First Proof: They said: If His vision were valid, then we would necessarily see Him now. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. The explanation of the entailment only becomes clear by presenting a premise: When eight types of conditions are present, vision necessarily occurs. First: the sense being sound. Second: the thing being such that its vision is valid. Third: that there not be extreme proximity. Fourth: that there not be extreme distance. Fifth: that the visible not be extremely small. Sixth: that it not be extremely subtle like air. Seventh: that there not intervene between the seer and the seen any obstruction. Eighth: that the visible be facing the seer or in the ruling of facing.
When you know this, we say: When these eight conditions are present, sight necessarily occurs, for if it were not necessary, it would be possible that there are hills and trumpets in our presence and we do not see or hear them. That leads to sophistry. So it is established that when these eight conditions are present, sight must occur.
When you know this, we say: If the vision of God were permissible, then only the first two conditions would be considered for its occurrence—namely, the sense being sound and the visible being such that its vision is valid. As for the remaining six conditions, it is impossible to consider them with respect to God Most High, because they can only be considered with respect to a body and a thing that exists in location and direction, and the Creator is exalted above this state. So it is established that, supposing the vision of God Most High is possible, the six conditions cannot be considered with respect to God Most High. Thus nothing remains for obtaining God's vision except soundness of senses and the visible being such that its vision is valid. And these two conditions are present now, so we would necessarily see Him now. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: If His vision were valid, we would necessarily see Him now. And it is known that we do not see Him now, so it must be said that we do not see Him now because He is in Himself such that His vision is impossible. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This is the first argument presented by those who deny the possibility of seeing God. The argument is a conditional: if vision of God were possible, we would see Him now. Since we do not see Him, it must be impossible. To establish the conditional, the arguer first lists eight conditions necessary for vision: sound sense, visible object, absence of extreme proximity, absence of extreme distance, not extremely small, not extremely subtle (like air), no obstructions, and facing or its equivalent. Then they argue that if ru'yah were possible for God, only the first two conditions are relevant, because the other six are physical (e.g., facing requires location, etc.) and God is transcendent beyond location. These two conditions are present now (we have sound eyes and God is visible in principle), so we would necessarily see Him. Since we do not, it must be that God is intrinsically invisible. The conclusion is that ru'yah is impossible.

Key Terms
شرائط الإبصار (shurāṭ al-ibṣār)
ملازمة (mulāzama)
السفسطة (safsafa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would happen if the eight conditions were present but sight did not occur?
A) We could trust our senses.
B) It would lead to sophistry.
C) It would be impossible.
D) We would need more conditions.

Key Takeaway
The arguer establishes that when eight specific conditions are met, sight is necessarily forced; otherwise our sensory knowledge could be doubted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly wish you could see God to remove all doubt? If the argument shows that is impossible, what does that reveal about the nature of faith? Commit to trusting His signs rather than demanding a vision.
?
The arguer says that if we doubted the necessity of sight under the eight conditions, we would fall into sophistry. When have you doubted obvious truths because you wanted more proof? How can you train yourself to accept clear evidence from God's creation?
?
God cannot be seen in this life. Does that feel like a limitation or a mercy? Are you willing to seek Him through His attributes and signs, or do you resent His hiddenness? Resolve to deepen your relationship with the unseen God.

Applying the Conditions to God's Vision
Application of the eight-condition principle to show impossibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only the first two conditions apply to God and how this leads to the conclusion that seeing God is impossible.

Core Idea
Since God is not a body, six conditions do not apply; only sound senses and visibility remain—and we don't see Him.

In this lesson, the arguer applies the previously established principle to the case of God. They argue that only two of the eight conditions are relevant to God because the other six are physical. Since these two are present now, we should see God. But we don't, so vision must be impossible. This completes the first argument against ru'yah.

Guided Path
1
Read the application passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the arguer transitions from the general principle to the specific case.
2
Identify why six conditions are excluded
The arguer states that the six conditions require a body and location, which do not apply to God. Find that reasoning in the text.
3
Understand the remaining two conditions
The only conditions that apply are sound senses and the visible being such that its vision is valid. These are present now.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since the conditions are present, we would see God if ru'yah were possible. But we don't, so ru'yah is impossible. This is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The eight conditions for vision apply to God in the same way as to physical objects.
Correction: Six of the conditions are physical and inapplicable to God, who is not a body and has no location.

Checkpoint
Why do the proponents of the first argument say that only the first two conditions for vision apply to God?
A) Because God is not a body and has no location.
B) Because God is invisible by essence.
C) Because the other conditions are unknown.
D) Because the first two are the only necessary ones.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against ru'yah concludes that since God is transcendent and not a body, His vision is impossible because we do not see Him now despite the relevant conditions being met.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's vision is impossible by necessity, what does that mean for your desire to see Allah in this life? How does that affect your longing for the Hereafter, where believers are promised to see Him? Are you content with seeking Him through faith alone?
?
The argument shows that the six conditions (proximity, direction, etc.) are irrelevant for God because He is not a body. How often do you imagine God in human terms or limit Him to your own conceptions? What concrete step can you take today to purify your understanding of His transcendence?
?
The author concludes that we do not see God now because He is inherently invisible. Does this challenge any assumption you had about experiencing God in this life? How will you respond to the longing for His presence when you cannot see Him?

The Condition of Facing: Second Argument Against Ru'yah
Second argument: facing requires spatial location, which God transcends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational argument against the possibility of seeing God, based on the condition of facing and its implications for divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Facing requires location; God has no location, so seeing Him is impossible.

The second argument against ru'yah builds on a simple but powerful observation: we only see things we can face. But facing implies being in a place. Since God is beyond space and direction, the condition for vision is absent. This passage presents that argument in a concise logical form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow become clear.
Notice the logical structure: condition → requirement → transcendence → impossibility.
2
Identify the condition of facing
The argument begins by stating that vision requires the seer to be facing the seen object (muqabala) or in its ruling. This is presented as a necessary condition.
The word 'muqabala' is key; it implies a spatial relation.
3
Understand why facing implies location and direction
Facing is only conceivable for something that occupies a location (hayyiz) and has a direction (jiha). These are attributes of bodies.
This step connects the condition to the nature of physical objects.
4
Apply God's transcendence
God is munazzah (exalted) above having a location or direction. Therefore, He cannot be the object of a facing relation.
This is the key theological premise.
5
Conclude the impossibility of ru'yah
Since the condition (facing) is absent, vision of God is impossible. The argument concludes decisively.
The argument is deductive and aims for certainty (qat').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن شرط حصول الرؤية كون الرائي مقابلا للمرئي، أو في حكم المقابل، والعلم بهذا الاشتراط ضروري، وهذا الشرط إنما يعقل ثبوته في حق الشيء الذي يكون مختصا بالحيز والجهة، ولما كان الله تعالى منزها عن هذه الصفة، كان شرط جواز الرؤية فائتا فوجب القطع بامتناع حصول هذه الرؤية.
The Second Proof: The condition for obtaining vision is that the seer be facing the seen, or in the ruling of facing, and knowledge of this condition is necessary. This condition can only be understood to hold for something that is characterized by location and direction. Since God Most High is exalted above this attribute, the condition for the possibility of vision is absent, so it is necessary to decisively conclude that this vision is impossible.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This is the second argument: vision requires that the seer face the seen object (muqabala) or be in the ruling of facing. This condition is known necessarily. But facing requires spatial location (hayyiz and jiha), and God is exalted above such attributes. Therefore, the condition for vision is absent, and ru'yah is impossible.

Key Terms
مقابلة (muqabala)
حيز (hayyiz)
جهة (jiha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that facing God is possible in a metaphorical sense without implying spatial location.
Correction: The argument shows that facing (muqabala) logically requires location and direction, which are attributes of bodies. Since God is not a body, the concept of facing cannot be applied even metaphorically in a way that preserves its rational meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why does the condition of facing make ru'yah impossible?
A) Because God is not a body and has no location.
B) Because the seer cannot face God.
C) Because facing is not necessary for vision.
D) Because God is everywhere.

Key Takeaway
The condition of facing (muqabala) proves ru'yah is impossible because it requires spatial location, which God transcends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument uses a condition you experience every time you see something—facing. Do you truly believe that Allah is absolutely free from the limitations of your senses, or do you sometimes imagine Him as being 'over there' or 'facing you'? What in your daily thoughts or prayers reveals a hidden assumption about Allah's location?
?
If you accept that the condition for seeing something requires it to be spatially confined, does your heart still yearn for a 'vision' of Allah as an experience like seeing a created thing? How can you train yourself to desire instead the ineffable, non-spatial knowledge of Him that surpasses all physical senses?
?
The argument says knowledge of the condition is necessary (daruri). Does your certainty in the necessary truths of reason (like A is A) translate into certainty about Allah's attributes? Or do you excuse yourself from logical rigor in matters of faith? What one logical step in your own belief about Allah needs to be corrected today?

Third Argument: Impression of Form in the Eye
Third proof: vision depends on impression of form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument against the possibility of seeing Allah, which relies on the necessity of an image impressing in the eye.

Core Idea
Seeing requires form; Allah has no form, so seeing Him is impossible.

Having established that vision is a physical process, theologians like al-Sanusi present a third argument: vision requires the impression of the image of the seen object in the eye. Since Allah is beyond shape and form, this condition cannot be met, making ru'yah rationally impossible. This lesson unpacks that concise but powerful proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Note the key terms: انطباع (impression), صورة (image), شكل (shape).
This is a short but dense proof.
2
Understand the logical flow
Identify the premises: (1) vision requires image impression, (2) image impression requires form, (3) Allah has no form. Then see the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implication
Reflect on why this argument is considered decisive by those who reject ru'yah. It ties vision to physicality, which cannot apply to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إن الرؤية يمتنع حصولها إلا عند انطباع صورة المرئي في حدقة الرائي، وهذا إنما يعقل إذا كان المرئي له شكل وصورة، ولما كان ذلك في حق الله تعالى محالا، كانت رؤية الله تعالى ممتنعة عقلا.
The Third Proof: Vision cannot occur except when the image of the seen object is impressed in the pupil of the seer. This is only intelligible if the seen object has a shape and form. Since that is impossible with respect to God Most High, the vision of God Most High is rationally impossible.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This is the third argument: vision requires the impression (intiba') of the image of the seen object in the pupil of the eye. This makes sense only if the seen object has a form and shape. Since God is beyond form and shape, ru'yah is rationally impossible.

Key Terms
انطباع (intiba')
صورة (ṣūra)
شكل (shakl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that vision of Allah is impossible simply because He is invisible, without a rational basis.
Correction: This argument provides a rational basis: vision requires physical impression, which cannot apply to the formless Creator.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what must happen for vision to occur?
A) The seen object must emit light.
B) The image of the seen object must be impressed in the pupil.
C) The seer must be at a proper distance.
D) The object must be colored.

Key Takeaway
Vision requires physical impression of form; since Allah is formless, seeing Him is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your longing for Allah ever slip into imagining a form or shape for Him? What would it mean to let go of every mental image and worship a God you cannot and will never see?
?
If this proof is correct, then even the desire to 'see Allah' in Paradise is a misguided wish. Are you willing to surrender that hope and instead yearn for a different kind of closeness to Him?
?
When you catch yourself trying to visualize Allah while praying or in distress, what specific thought can you recall from this lesson to stop yourself and reaffirm His absolute transcendence?
