Fourth Argument: The Visible Must Be Colored or Shaped
Fourth argument against ru'yah: visible must be colored or shaped, but God is transcendent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth rational argument against ru'yah, which posits that any visible object must possess color and shape, and that God's transcendence excludes these attributes, making vision of Him impossible.

Core Idea
If seeing requires color and shape, then the transcendent God cannot be seen.

The fourth argument follows a similar structure to the previous ones: it identifies a necessary condition for visibility, then asserts that God is free from that condition. Here, the condition is that a visible object must be either a color or something colored, and it must have a shape. Because Allah is exalted above such physical attributes, the argument concludes that vision of Him is impossible. This passage, though cut off, completes the pattern of the preceding proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

The passage ends abruptly; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the premise

3
Apply divine transcendence
The argument states that Allah is exalted above these states (color, shape). Consider why this would make vision impossible if the premise is accepted.
4
Infer the missing conclusion
Based on the pattern of earlier arguments, the missing conclusion should be that ru'yah is impossible. Try to complete the sentence in your own words.
This trains you to follow the logic even with incomplete text.
5
Evaluate the premise
Reflect: Is it true that all vision requires color and shape? Could there be a different kind of vision for non-material beings? This is a key point of debate.
The argument's strength depends on the universality of its premise.

The Fourth Proof: The visible object must be a color or something colored, and must be a shape. Since the Truth Most High is exalted above these states, it is necessary to decisively conclude that vision is impossible ...

The Second Argument: Vision Requires Facing
Al-Razi's second proof against vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second argument concludes that vision of God is impossible based on the requirement of spatial facing.

Core Idea
Vision requires facing; God has no place—so vision is impossible.

Al-Razi presents the second of four arguments against the possibility of seeing God. This argument identifies a necessary condition for vision—that the seer and the seen must be opposite each other—and shows that this condition cannot apply to God, who transcends space and direction. The argument is simple but powerful, relying on a necessary condition that God cannot fulfill.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a very condensed style here.
2
Identify the condition
The argument states that vision requires the seer to be opposite (muqābil) the visible object. This is presented as a necessary condition known necessarily.
The term 'muqābila' is key.
3
Consider why God cannot meet it
The condition only makes sense for something that occupies a specific place and direction. Since God is free from spatial attributes, the condition fails, making vision impossible.
This reasoning shows that the impossibility is conceptual, not just practical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن شرط حصول الرؤية كون الرائي مقابلا للمرئي ، أو في حكم المقابل، والعلم بهذا الاشتراط ضروري ، وهذا الشرط إنما يعقل ثبوته في حق الشيء الذي يكون مختصا بالحيز والجهة ، ولما كان الله تعالى منزها عن هذه الصفة ، كان شرط جواز الرؤية فائتا فوجب القطع بامتناع حصول هذه الرؤية . الحجة الثالثة : إن الرؤية يمتنع حصولها إلا عند انطباع صورة المرئي في حدقة الرائي ، وهذا إنما يعقل إذا كان المرئي له شكل وصورة ، ولما كان ذلك في حق الله تعالى محالا ، كانت رؤية الله تعالى ممتنعة عقلا . الحجة الرابعة : المرئي يجب أن يكون لونا أو متلونا ، ويجب أن يكون شكلا ولما كان الحق تعالى منزها عن هذه الأحوال ، وجب القطع بامتناع رؤية الله تعالى فهذا مجموع دلائل القاطعين بامتناع رؤية الله تعالى (١)
The second proof: The condition for the occurrence of vision is that the seer is opposite the visible object, or in the ruling of being opposite, and knowledge of this condition is necessary. This condition can only be conceived as established for something that is particularized by place and direction. Since God the Exalted is transcendently above this attribute, the condition for the permissibility of vision is lacking, so it is necessary to decisively judge the impossibility of this vision. The third proof: Vision cannot occur except when the image of the visible object is imprinted in the pupil of the seer. This is only conceivable if the visible object has shape and form. Since that is impossible with respect to God the Exalted, the vision of God the Exalted is rationally impossible. The fourth proof: The visible object must be a color or a color-bearer, and it must have shape. Since the Truth the Exalted is transcendently above these states, it is necessary to decisively judge the impossibility of the vision of God the Exalted. So this is the totality of the proofs of those who decisively affirm the impossibility of the vision of God the Exalted.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage lists three additional arguments that, together with the first, form the total proof for those who assert the impossibility of seeing God. Each argument identifies a condition for vision that requires physicality, which God lacks. The second argument: vision requires facing (muqābala) or its ruling, which necessitates being in a place and direction; God is free from these, so the condition fails. The third argument: vision requires the imprint of the object's image on the pupil, requiring shape and form; God has no shape or form, so vision is rationally impossible. The fourth argument: the visible must be a color or something colored, and must have shape; God transcends all such attributes, so vision is impossible. The author concludes that these constitute the total set of arguments for those who affirm the impossibility of seeing God.

Key Terms
المقابلة
الحيز
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that vision is possible if God makes Himself visible in some accommodated way.
Correction: The argument shows that the very condition for vision—facing—is impossible for God, regardless of divine power, because it is a conceptual impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what is a necessary condition for vision?
A) The visible object must be colored.
B) The seer must be opposite the visible object.
C) The seer must have a clear pupil.
D) The visible object must be within a certain distance.

Key Takeaway
The second argument establishes that vision necessitates facing, which requires spatial location, and since God has none, vision is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to picture or imagine God, do you unconsciously place Him in a specific location—like the sky or inside your heart? How does al-Razi's argument challenge that mental habit, and what does it mean for your worship to rely on revealed attributes instead of spatial imagination?
?
How does the fact that vision requires facing expose a limitation in your own ability to directly perceive God? In what ways might you be seeking a 'direct experience' of God that is impossible, and how can you redirect that desire toward knowing Him through His signs and verses?
?
If you cannot face God spatially, what does it mean to 'face' Him with your heart and actions? What one change in your daily life would make your intentions more aligned with being in the presence of the transcendent One?

The Third Argument: Vision Requires Imprint of Form
Al-Razi's third proof against vision of God.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Vision requires image imprint; God has no form—so vision is impossible.

The third argument tackles another necessary condition: vision occurs only when the form of the seen object is imprinted on the eye. This condition assumes the visible object has shape and form, which cannot be attributed to God. This argument further reinforces the conceptual impossibility of seeing God.

Identify the condition

The term 'inṭibāʿ al-ṣūra' is key.
3
Consider why God cannot meet it
This condition is only conceivable if the visible object has shape (shakl) and form (ṣūra). Since God is free from physical attributes, the condition fails, making vision impossible.
This reasoning shows that the impossibility is conceptual, based on the nature of vision.

The Fourth Argument: Visible Must Be Colored or Have Shape
Al-Razi's fourth proof against vision of God and conclusion of the set.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth argument concludes that vision of God is impossible based on the requirements of color and shape.

Core Idea
The visible must be colored or have shape; God is free from such—so vision is impossible.

The fourth and final argument in this series posits that whatever is seen must either be a color itself or bear color, and must have shape. God transcends all such physical attributes. This argument completes the set of proofs for those who affirm the impossibility of seeing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concluding sentence that sums up all four proofs.
2
Identify the conditions
The argument states that the visible object must be a color (lawn) or a color-bearer (mutalawwin), and it must have shape (shakl).
These are presented as necessary properties of anything seen.
3
Consider why God cannot meet them
God is transcendently above (munazzah) these attributes. Therefore, the conditions for being seen are not satisfied, making vision impossible.
The conclusion is decisive: 'وجب القطع بامتناع رؤية الله تعالى'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن شرط حصول الرؤية كون الرائي مقابلا للمرئي ، أو في حكم المقابل، والعلم بهذا الاشتراط ضروري ، وهذا الشرط إنما يعقل ثبوته في حق الشيء الذي يكون مختصا بالحيز والجهة ، ولما كان الله تعالى منزها عن هذه الصفة ، كان شرط جواز الرؤية فائتا فوجب القطع بامتناع حصول هذه الرؤية . الحجة الثالثة : إن الرؤية يمتنع حصولها إلا عند انطباع صورة المرئي في حدقة الرائي ، وهذا إنما يعقل إذا كان المرئي له شكل وصورة ، ولما كان ذلك في حق الله تعالى محالا ، كانت رؤية الله تعالى ممتنعة عقلا . الحجة الرابعة : المرئي يجب أن يكون لونا أو متلونا ، ويجب أن يكون شكلا ولما كان الحق تعالى منزها عن هذه الأحوال ، وجب القطع بامتناع رؤية الله تعالى فهذا مجموع دلائل القاطعين بامتناع رؤية الله تعالى (١)
The second proof: The condition for the occurrence of vision is that the seer is opposite the visible object, or in the ruling of being opposite, and knowledge of this condition is necessary. This condition can only be conceived as established for something that is particularized by place and direction. Since God the Exalted is transcendently above this attribute, the condition for the permissibility of vision is lacking, so it is necessary to decisively judge the impossibility of this vision. The third proof: Vision cannot occur except when the image of the visible object is imprinted in the pupil of the seer. This is only conceivable if the visible object has shape and form. Since that is impossible with respect to God the Exalted, the vision of God the Exalted is rationally impossible. The fourth proof: The visible object must be a color or a color-bearer, and it must have shape. Since the Truth the Exalted is transcendently above these states, it is necessary to decisively judge the impossibility of the vision of God the Exalted. So this is the totality of the proofs of those who decisively affirm the impossibility of the vision of God the Exalted.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage lists three additional arguments that, together with the first, form the total proof for those who assert the impossibility of seeing God. Each argument identifies a condition for vision that requires physicality, which God lacks. The second argument: vision requires facing (muqābala) or its ruling, which necessitates being in a place and direction; God is free from these, so the condition fails. The third argument: vision requires the imprint of the object's image on the pupil, requiring shape and form; God has no shape or form, so vision is rationally impossible. The fourth argument: the visible must be a color or something colored, and must have shape; God transcends all such attributes, so vision is impossible. The author concludes that these constitute the total set of arguments for those who affirm the impossibility of seeing God.

اللون
المتلون
الشكل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could be seen in a metaphorical or spiritual sense even without these attributes.
Correction: The argument is about physical vision; metaphysical or spiritual 'seeing' is not under discussion here.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what must any visible object be?
A) A color or a color-bearer, and have shape.
B) Facing the seer and have a form.
C) Imprinted in the pupil and have color.
D) A physical body and have direction.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument establishes that the visible must have color or bear color, and have shape, which God lacks, so vision is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof says that anything visible must have color or shape. God is above that. When you pray, do you ever unconsciously imagine God as having some form or location? How can you train your heart to worship a God who is absolutely transcendent, without any mental image? What specific step will you take this week to purify your awareness of Him from any trace of physicality?
?
If you genuinely believed that seeing God is impossible not only in this life but also in the afterlife (as this argument concludes), how would that change your longing for the Hereafter? Are you content to seek God's pleasure without ever seeing Him? Or does your soul secretly yearn for a vision? Be honest: what does that reveal about your current state of faith?
?
Imam al-Razi presents this as one of four decisive proofs against vision of God. Have you ever felt that not seeing God makes Him distant? This proof argues that visibility actually implies limitation. Could your desire to 'see' God be a subtle form of trying to reduce Him to something your senses can grasp? What would it mean for you to love and trust a God who is beyond perception entirely? Write down one practical way to strengthen that love today.

Critical Refutation of the Proof for Ru'ya
A critical footnote refuting ru'ya from multiple angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive refutation of the belief that Allah can be seen, using rational, scriptural, and hermeneutical arguments.

Core Idea
Ru'ya is negated by reason, all scriptures, and the decisive Quranic verse.

In this dense footnote, the author systematically dismantles the arguments for ru'ya. He begins with a rational critique, then turns to scriptural evidence from the Torah, Isaiah, John, and the hadith, and concludes with a powerful hermeneutical principle distinguishing muhkam from mutashabih. This lesson walks through each layer.

Guided Path
1
Start with the rational argument
Read the first part where the author denies a rational proof, citing Quran 6:103 and the falsity of the major premise that every existent is visible.
Note the examples: intellect, humanity, courage—abstract existents that are not seen.
2
Examine the scriptural refutation
Review the quotes from Torah, Isaiah, and John that clearly state God cannot be seen. Also note the hadith of Aisha reciting 'la tudrikuhu al-absar'.
These texts are presented as decisive evidence.
3
Understand the Christian influence and its consequences
The author shows how Christians contradicted their own scriptures by claiming God appeared in Christ, and this error influenced some Muslims.
This is a critique of anthropomorphism and its impact.
4
Apply the muhkam/mutashabih principle
Learn how the decisive verse (6:103) is muhkam with one interpretation, while the two ambiguous verses (83:15 and 75:23) must be interpreted in light of it.
This hermeneutical rule resolves apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) لا يصح لهم الإثبات بدليل عقلي . لورود النص بمنع الرؤية في قوله تعالى : « لا تدركه الأبصار : [ الأنعام ۱۰۳ ] ولان القضية الكبرى كاذبة فإن العقل موجود ، ولا يرى والإنسانية لا ترى ، والشجاعة لا ترى. وهي أشياء موجودة على رأى المؤلف وابن سينا . وقول المؤلف : إن الرؤية تثبت ۱ - بأخبار الانبياء ۲ - ورؤى المتصوفة : قول يدل على منع الرؤية . فإن الرؤى لا تثبت عقائد دينية . وأخبار الأنبياء دلت على منع الرؤية . ففي التوراة في أسفار موسی الخمسة لما طلب موسی عليه السلام من الله أن يراه : قال له : لا تقدر أن ترى وجهي لأن الإنسان لا يراني ويعيش. وهذا النص يمنع الرؤية، لأن الانسان لا يتحمل الرؤية. وفي سفر أشعياء وحقاً: أنت إله محتجب يا إله إسرائيل ، وفي إنجيل يوحنا : و الله لم يره أحد قط ، واليهود يقولون : إن الله لا يرى - يضم الياء - كما يقول العقلاء من المسلمين، والنصاري خالفوا النصوص المقدمة عندهم التي تمنع الرؤية ، وقالوا : بجواز الرؤية ، لأنهم يعتقدون - زورا - أن الله ظهر للناس في بد المسيح . فالناس لما رأوا المسيح ، فإنهم قد رأوا الله - على زعمهم - ودخل زعمهم هذا في عقائد بعض المسلمين ، كما دخل زعمهم في كلمة الله التي هي أقنوم العلم عندهم لقد زعموا إن المسيح ، قديمة مع الله . فيكون المسيح الذي هو الكلمة إلها قديما - الله تعالى عما الكلمة التي هي يقولون علوا كبيرا ـ فيكون الكلام زائدا على الذات وفي الأحاديث النبوية : أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها - قالت : من حدثكم أن محمدا قد رأى ربه ، فقد أعظم الغرية . ثم قرأت و لا تدركه الأبصار ، وهذا النص محكم لأن له تفسيرا واحدا هو منع الإدراك وفي القرآن نصان متشابهان هما : ١ - ١ إنهم عن ربهم يومئذ المحجوبون ، ٢ - ٥ إلى ربها ناظرة ، والنص الأول يحتمل أنهم محجوبون عن رضا الله ورحمته ، لا عن رؤية وجهه والنص الثاني يحتمل أنهم ينظرون إلى نعم الله وخيراته على أهل الجنة . والدليل إذا تطرق إليه الاحتمال ، صار متشابها ، ويلزم رده . والحالة هذه إلى المحكم ، لبيان مراد الله عز وجل .
They cannot establish proof by rational evidence because the text prevents vision in His saying: 'Vision comprehends Him not' (6:103). And because the major premise is false, for intellect is existent but is not seen, humanity is not seen, and courage is not seen. They are existing things according to the author and Ibn Sina. And the author's statement that vision is established by 1- the reports of prophets and 2- the visions of Sufis is a statement that indicates the prevention of vision. For visions do not establish religious beliefs. And the reports of the prophets indicate the prevention of vision. In the Torah, in the five books of Moses, when Moses asked Allah to see Him, He said: 'You cannot see My face, for man shall not see Me and live.' This text prevents vision because man cannot bear vision. And in the book of Isaiah: 'Truly, you are a hidden God, O God of Israel.' And in the Gospel of John: 'No one has ever seen God.' And the Jews say that Allah is not seen (with ya') as the rational ones among Muslims say. The Christians contradicted the texts they have that prevent vision and said: the permissibility of vision, because they believe falsely that God appeared to people in the person of Christ. So when people saw Christ, they have seen God according to their claim. And this claim entered into the beliefs of some Muslims, just as their claim entered into the Word of God which is the hypostasis of knowledge for them. They claimed that Christ is eternal with God. So Christ who is the Word becomes an eternal god – Allah is high above what they say, great is the elevation – so speech becomes additional to the essence. And in prophetic hadith: Aisha said: 'Whoever tells you that Muhammad saw his Lord has committed a great calumny.' Then she recited: 'Vision comprehends Him not.' This text is decisive (muhkam) because it has only one interpretation: the negation of comprehension (idrak). And in the Quran there are two ambiguous texts (mutashabih): 1- 'Their Lord will keep them away from seeing Him that day' (83:15), 2- 'Looking at their Lord' (75:23). The first text can mean they are veiled from Allah's pleasure and mercy, not from seeing His face. The second text can mean they are looking at Allah's favors and bounties upon the people of Paradise. When a proof is subject to interpretation, it becomes ambiguous (mutashabih) and must be referred back to the decisive (muhkam) to clarify Allah's intent.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a critical footnote that systematically refutes the author's position in the main text. It begins by denying that the affirmers of ru'ya can have a rational proof, citing Quran 6:103 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision comprehends Him not). It then points out that the major premise 'every existent can be seen' is false because abstract entities like intellect, humanity, and courage are existent but not seen. The author's reliance on prophetic reports and Sufi visions is then examined. The footnote argues that visions (ru'ya) cannot establish religious beliefs. As for prophetic reports, they actually negate ru'ya. It cites Torah (Exodus 33:20), Isaiah (45:15), and Gospel of John (1:18) to show that even previous scriptures deny the possibility of seeing God. The Jews affirm this. The Christians contradicted these texts by claiming God appeared in Christ, and this false belief influenced some Muslims. The footnote also mentions the hadith of Aisha denying that Muhammad saw his Lord. It distinguishes between decisive (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih) Quranic verses. The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is decisive, having only one meaning: negation of comprehension. The verses about veiling and looking (83:15 and 75:23) are ambiguous; they can be interpreted as referring to deprivation of mercy or looking at blessings. Therefore, they must be interpreted in light of the decisive verse. The overall argument is a strong rational and scriptural case against the possibility of ru'ya.

المحكم
المتشابه
الإدراك

النصارى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can be seen in the afterlife based on ambiguous Quranic verses like 'Looking at their Lord' (75:23).
Correction: The decisive verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (6:103) negates comprehension, and ambiguous verses must be interpreted accordingly—e.g., looking at Allah's blessings, not His essence.

Checkpoint

C) Because it is from the Quran.
D) Because it is supported by the hadith of Aisha.

Key Takeaway
The cumulative evidence from reason, scripture, and Quranic hermeneutics conclusively negates the possibility of seeing Allah.

?

The Four Categories of Knowledge About Allah
Al-Razi presents the first proof that we do not know Allah's specific essence in this life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that our knowledge of Allah is limited to existence, modality, negations, and relations, and that this does not constitute knowledge of His specific essence.

Core Idea
We know Allah through attributes, not His essence.

Al-Razi begins a crucial proof that in this life, humans cannot know Allah's specific essence (al-dhāt al-makhṣūṣa). He argues that everything we know about Allah falls into just four categories: existence, modality, negations, and relations. This lesson explores those categories and why they are not the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Notice the structure: thesis, proof, first premise, second premise.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the four categories
As you read, identify what Al-Razi lists as the four things we can know about Allah. Look for the words: وجود, كيفية الوجود, سلوب, إضافات.
These are existence, modality, negations, and relations.
3
Understand the second premise
Study why Al-Razi says each category is not the essence. For existence, it is an attribute; for modality, a quality of existence; for negations, mere denials; for relations, distinct from both relata.
Each is a distinct concept from the essence itself.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After reading, ask yourself: Why does Al-Razi think these four categories exhaust our knowledge? Does the conclusion follow?
The proof anticipates further reasoning that these categories do not lead to essence-knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي عشر
في
أنا في هذه الحياة. هل تعرف ذات الله تعالى من حبيت او تنها هي: أعني : تلك الحقيقة الخصوصية. وبتقدير أن ذل نعرفها فهل يمكن حصول تلك المعرفة لأحد من الخلاقة، أو وكلهم. أم لا؟
نقول : أما البحث الأول . وهو أنا في هذه الحياة الدنيا . هل نعرف تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة ؟ فنقول : إن هذه المعرفة : غير حاصلة . ويدل عليه
وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن المعلوم عندنا من الحق سبحانه ، إما الوجود ، أو كيفية ذلك الوجود أو الإضافات أو السلوب . والعلم بهذه المعلومات ليس نفس العلم بذات الله المخصوصة، ولا أيضا هذه العلوم توجب العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة ، فوجب أن يقال : إنا لا نعرف تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة
فنفتقر في تقرير هذا الدليل إلى مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : في بيان أنا لانعرف من الله إلا هذه الأمور الأربعة. فالأول : إنا إذا علمنا أن العالم محدث وكل محدث فله محدث. فههنا قد علمنا وجود الله . وأما الثاني : وهو معرفة كيفية ذلك الوجود فهي من وجهين : الأول : إنا نعرف أنه واجب الوجود لذاته ، وهو عبارة عن معرفة أن ذلك الوجود واجب الثبوت له ، لما هو هو ، وذلك عبارة عن معرفة صفة من ذلك الوجود . والثاني : إنا نعرف كونه قديما أزليا باقيا سرمديا ، وهو عبارة عن كون ذلك الوجود دائما مبرأ عن العدم فيها قبل وفيهما بعد ، ويرجع حاصله إلى معرفة صفة من صفات ذلك الوجود
وأما الثالث : وهو معرفة السلوب . فهي مثل علمنا بأنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز ، ولا في مكان ، ولا حال ، ولا في محل ، ولا ملون ، ولا يكيف ، ولا مركب ، ولا مبعض
وأما الرابع : وهو معرفة الإضافات فهو مثل علمنا بأنه يصح منه الفعل والترك. وهذه الصحة إضافة مخصوصة ، ومثل علمنا بكونه عالما ، والعلم عبارة عن إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم. فهذه الأنواع الأربعة من المعارف حاصلة للعقول البشرية .
المقدمة الثانية : إن العلم بهذه المعلومات ليس علما بالذات المخصوصة ، التي هي ذات الله تعالى . أما العلم بالوجود فليس هذا العلم علما بذات الله لانا قد دللنا على أن وجود الله صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ، وأما العلم بكونه واجب الوجود لذاته ، ويكونه دائم الوجود، فذلك أيضا ليس علما بالذات المخصوصة ، لأنا بينا أن العلم بالوجوب علم بكيفية مخصوصة من كيفيات ذلك الموجود ، وذلك العلم [ بالدوام، علم بكيفية مخصوصة من كيفيات ذلك الوجود ، ولما ثبت أن العلم (۱)] بالوجود ، ليس نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة ، فالعلم بكيفيات ذلك الوجود أولى أن لا يكون نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . وأما العلم بتلك السلوب ، فهو ليس نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . لأنا إذا قلنا : إنه ليس بجوهر ، ولا بعرض ، فالمفهوم منه : سلب الجوهرية والعرضية . وذاته المخصوصة ، ليست عبارة عن عين هذه السلوب ، والعلم به ضروري .
وأما العلم بتلك الإضافات ، فليس هو نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة ، لأن الإضافات الحاصلة بين الشيئين ، مغاير لهما معا . فالعلم بتلك الإضافة لا يكون نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه العلوم الأربعة ليست عبارة عن العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة .
Chapter Eleven: On whether in this life we know the specific essence of God Almighty? I mean: that particular reality. And assuming that we know it, can that knowledge be attained by any of the creatures, or by all of them? Or not? We say: As for the first inquiry—which is whether in this worldly life we know that particular reality—we say that this knowledge is not attained. Evidence for it is [given] through proofs: The first proof: What is known to us of the Truth, exalted is He, is either existence, or the modality of that existence, or relations, or negations. Knowledge of these items is not itself knowledge of the specific essence of God, nor do these knowledges necessitate knowledge of that specific essence. Therefore it must be said: we do not know that particular reality. So in establishing this proof we need premises: The first premise: An explanation that we know of God only these four matters. The first: When we know that the world is created and every created thing has a creator, here we have known the existence of God. As for the second: knowledge of the modality of that existence—it is from two aspects: first, that we know He is necessary of existence in Himself, which is knowledge that that existence is necessarily established for Him as He is, and that is knowledge of a description of that existence. Second, that we know He is eternal, pre-eternal, everlasting, perpetual, which is that that existence is perpetually free from non-existence before and after, and its conclusion is knowledge of a description among the descriptions of that existence. As for the third: knowledge of the negations—such as our knowledge that He, exalted, is not located in a place, not in a direction, not in a substratum, not in a locus, not colored, not qualified by modality, not composite, not partitioned. As for the fourth: knowledge of the relations—such as our knowledge that it is possible for Him to act or refrain; this possibility is a specific relation; and such as our knowledge that He is knowing—knowledge is a specific relation between the knower and the known. So these four types of knowledge are attained by human intellects. The second premise: That knowledge of these items is not knowledge of the specific essence which is the essence of God Almighty. As for knowledge of existence, this knowledge is not knowledge of God's essence because we have demonstrated that God's existence is an attribute subsisting in His essence. And as for knowledge that He is necessary of existence in Himself and that He is perpetual in existence, that too is not knowledge of the specific essence, because we have explained that knowledge of necessity is knowledge of a specific modality among the modalities of that existent, and that knowledge [of perpetuity, knowledge of a specific modality among the modalities of that existence, and since it has been established that knowledge (1)] of existence is not itself knowledge of that specific essence, then knowledge of the modalities of that existence is even more surely not itself knowledge of that specific essence. And as for knowledge of those negations, it is not itself knowledge of that specific essence. For when we say 'He is not a substance, nor an accident,' the meaning derived is the negation of substantiality and accidentalness. His specific essence is not identical to these negations, and this is known necessarily. And as for knowledge of those relations, it is not itself knowledge of that specific essence, because relations occurring between two things are distinct from both of them. So knowledge of that relation is not itself knowledge of that specific essence. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that these four knowledges are not identical to knowledge of that specific essence.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the chapter's thesis that in this life we do not know Allah's specific essence (al-dhāt al-makhṣūṣa). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, structures the argument by first stating the conclusion that this knowledge is not attained, then introducing the first proof. The proof relies on the observation that everything known to us about Allah falls into four categories: existence (wujūd), modality of existence (kayfiyyat al-wujūd, which includes necessity and eternity), negations (sulūb, e.g., not being a body or location), and relations (iḍāfāt, e.g., being able to act or being knowing). Al-Razi then argues that these four types of knowledge are not identical to knowledge of the specific essence, nor do they necessitate it. To elaborate, he provides two premises. The first premise explains that human beings indeed know only these four categories from Allah, giving examples for each. The second premise demonstrates that none of these categories constitutes knowledge of the essence itself: existence is an attribute distinct from the essence; modality is a attribute of that existence; negations are not the essence but mere denials; relations are distinct from both relata. The passage ends by establishing that these four knowledges are not the same as knowledge of the specific essence. Note that the proof is incomplete in this window—the author intended also to argue that these four do not even lead to knowledge of the essence, but that part is cut off. Nevertheless, the core idea that our knowledge of Allah is limited to these categories and is not a direct apprehension of His essence is clearly stated.

Key Terms
ذات مخصوصة (specific essence)
وجود (existence)
كيفية الوجود (modality of existence)
سلوب (negations)
إضافات (relations)
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
قديم (eternal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing Allah's attributes, like His existence or eternity, means we know His essence directly.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that attributes are distinct from the essence; knowing them does not constitute knowing the essence itself.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi establishes that human knowledge of Allah is limited to four categories, none of which reveals His specific essence.

First Proof: Attributes Do Not Yield Knowledge of the Essence
Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the first proof from the four premises that we cannot know God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi that knowing God's attributes does not lead to knowing His essence.

Core Idea
Knowing God's attributes is not knowing His essence.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a logical argument based on four premises to show that human knowledge of God is limited to attributes, not the essence. This proof emphasizes divine transcendence and the limits of human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: the passage states a conclusion, then elaborates on the third premise.
2
Identify the four premises
The passage mentions 'these four premises' and then focuses on the third. Identify what the third premise asserts about human knowledge of God.
The third premise is the key: introspection shows we only know four types of attributes.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow the reasoning: from the third premise and the fact that attributes are not the essence, the conclusion that we do not know the essence follows necessarily.
The argument is deductive: if we only know attributes, and attributes are not the essence, then we do not know the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو ظاهر . لأنا عند العلم بهذه المقدمات الأربعة ، لا نجد من أنفسنا إلا أنه شيء ما . لا نعرف أنه ما هو . إلا أنه موصوف بهذه الأنواع الأربعة من الصفات . وذلك يدل على أن العلم بهذه المعلومات، لا يوجب العلم بالذات المخصوصة. وأما المقدمة الثالثة : فهي في بيان أنها لا تعرف من الله تعالى إلا هذه الأنواع الأربعة من المعلومات . وهي من رجع إلى نفسه واعتبر حال عقله وفهمه ، علم بالضرورة أنه ليس عنده من المعارف الإلهية إلا هذه الأنواع الأربعة ، ولما ثبت أنه ليس عنده إلا هذه المعارف الأربعة ، وثبت أنها ليست نفس العلم بذات الله تعالى ، ولا أنها توجب العلم بذات الله ، ظهر حينئذ أن العلم بالذات المخصوصة الله تعالى غير حاصل عندنا البتة
It is clear. For when we know these four premises, we find from ourselves only that it is something. We do not know what it is, except that it is described by these four types of attributes. And that indicates that knowledge of these items of knowledge does not necessitate knowledge of the particular essence. As for the third premise: it is an explanation that from God Almighty only these four types of items of knowledge are known. And it is that whoever turns to himself and considers the state of his intellect and understanding knows necessarily that he has from divine knowledge only these four types. And when it is established that he has only these four types of knowledge, and it is established that they are not themselves knowledge of the essence of God Almighty, nor do they necessitate knowledge of God, then it appears that knowledge of the particular essence of God Almighty is not at all obtained by us.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that we cannot know God's essence (al-dhat al-makhsusah). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by stating the conclusion: it is clear that knowing the four premises does not give knowledge of the essence. The first two premises are implied: we only know that God is something (shay' ma) described by four types of attributes, but not what that something is. The third premise is then elaborated: introspection shows that human beings only possess these four types of divine attributes (e.g., existence, eternity, power, will) and nothing more. Since these attributes are not the essence itself, and they do not lead to knowledge of the essence, it follows that knowledge of the essence is entirely absent in us. The argument follows a logical sequence: (1) premises lead to knowledge of attributes only, not essence; (2) the third premise confirms the limitation; (3) conclusion: no knowledge of essence.

Why God's Essence Cannot Be Conceptualized
Author argues that God's essence is outside the four categories of conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to the author, human conceptualization cannot grasp the essence of God, and how the four-fold division of conceptualizations supports this.

Core Idea
God's essence lies beyond all four categories of human thought.

This lesson presents a rigorous argument from classical kalam that God's essence is beyond all human conceptualization. The author first categorizes every possible human idea into four types, then demonstrates that the reality of God (dhāt Allāh) falls outside all these categories. A potential counterexample—our ability to conceive of a 'partner of God'—is shown to be a composite concept, not a direct grasp of God's essence. By the end, you will see why the author concludes that conceptualizing God's essence is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four categories
Read the passage carefully to identify the four divisions of conceptualization. Note that they include simple concepts from childhood, imaginative combinations, rational compositions (premises and syllogisms), and compounds of the previous three.
These categories are exhaustive for all human concepts.
2
See the inductive proof
The author claims that every conceptualization falls into one of the four categories. He supports this by introspection: we cannot think of any concept outside these.
3
Examine the objection
The objector raises the concept of 'partner of God' (sharīk al-ilāh) as a possible counterexample. Understand the objection: we conceive it and judge it impossible, yet it seems outside the four categories.
4
Follow the resolution
The author responds that 'partner of God' is actually a composite concept, formed by combining the known meanings of 'partner' and 'God'. Thus it falls under the fourth category (composition).
5
Apply to God's essence
Now, examine where God's essence falls: not in sensory, not in psychic foundlings, not in rational simples, not in composites. Therefore it is outside all categories, and thus unconceptualizable.
This is the final conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأس، لأن هذه التصورات كانت حاضرة عند الإنسان، فالخيال ركب بعضها مع البعض . وأما تركيبات العقل فمثل تركيب أحد التصورين بالآخر حتى تتركب منهما مقدمة، وتركيب أحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى حتى يتركب منهما قياسا . إذا عرفت هذه الأقسام الأربعة من التصورات . فنقول : الإنسان لا يمكنه أن يستحضر نوعا من أنواع التصورات، إلا على أحد هذه الأقسام الأربعة ، فأما ما يكون مغايرا لهذه الأقسام الأربعة فإنه لا يمكنه [ أن يستحضر (۱) ] تصورها البتة . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا رجعنا إلى انفسنا واعتبرنا أحوال إدراكاتنا وتعقلاتنا ، علمنا أنه لا يمكننا أن نستحضر شيئا من التصورات إلا على أحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة. فإن قالوا : إنا نحكم بأن شريك الإله ممتنع ، ولولا أنا تصورنا معنى شريك الإله ، وإلا لما أمكننا أن نحكم عليه بالامتناع، فيثبت أن معنى شريك الإله : متصور لنا ، مع أنه خارج عن الأقسام الأربعة المذكورة . فنقول : بل هذا من باب تركيب العقل ، وذلك لأنه حصل عنده معنى الشريك ، حيث وجد . وحصل عنده معنى الإله . فنقول عند هذا : حصول شيء : الله تعالى نسبته إليه كنسبة أحد الشريكين إلى الآخر فيما بيننا : محال . فيثبت أن هذا من باب التصورات المركبة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : حقيقة الإله : ما وجدناها بحواسنا ، ولا من القسم الثاني وهو الوجدانيات النفسانية ، ولا من التصورات العقلية مثل الوجود والعدم ، [ ولا من القسم الرابع (٣) ] وهو التصورات المركبة من تلك الأقسام الثلاثة . ولما كانت الحقيقة المخصوصة التي هي ذات الله مخالفة لهذه الأقسام الأربعة وثبت بالاستقراء أنه لا يمكننا تحصيل شيء من التصورات، إلا على أحد تلك الأقسام الأربعة ، وجب الجزم بأن تصور حقيقة تلك الذات المخصوصة غير حاصل للبشر .
Head, because these conceptualizations were present in man, so imagination combined some with others. As for the compositions of reason, they are like combining one of the two concepts with the other until a premise is composed from them, and combining one of the two premises with the other until a syllogism is composed from them. If you have known these four divisions of conceptualizations, then we say: man cannot bring to mind any type of conceptualization except in one of these four divisions; as for that which is different from these four divisions, he can never [ (1) ] conceive it at all. The proof for this: that if we return to ourselves and consider the states of our perceptions and intellects, we know that we cannot bring to mind any conceptualization except in one of these four ways. If they say: we judge that the partner of God (sharīk al-ilāh) is impossible, and were it not that we conceived the meaning of partner of God, we would not have been able to judge it as impossible, so it is established that the meaning of partner of God is conceived by us, while it is outside the mentioned four divisions. Then we say: Rather, this belongs to the category of reason's composition, because he has acquired the meaning of partner, where it is found, and he has acquired the meaning of God. So we say regarding this: the existence of something: God most high—its relation to Him is like the relation of one of the two partners to the other among us: impossible. So it is established that this is from the category of composite conceptualizations. And when this is established, we say: the reality of God: we have not found it by our senses, nor from the second division, which is psychic foundlings, nor from the rational conceptualizations like existence and non-existence, [ nor from the fourth division (3) ] which is the conceptualizations composed from these three divisions. And since the specific reality which is the essence of God is opposed to these four divisions, and it has been established by induction that we cannot obtain any conceptualization except in one of those four divisions, it becomes necessary to assert that the conceptualization of the reality of that specific essence is not attained by humans.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the essence of God cannot be conceptualized by humans. It begins by establishing that all conceptualizations fall into four categories: simple concepts present from childhood, imaginative compositions, rational compositions (like premises and syllogisms), and composite concepts formed from the other three. The author argues inductively that every conceivable idea belongs to one of these four. An objection is raised: we can conceive of 'partner of God' (sharīk al-ilāh) and judge it impossible, yet it seems outside the four categories. The answer is that this concept is a rational composition combining the meaning of 'partner' (which is known) and 'God' (known in a general sense), so it is in fact composite. The author then examines the reality of God: it is not sensory, not psychic foundling (like pleasure or pain), not a rational simple like existence, and not a composite of these. Therefore, God's essence falls outside all four categories, and since all conceptualizations must be within them, we cannot conceive His essence. The conclusion is categorical: conceptualization of God's essence is impossible for humans.

Knowing the Cause Implies Knowing All Effects
Philosophers' argument that knowing the essence of the First Cause would imply omniscience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why philosophers argue that knowledge of God's essence would necessitate omniscience, and why this leads to the conclusion that God's essence is unknowable.

Core Idea
If we knew God's essence, we would know everything—so we clearly don't.

The philosophers put forward a powerful argument built on causality: knowing the complete cause of something means knowing its effects. Since contingent things all depend on God as their cause, knowing God's essence would mean knowing every detail of creation. But it's obvious that no human has such comprehensive knowledge, so we must conclude that God's essence remains beyond our grasp.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional: if this, then that.
Pay attention to the phrase 'العلم بماهية العلة يوجب العلم بالمعلولات'.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Identify the key premise: knowledge of the complete cause necessitates knowledge of all effects. Then trace how the author applies this to the First Cause and contingent things.
The conclusion is that we do not know God's essence.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this proof implies about our knowledge of God. Does it mean we know nothing at all? The common misunderstanding clarifies that we can know attributes and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن الفلاسفة بينوا أن العلم بماهية العلة ، يوجب العلم بالمعلولات ولا شك أن الممكنات منتهية في سلسلة الحاجات ، ودرجات الافتقارات إلى الحقيقة المخصوصة التي لواجب الوجود لذاته . فلو كانت تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة ، معقولة لأحد من البشر ، لكان ذلك الإنسان عالما بالعلة التامة الحقيقية لجميع الممكنات ، والعلم بالعلة التامة يوجب العلم بالمعلول ، فكان يجب أن يكون ذلك الإنسان عالما بجميع أقسام الممكنات وأجناسها وأنواعها وأصنافها وأشخاصها على الترتيب النازل من عنده طولا وعرضاً، لكن الأمر ليس كذلك . وهو معلوم بالضرورة. فوجب القطع بأنا لا تعرف ماهية الشيء الذي هو علة لجميع الممكنات . ولا شك أن ذاته المخصوصة هي المبدا الممكنات . وهذا يفيد الجزم بأنا لا نعرف تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة من حيث هي هي
The third proof: The philosophers have shown that knowledge of the essence of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effects. And there is no doubt that contingent things (mumkināt) terminate in the chain of needs and degrees of dependency upon the specific reality which is the Necessary Existence in itself. So if that specific reality were conceivable by any human, that human would be knowing the true complete cause of all contingent things, and knowledge of the complete cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. So that human would be knowing all divisions of contingent things, their genera, species, varieties, and individuals in descending order from it lengthwise and widthwise, but the matter is not so. And this is known necessarily. So it must be concluded that we do not know the essence of the thing which is the cause of all contingent things. And there is no doubt that His specific essence is the principle of contingent things. And this gives the certainty that we do not know that specific reality in itself.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the impossibility of knowing God's essence. It is based on a philosophical premise: knowledge of the cause's essence entails knowledge of all its effects. The author argues that contingent things are all caused and ultimately depend on the Necessary Being. If any human knew the essence of the Necessary Being, that human would know the complete cause of all contingent things, and thus would know all contingent things in their full detail. However, it is self-evident that no human has such comprehensive knowledge. Therefore, we must conclude that no human knows the essence of the First Cause (God). The proof decisively shows that we do not know God's specific reality in itself (min ḥaythu hiya hiya).

Key Takeaway

The Fourth Proof: Why We Cannot Know God's Essence
Argument that knowing God would require a mental form equal to His essence, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that knowing God's essence would require a mental form equal to His essence, leading to the conclusion that such knowledge is impossible.

Core Idea
If knowledge is a mental copy, knowing God would make His essence a species.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument rooted in a particular theory of knowledge. The author assumes that knowledge consists of a mental form that matches the essence of the known object. From this, he argues that knowing God would force His unique essence to become a generic species, which is absurd. This leads to the conclusion that God's essence cannot be known by any creature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow: premise, conditional, consequence, conclusion.
2
Identify the premise about knowledge
Find the definition of knowledge that the author borrows from the philosophers. Understand that it says knowledge is a mental form equal in essence to the known object.
This premise is key; without it the argument collapses.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace the conditional: if we knew God, the mental form would equal His essence. Then note the consequence: God's essence would be a species under which individuals fall. The author says this is impossible.
Ask yourself why making God a species is impossible (because He is unique and cannot be multiplied).
4
Reflect on the dependency
The author notes that this proof works only if the theory of knowledge as mental form is accepted. Consider how the argument changes if one rejects that theory.
This shows the argument is not an independent revealed truth but a philosophical inference.
5
Grasp the final conclusion
The conclusion is that knowledge of that specific essence (God's) is impossible for all creatures. Understand why this is the endpoint of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الفلاسفة أثبتوا : أن العلم بالشيء عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية في الماهية للمعلوم في العالم. وإذا ثبت هذا الأصل فنقول : لو عرفنا تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة ، لوجب أن تكون تلك الصورة العقلية ، مساوية لذات الله تعالى في تمام الماهية ، وحينئذ تكون تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة نوعا حصل تحته أشخاص ، لكنه ثبت بالبراهين أن ذلك محال ، فوجب القطع بأنه يمتنع حصول العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . واعلم أنه لو ثبت أن العلم لا عند حصول صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم، لكان هذا برهانا يقينيا يحصل في أن معرفة تلك الذات المخصوصة ممتنعة في حق كل المخلوقات
The fourth proof: The philosophers have established that knowledge of a thing is the obtaining of a form equal in essence to the known object in the intellect. And when this principle is established, we say: If we knew that specific reality, then that mental form would have to be equal to the essence of God most high in complete essence, and then that specific reality would be a species under which individuals fall, but it has been established by proofs that that is impossible. So it must be concluded that obtaining knowledge of that specific essence is impossible. And know that if it is established that knowledge is but the obtaining of a form equal to the known object in the intellect, this would be a certain proof that knowledge of that specific essence is impossible for all creatures.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof based on a theory of knowledge: knowledge consists of a mental form that is equal in essence to the object known. The author argues that if we knew God's essence, the mental form in our intellect would have to be equal to God's essence in every respect. This would imply that God's essence becomes a species with individuals (i.e., multiple instances of that form), which is impossible because God is unique and cannot be multiplied. Therefore, it is impossible to have knowledge of God's essence. The author notes that if the theory of knowledge as a mental form equal to the object is accepted, this argument becomes a certain proof that no creature can know God's essence in itself.

Key Terms

مساوية

نوع
أشخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth proof is an independent scriptural argument that does not rely on any philosophical theory.
Correction: The author explicitly bases this proof on the philosophers' theory that knowledge is a mental form equal to the object. If that theory is rejected, the argument does not hold. It is a conditional philosophical argument, not a direct revealed proof.

Checkpoint
Why would knowing God's essence require that His essence become a species with individuals?
A) Because knowledge is a mental form equal to the object.
B) Because God is the cause of all things.
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of unique things.
D) Because God has many attributes.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof argues that since knowledge involves a mental form equal to the object, knowing God would require His unique essence to be multiplied, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. You claim to believe that God is beyond your full comprehension, but have you ever caught yourself forming a mental image of Him, even unintentionally? How does this proof expose the subtle idolatry of reducing God to a concept you can grasp? What will you do today to guard against that?
?
2. If knowing God's essence is impossible, then what is the foundation of your certainty in faith? Can you still be utterly certain of His existence, mercy, and justice without fully knowing His essence? How does that reshape your trust in Him?
?
3. The philosophers say knowledge is a mental copy. But your relationship with God is not merely intellectual—it involves love, awe, and submission. How can you genuinely worship a God you cannot 'capture' in your mind? What needs to change in your worship today to reflect this truth?

The Universal Nature and Restriction: An Incomplete Proof
Principle and examples of universal natures with restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical principle that a universal nature remains universal when restricted by a universal restriction, and see how this principle begins to be applied in the fifth proof.

Core Idea
A universal nature remains universal even when restricted.

This lesson introduces the fifth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which attempts to argue that God's essence cannot be known because any restriction of a universal nature keeps it universal. However, the text is incomplete due to a page break. We will examine the principle and examples provided, and note the intended direction of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the principle
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Pay attention to the opening statement: when a universal nature is restricted by a universal restriction, the resulting nature remains universal.
The principle is stated as established in philosophy.
2
Follow the examples
The author gives two examples: first restricting 'man' by 'world' to get 'worldly man', which is still universal; then restricting that by 'ascetic' to get 'ascetic worldly man'. Notice that each step preserves universality.
These examples illustrate the principle in action.
3
Recognize the incompleteness
The passage ends abruptly. The full argument would likely extend this chain to show that no amount of universal restrictions can yield knowledge of God's unique essence.
This lesson only presents the foundation; the conclusion is missing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The fifth proof: It has been established in philosophy that the universal nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-kulliyya) when restricted by a universal restriction, the essence restricted by that universal restriction becomes also universal. Its example: Our saying 'man' is a universal nature. And our saying 'the world' is also a universal nature. So if we restrict 'man' by 'world', the result is the universal man, which is also a universal nature. So if we restrict 'worldly man' by the restriction of being an ascetic, the result is the man...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of the fifth proof, but it is incomplete as the page ends. The argument is based on a philosophical principle: a universal nature, when restricted by a universal restriction, remains universal. The author gives examples: 'man' is universal; 'world' is universal; restricting 'man' by 'world' yields 'worldly man', which is still universal; then restricting that further by 'ascetic' yields 'ascetic worldly man', still universal. The intended conclusion likely is that if God's essence were somehow restricted or described, it would remain universal and thus not be a particular known object, or that the process of restriction cannot lead to knowledge of a unique essence. Since the argument is incomplete, the commentary cannot fully explain it.

Fourth Proof: Knowledge as Mental Image Cannot Equal God
Razi's fourth proof: knowledge as mental image leads to impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that knowing God's essence is impossible because it would require a mental image equal to Him, reducing Him to a species with individuals.

Core Idea
Knowing God would make Him a species — an impossibility.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, targeting the philosophers' definition of knowledge. Razi argues that if knowledge consists of a mental image perfectly equal to the known, then knowing God would force God to be a species with individuals — contradicting His absolute uniqueness. The argument is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's argument structure emerge.
Notice the logical flow: premise → application → consequence → conclusion.
2
Understand the philosophical premise
The philosophers defined knowledge as a mental image (sura) equal in essence (mahiyya) to the known object. Make sure you grasp this concept.
This premise is the foundation of the proof.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Apply the premise to knowledge of God: if we could know God, the mental image would have to equal God's essence, making God a species with individuals — an impossibility.
Ask yourself: why can't God be a species?
4
Reach the conclusion
Since the consequence is impossible, the only conclusion is that knowledge of God's essence is impossible for all creatures.
Razi adds that if the premise holds, this is a certain proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الفلاسفة أثبتوا : أن العلم بالشيء عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية في الماهية للمعلوم في العالم. وإذا ثبت هذا الأصل فنقول : لو عرفنا تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة ، لوجب أن تكون تلك الصورة العقلية ، مساوية لذات الله تعالى في تمام الماهية ، وحينئذ تكون تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة نوعا حصل تحته أشخاص ، لكنه ثبت بالبراهين أن ذلك محال ، فوجب القطع بأنه يمتنع حصول العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة . واعلم أنه لو ثبت أن العلم لا عند حصول صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم، لكان هذا برهانا يقينيا يحصل في أن معرفة تلك الذات المخصوصة ممتنعة في حق كل المخلوقات
The fourth proof: The philosophers established that knowledge of a thing consists in the occurrence of an image equal in essence (mahiyya) to the known in the intellect. When this principle is established, we say: If we knew that specific reality, it would be necessary that that mental image be equal to the essence of God Most High in the entirety of mahiyya, and then that specific reality would be a species under which individuals fall. But it has been established by proofs that this is impossible. So it is necessary to conclude that obtaining knowledge of that specific essence is impossible. And know that if it were established that knowledge is [nothing but?] the occurrence of an image equal to the known in the intellect, this would be a certain proof that knowledge of that specific essence is impossible for all creatures.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, again drawing on a philosophical principle: knowledge of something consists of a mental image (sura) that is equal in essence (mahiyya) to the thing known. If we were to know God's specific essence, the mental image would have to be equal to God's essence in every respect. However, that would make God's essence a species (naw') with individuals (ashkhas) falling under it—for example, a mental image of a horse is a universal concept applying to many individual horses. But God's essence is unique and cannot be an instance of a genus. Since it is impossible for God to be a species with individuals, obtaining knowledge of that specific essence is impossible. The argument is a reductio: the premise that knowledge requires equality leads to an impossibility, so knowledge is impossible. Razi adds that if the premise is correct, the proof is certain for all creatures.

Key Terms
صورة
ماهية
نوع
أشخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think mental images are only approximations, not exact equals.
Correction: The argument uses the philosophers' own definition that the mental image is exactly equal in essence. Even if one rejects that definition, Razi is showing that if it is accepted, knowledge of God is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof claim would happen if we knew God's essence?
A) We would become divine.
B) God would become a species with individuals.
C) The mental image would be imperfect.
D) Knowledge would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that knowing God's essence is impossible because it would reduce God to a species with individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that knowing God's essence is impossible. Do you ever try to define God in finite categories or expectations? What assumptions about God do you need to surrender today?
?
If you can never fully comprehend God's essence, then what does it mean for you to say you 'know' Him? Are you content with knowing Him through His names, attributes, and actions, or do you secretly desire a direct vision that would reduce Him to a limited being?
?
Razi says this impossibility is for all creatures — including you. Does that humble your intellect? How does this realization change how you approach prayer, worship, and your relationship with the One whom you can never fully grasp?

Fifth Proof: All Knowledge of God Is Universal, Not Particular
Razi argues that all we know of God are universal concepts, not His particular essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that human knowledge of God is limited to universal concepts and cannot reach His particular essence.

Core Idea
We only know God through universal ideas, never His unique reality.

Razi presents a philosophical proof that challenges the possibility of knowing God's essence. Using a series of logical premises about universals and particulars, he demonstrates that all our concepts of God—existence, oneness, attributes—are universal notions, which by definition cannot capture His unique, particularized reality. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the flow of the argument settle.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the answer to the objection continues on the next page.
2
Identify the three premises
The author states three premises. Find them and understand each: universal restriction maintains universality; universal ≠ particular; God's essence is particular.
The first premise uses examples like 'human' and 'scholar'.
3
Trace the application to God
Follow how each attribute (existent, necessary, etc.) is a universal restriction on a universal, so the result is still universal. Conclude that our knowledge is of universals, not of the particular essence.
Notice the repeated phrase 'ثبت أن' which marks conclusions.
4
Consider the objection
The objection claims that knowing God's oneness is particular. Observe how the answer begins but is cut off. Reflect on what the completed answer might be.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قالوا'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : ثبت في الحكمة : أن الطبيعة الكلية إذا قيدت بقيد كلي ، كانت الماهية المتقيلة بذلك القيد الكلي ، تكون كلية أيضا مثاله : إن قولنا : الإنسان : طبيعة كلية . وقولنا : العالم : طبيعة أيضا كلية . فإذا قيدنا الإنسان بالعالم ، كان الحاصل هو الإنسان الكلي، وهو أيضا طبيعة كلية . فإذا قيدنا الإنسان العالم بقيد كونه زاهدا كان الحاصل هو الإنسان الكلي ، وهو أيضا طبيعة كلية . فإذا قيدنا الإنسان العالم بقيد كونه زاهدا كان الحاصل هو الإنسان العالم الزاهد، وهو أيضا كلي . ولو أضفت إليه قيد [ بعد قيد (١) ] إلى ألف ألف مرتبة ، كان الحاصل هو الكلي . فهذا إحدى المقدمات ضروري . والمقدمة الثانية : إن الكلي ليس عين الجزئي والشخصي ، والعلم به . والمقدمة الثالثة : إن الذات المخصوصة التي هي الله تعالى ليس أمرا كليا ، بل هو ذات معينة وحقيقة مشخصة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : كل ما نعرفه من الله تعالى ، فهو أمر كلي [ مقيد بقيد كلي . مثلا : إذا قلنا : موجود فهو كلي (۲) ] فإذا قيدناه بقيد كونه موجودا واجب الوجود . كان المجموع هو أنه موجود واجب الوجود لذاته . ثم إذا قیدناه بقيد أنه ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر ولا بعرض ، كان المجموع كليا . فإذا قيدناه بالصفات الإضافية مثل قولنا : إنه عالم ، قادر ، حي ، كان ذلك تقييدا للكلي بالكلي ، فيكون المجموع الحاصل بعد ضم تلك القيود إليه أمرا كليا ، فيثبت : أن كل ما نعلمه من الله تعالى فهو أمر كلي . وثبت : أن هذا الكلي ليس هو نفس تلك الذات المخصوصة . ثبت : أن العلم بهذه المعلومات الكلية ، ليس علما بتلك الذات المخصوصة . وإذا ثبت أنا لا نعلم إلا ذلك المعلوم الكلي ، وثبت أن العلم بذلك المعلوم الكلي ، ليس نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة ، لزم القطع بأنا لا نعرف تلك الذات المخصوصة . فإن قالوا : [ لما علمنا أنه سبحانه واحد ، وأنه لا يمكن أن تكون تلك الحقيقة حاصلة لشيء آخر سواه (۳)] فهذا المعلوم مانع من احتمال الشركة ، فلم يكن هذا المعلوم كليا ، بل كان علمنا به من حيث إنه تلك الذات المعينة .. فنقول : العلم بأنه سبحانه واحد ، وبأنه لا يمكن وقوع الشركة فيما بينها وبين
The fifth proof: It is established in philosophy that when a universal nature is restricted by a universal restriction, the essence restricted by that universal restriction remains universal. An example: Our saying 'human' is a universal nature. Our saying 'scholar' is also a universal nature. So if we restrict 'human' by 'scholar', the result is 'scholarly human', which is also a universal nature. If we restrict 'scholarly human' by the restriction 'ascetic', the result is 'ascetic scholarly human', which is also universal. And if you add to it restriction after restriction up to a thousand thousand levels, the result is still universal. This is one of the premises, which is necessary. The second premise: The universal is not identical with the particular and individual, and knowledge of it [is not knowledge of the individual]. The third premise: The specific essence which is God Most High is not a universal thing, but rather a determinate essence and a particularized reality. If you know this, we say: Everything we know of God Most High is a universal thing [restricted by a universal restriction. For example: if we say 'existent', it is universal]. So if we restrict it by the restriction 'existent, necessary of existence', the composite is 'existent, necessary of existence by itself'. Then if we restrict it by the restriction that it is not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident, the composite is universal. And if we restrict it by relational attributes such as our saying 'He is knowing, powerful, living', that is a restriction of the universal by a universal, so the composite resulting after attaching these restrictions to it is a universal thing. So it is established that everything we know of God Most High is a universal thing. And it is established that this universal is not itself that specific essence. It is established that knowledge of these universal pieces of information is not knowledge of that specific essence. And if it is established that we only know that universal known thing, and it is established that knowledge of that universal known thing is not itself knowledge of that specific essence, then it necessarily follows that we do not know that specific essence. If they say: [Since we know that He is one, and that it is impossible for that reality to be obtained by anything other than Him] — this knowledge prevents the possibility of sharing, so this known thing is not universal; rather, our knowledge of Him is in respect that He is that determinate essence... We say: The knowledge that He is one and that it is impossible for sharing to occur between it and...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, which is built on three premises about universals and particulars. The first premise: When a universal nature (tabi'a kulliyya) is restricted by a universal restriction (qayd kulli), the result remains universal. Example: 'human' (universal) restricted by 'scholar' (universal) yields 'scholarly human', still universal; no matter how many restrictions are added, the result remains universal. The second premise: The universal is not identical to the particular (juz'i) and individual (shakhsi). The third premise: God's specific essence is not universal but a determinate particularized reality. From these premises, the argument proceeds: Everything we know of God involves universal concepts—like 'existent', 'necessary of existence', 'not a body', 'knowing', etc. Even when we combine these, the result is still universal. So all our knowledge of God is of universal notions, not of the particular essence itself. Therefore, we do not know God's essence. An objection is then raised: Our knowledge that God is one and that His reality cannot be shared with anything else prevents the possibility of sharing (shirka), so this known thing is not universal but rather points to the determinate essence. The answer begins but is cut off at the end of page 92; it likely explains that the concept of oneness is still a universal notion applied to God, not knowledge of His essence per se.

Key Terms
طبيعة كلية
قيد كلي
كلي
جزئي
شخصي
ذات مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing God through His attributes gives us knowledge of His essence.
Correction: The proof argues that all attributes are universal concepts, which by definition cannot capture the particular, unique essence of God.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fifth proof?
A) We can know God's essence through attributes.
B) Our knowledge of God is always universal, so we do not know His particular essence.
C) God's essence is a universal nature.
D) Universal and particular are the same.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof establishes that all human knowledge of God consists of universal concepts, not His particular essence.

?
Razi argues that even oneness is a universal concept. Does this challenge your understanding of tawhid? How can you reconcile a oneness that is beyond your full comprehension with your daily declaration of 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh'?
