The Argument from Universals: We Only Know Universals, Not God's Essence
Al-Razi enumerates premises, deduces that we know only universals about God, then answers an objection about divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that human knowledge of God is limited to universal concepts and does not attain His particular essence, and why the objection from divine oneness does not refute this.

Core Idea
Knowing God's attributes only yields universal concepts, not His unique essence.

This lesson presents one of the most subtle arguments in Islamic theology about the limits of human knowledge of God. Al-Razi argues that every concept we form about God—even 'oneness'—is a universal that could apply to any individual. He then answers a powerful objection: doesn't knowing that God is one mean we know His particular essence? The passage is a masterclass in philosophical precision.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three premises
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Razi lays down three necessary premises: (1) adding restrictions to a universal yields a universal; (2) the universal is not identical to the particular; (3) God's essence is particular, not universal. Make sure you grasp each premise before proceeding.
The first premise is crucial: no matter how many restrictions you add, the result remains a universal.
2
Follow the proof from premises to conclusion
Al-Razi argues that everything we know of God (existence, attributes, etc.) is a universal restricted by universal predicates. Since this universal is not the particular essence, and we only know universals, we do not know God's essence. Trace the logical steps carefully.
The conclusion is negative: we cannot know the essence, only its conceptual aspects.
3
Consider the objection about oneness
An objector points out that we know God is one, and this seems to refer directly to His particular essence, not a universal. Why would that knowledge be a universal? Think about how the objector expects 'oneness' to pick out God uniquely.
The objection is natural: if oneness is unique to God, doesn't that capture His essence?
4
Examine Al-Razi's answer
Al-Razi replies that the judgment 'He is one' is a universal because it would be true of any one, regardless of which particular entity is the one. It does not identify the particular essence itself. Understand the substitution argument with 'alif' and 'ba'.
This shows that even oneness is a universal concept, not knowledge of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم الزاهد، وهو أيضا كلي . ولو أضفت إليه قيد [ بعد قيد (١) ] إلى ألف ألف مرتبة ، كان الحاصل هو الكلي . فهذا إحدى المقدمات ضروري . والمقدمة الثانية : إن الكلي ليس عين الجزئي والشخصي ، والعلم به . والمقدمة الثالثة : إن الذات المخصوصة التي هي الله تعالى ليس أمرا كليا ، بل هو ذات معينة وحقيقة مشخصة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : كل ما نعرفه من الله تعالى ، فهو أمر كلي [ مقيد بقيد كلي . مثلا : إذا قلنا : موجود فهو كلي (۲) ] فإذا قيدناه بقيد كونه موجودا واجب الوجود . كان المجموع هو أنه موجود واجب الوجود لذاته . ثم إذا قیدناه بقيد أنه ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر ولا بعرض ، كان المجموع كليا . فإذا قيدناه بالصفات الإضافية مثل قولنا : إنه عالم ، قادر ، حي ، كان ذلك تقييدا للكلي بالكلي ، فيكون المجموع الحاصل بعد ضم تلك القيود إليه أمرا كليا ، فيثبت : أن كل ما نعلمه من الله تعالى فهو أمر كلي . وثبت : أن هذا الكلي ليس هو نفس تلك الذات المخصوصة . ثبت : أن العلم بهذه المعلومات الكلية ، ليس علما بتلك الذات المخصوصة . وإذا ثبت أنا لا نعلم إلا ذلك المعلوم الكلي ، وثبت أن العلم بذلك المعلوم الكلي ، ليس نفس العلم بتلك الذات المخصوصة ، لزم القطع بأنا لا نعرف تلك الذات المخصوصة . فإن قالوا : [ لما علمنا أنه سبحانه واحد ، وأنه لا يمكن أن تكون تلك الحقيقة حاصلة لشيء آخر سواه (۳)] فهذا المعلوم مانع من احتمال الشركة ، فلم يكن هذا المعلوم كليا ، بل كان علمنا به من حيث إنه تلك الذات المعينة .. فنقول : العلم بأنه سبحانه واحد ، وبأنه لا يمكن وقوع الشركة فيما بينها وبين غيرها : علم أيضا بأمر كلي مشترك فيه ، وذلك لأن بتقدير أن يكون ذلك الواحد هو الألف كان ذلك الحكم صادقا [ وبتقدير أن لا يكون ذلك الواحد هو الألف بل كان هو الباء ، بدلا عن كونه ألفا، كان ذلك الحكم أيضا صادقا (۱) ] . فيثبت بهذا : أن ذلك المعلوم لا يمنع من وقوع الشركة فيه [ وأما تلك الذات المخصوصة من حيث إنها تلك الذات المعينة ، فإنها مانعة من وقوع الشركة فيه (۲) ] فعلمناه أن العلم بأنها ذات واحدة ، غير قابلة للشركة ، لا يكون علما بتلك الذات المعينة المخصوصة
The ascetic scholar, and it is also a universal. And if you add to it a restriction after restriction (1) up to a thousand thousand degrees, the result is the universal. So this is one of the premises, necessary. The second premise: that the universal is not identical to the particular and the individual, and knowledge of it. The third premise: that the particular essence which is God Most High is not a universal matter, but rather a determinate essence and a specific reality. When you know this, we say: Everything we know of God Most High is a universal matter [restricted by a universal restriction. For example: when we say 'existent' it is a universal (2)]. So when we restrict it with the restriction of being existent necessarily-existent, the aggregate is that it is existent necessarily-existent by itself. Then when we restrict it with the restriction that it is not a body nor a substance nor an accident, the aggregate is a universal. So when we restrict it with relational attributes such as our saying: He is knowing, powerful, living, that is a restriction of the universal by the universal, so the aggregate obtained after attaching those restrictions to it is a universal matter. Thus it is established: that everything we know of God Most High is a universal matter. And it is established: that this universal is not itself that particular essence. It is established: that knowledge of these universal items of knowledge is not knowledge of that particular essence. And when it is established that we do not know except that universal known thing, and it is established that knowledge of that universal known thing is not itself knowledge of that particular essence, then it necessarily follows that we do not know that particular essence. If they say: [Since we know that He is one, and that it is impossible for that reality to belong to anything other than Him (3)], so this known thing prevents the possibility of sharing, so this known thing was not a universal, rather our knowledge of it is insofar as it is that determinate essence... Then we say: Knowledge that He is one, and that sharing cannot occur between it and others, is also knowledge of a universal common matter, because on the supposition that that one is the alif, that judgment is true [and on the supposition that that one is not the alif but rather it is the ba, instead of being alif, that judgment is also true (1)]. So by this it is established: that that known thing does not prevent sharing from occurring in it [And as for that particular essence insofar as it is that determinate essence, it prevents sharing from occurring in it (2)]. So we know that knowledge that it is a single essence, not admitting sharing, is not knowledge of that determinate particular essence.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents a complete argument (likely the fifth proof) that human beings cannot know God's essence, only universal concepts. It opens with three premises: First, the universal remains universal even when many restrictions are added (e.g., adding restrictions infinitely still yields a universal). Second, the universal is not identical to the particular individual. Third, God's essence is a particular, determinate reality, not a universal. From these, the author argues: everything we know of God is a universal restricted by universal predicates (existence, necessary existence, not being body/substance/accident, attributes like knowledge, power, life). The aggregate after all restrictions is still a universal, not God's particular essence. Therefore, knowledge of these universal concepts is not knowledge of God's essence. Since we only know such universals, we do not know the essence. The author then anticipates an objection: if we know that God is one and unique, that knowledge seems to refer to His particular essence, not a universal. The answer: knowledge of oneness is also a universal notion—it applies to any unique entity, regardless of which particular entity it is. On the supposition that the one is 'alif' or 'ba', the judgment 'it is one' remains true. Thus it does not pick out the particular essence. The particular essence itself prevents sharing, but our knowledge of oneness does not capture that particularity. Hence, the objection fails.

Key Terms
كلي (universal)
جزئي (particular)
ذات مخصوصة (particular essence)
علم (knowledge)
تقييد (restriction)
شركة (sharing/participation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowing God's attributes (like oneness) means we know His essence.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that even oneness is a universal concept because the judgment applies to any one, not specifically to God's essence. Knowledge of attributes does not provide knowledge of the particular essence.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, why does the knowledge that God is one not constitute knowledge of His particular essence?
A) Because oneness is a universal attribute that can apply to any unique being.
B) Because we cannot be certain that God is one.
C) Because oneness is not a real attribute of God.
D) Because the objection was not answered.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi concludes that human knowledge of God is limited to universal concepts and cannot reach His particular essence, even when we know that He is one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'knowing' or 'powerful', do you feel that you have actually encountered God, or are you aware that these are only human concepts applied to Him? How does this awareness change the way you speak about Him?
?
You often say 'Allahu Akbar'—but if you admit that you don't know His essence, are you truly magnifying Someone you don't know? How can your heart be sincere in takbir while your mind accepts this limitation?
?
If you had to face God and admit that all your theological knowledge is only about universals, not about His very self, would you be satisfied? What would you say to Him about how you used that knowledge?

The Finite Cannot Comprehend the Infinite
Al-Razi argues that finite minds cannot grasp the infinite essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the finite human mind cannot comprehend the infinite essence of God, based on the argument from pre-eternity.

Core Idea
The finite cannot encompass the infinite—no matter how much we imagine, it remains beyond us.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from the earlier scholars (al-mutaqaddimun): God is infinite, our minds are finite, and the finite cannot grasp the infinite. Al-Razi explains this through the attribute of pre-eternity (azaliyya). Even if we try to imagine vast durations, pre-eternity stays unchanged—showing our minds can never reach it. If we cannot know this one attribute, we certainly cannot know the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the argument flow naturally.
The passage contains a thought experiment using large numbers.
2
Understand the core distinction
Identify the key contrast: God is infinite (ghayr mutanahi), human minds are finite (mutanahiya). The principle: the finite cannot encompass the infinite.
This principle is the backbone of the proof.
3
Follow the thought experiment
Imagine subtracting vast numbers of years from pre-eternity. Notice that pre-eternity remains unchanged. This shows that everything our minds can conceive is finite, and thus cannot reach pre-eternity.
The experiment demonstrates the infinite nature of pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : قال المتقدمون : إنه تعالى غير متناهي ، والعقول البشرية متناهية ، والمتناهي يمتنع أن يحيط بغير المتناهي . وتفسير هذا الكلام : إن من جملة صفات الله تعالى كونه قديما أزليا ، فإذا أردنا أن يحيط عقلنا بالأزل . ففرضنا مائة ألف ألف سنة ، ونحسب كل لحظة ولمحة من هذه المدة الطويلة ، وفرضنا مائة ألف ألف سنة ، وبالغنا في استحضار هذه الأعداد في عقولنا وأفكارنا وإن كانت مع كثرتها متناهية محدودة ثم أسقطناها من معنى الأزل بقى الأزل كما كان ، من غير أن ينتقص منه شيء ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ ظهر أن كل ما تصل إليه عقولنا وأفكارنا فإنه متناهي . وكل متناهي فإنه خارج عن الأزل . وهذا يفيد أن عقلنا لا يصل البتة إلى تصور معنى الأزل ، بل كل ما يتصوره فإنه يكون خارجا عن معنى الأزلية . وإذا ظهر بهذا البيان [ عجز عقول الخلق ، عن معرفة هذه الصفة الواحدة ، وهي معنى الأزلية ٣ ] فبأن تكون العقول عاجزة عن معرفة الموصوف ، كان ذلك أليق وأحق
The sixth proof: The earlier scholars said: He Most High is infinite, and human minds are finite, and the finite is prevented from encompassing the infinite. Explanation of this statement: Among the attributes of God Most High is His being pre-eternal and eternal. So if we want our mind to encompass pre-eternity, we posit a hundred thousand thousand years, and we count every moment and glance of this long duration, and we posit a hundred thousand thousand years, and we exaggerate in bringing these numbers to mind in our intellects and thoughts—even though with their multiplicity they are finite and limited—then we subtract them from the meaning of pre-eternity; pre-eternity remains as it was, without any diminution from it. When it is thus, then it becomes apparent that everything our minds and thoughts reach is finite. And everything finite is outside pre-eternity. This indicates that our mind does not at all attain to conceiving the meaning of pre-eternity; rather, everything it conceives is outside the meaning of pre-eternity. And if it is shown by this explanation [the incapacity of the minds of creatures to know this one attribute, which is the meaning of pre-eternity (3)], then that minds are incapable of knowing the described essence is more fitting and more appropriate.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof that we cannot know God's essence. The proof is from the ancients (al-mutaqaddimun): God is infinite (ghayr mutanahi) and human minds are finite (mutanahiya), and the finite cannot encompass the infinite. The author explains by focusing on the attribute of pre-eternity (azaliyya). Even if we imagine vast durations like a hundred thousand thousand years and subtract them from pre-eternity, pre-eternity remains undiminished. Everything our minds can grasp is finite, and therefore falls outside the meaning of pre-eternity. Our minds never reach the concept of pre-eternity itself. If we are incapable of knowing even this single attribute (pre-eternity), it is even more fitting that we are incapable of knowing the essence described by it. The conclusion is that the finite mind cannot comprehend the infinite essence.

Key Terms
غير متناهي
متناهي
أزل
عقل
إحاطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That long contemplation or calculation can help grasp God's infinity.
Correction: The proof shows no finite process can ever reach the infinite; pre-eternity remains untouched by any finite subtraction.

Checkpoint
What does the example of subtracting vast durations from pre-eternity demonstrate?
A) That pre-eternity is very long but still finite.
B) That pre-eternity remains unchanged, indicating it is infinite and beyond finite measure.
C) That our minds can eventually understand pre-eternity with enough numbers.
D) That God's essence is finite.

Key Takeaway
The finite human mind cannot grasp the infinite; therefore, God's essence remains beyond our comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's eternity, what finite images or concepts do you habitually rely on (e.g., 'a long time', 'always existing')? How does Al-Razi's proof show that these mental pictures fall short? Will you allow this to humble your confidence in your own theological ideas?
?
Al-Razi says that even after stretching our imagination to its limit, pre-eternity remains untouched. Does this make you feel distant from God or more awed? How will you respond to this limitation in your worship and daily life?
?
The proof concludes that if we cannot grasp one of God's attributes, we cannot grasp His essence. How should this affect the way you speak about God's nature? Will you commit to pausing before claiming you 'know' what God is like?

Knowledge of a Universal Does Not Constitute Knowledge of the Particular Essence
An argument that knowledge of a universal attribute does not yield knowledge of the specific essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of universal attributes (like oneness) does not equate to knowledge of God's specific essence.

Core Idea
Knowing that something is one is not the same as knowing what that specific thing is.

In this passage, the author argues that knowing a universal property – such as 'oneness' – does not mean you know the particular essence that has that property. He uses a clever example with letters to show that multiple individuals can share a universal attribute, but that attribute does not uniquely identify any one. This lesson unpacks that argument and its theological implication for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the flow: the author presents an example and draws a conclusion.
The example uses 'alif' and 'ba' – imagine these as two distinct individuals.
2
Understand the example
The example shows that if you know something is 'one' (like 'alif'), and you also know that if it were 'ba', the same judgment would be true, then the judgment is universal and shared. This universality means the knowledge does not pinpoint the specific essence.
The key is that the universal attribute (being 'one') does not exclude sharing among multiple essences.
3
Grasp the distinction
Contrast the universal knowledge with knowledge of a particular essence: the particular essence (e.g., the specific 'alif') prohibits sharing because it is uniquely itself. Therefore, knowing that something is a single essence that does not allow sharing is not the same as knowing that particular essence itself.
This distinction is crucial for understanding tawhid: knowing God is one does not mean we comprehend His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيرها : علم أيضا بأمر كلي مشترك فيه ، وذلك لأن بتقدير أن يكون ذلك الواحد هو الألف كان ذلك الحكم صادقا [ وبتقدير أن لا يكون ذلك الواحد هو الألف بل كان هو الباء ، بدلا عن كونه ألفا، كان ذلك الحكم أيضا صادقا (۱) ] . فيثبت بهذا : أن ذلك المعلوم لا يمنع من وقوع الشركة فيه [ وأما تلك الذات المخصوصة من حيث إنها تلك الذات المعينة ، فإنها مانعة من وقوع الشركة فيه (۲) ] فعلمناه أن العلم بأنها ذات واحدة ، غير قابلة للشركة ، لا يكون علما بتلك الذات المعينة المخصوصة
Another [argument]: knowledge is also of a universal matter shared in it, and that because on the assumption that that one is the alif, that judgment is true, [and on the assumption that that one is not the alif but rather is the ba, instead of being alif, that judgment is also true (1)]. So it is established by this: that that known thing does not prevent the occurrence of sharing in it [and as for that particular essence inasmuch as it is that specific essence, then it prevents the occurrence of sharing in it (2)]. So we know that the knowledge that it is a single essence, not susceptible to sharing, is not knowledge of that specific particular essence.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the difference between knowledge of a universal and knowledge of a particular essence. It begins by stating that knowledge can be of a universal matter that is shared (مشترك فيه). The argument uses an example: if we suppose that the one in question is 'alif', the judgment is true; and if we suppose it is 'ba', the judgment is also true. This shows that the known thing does not prevent sharing (شركة). However, a particular essence (ذات مخصوصة) in its specific identity does prevent sharing. Therefore, knowing that something is one and indivisible is not the same as knowing that specific essence. The implication is that knowing God as one and not sharing does not mean we know His particular essence.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
شركة
ذات مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowing God's oneness is equivalent to knowing His essence.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that knowledge of a universal attribute (like oneness) does not give knowledge of the particular essence (God Himself). So affirming tawhid does not mean we comprehend the divine essence.

Checkpoint
What does the passage argue about knowledge of a universal vs. a particular essence?
A) Knowledge of a universal is impossible.
B) Knowledge of a universal gives full knowledge of the particular.
C) Knowledge that something is one and not shared is not knowledge of its particular essence.
D) Knowledge of a particular essence is universal.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a universal attribute like oneness does not constitute knowledge of the particular essence that possesses it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often affirm that God is One, but is your heart truly seeking His essence beyond the concept? Have you ever stopped to ask yourself: 'Do I know the One I worship, or do I only know that He is one?' What would it cost you to press deeper into knowing Him personally, not just knowing about Him?
?
If the oneness of God is a universal that could in principle apply to many, what makes God's oneness different and how does that difference change your worship? Will you let this distinction humble you into realizing that your knowledge of God is incomplete, and that you must seek Him more earnestly?
?
When you recite 'He is Allah, the One' (Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad), do you assume you have grasped His essence? The lesson warns that knowing oneness is not knowing the specific essence. How will you respond—by becoming arrogant in your theological certainty, or by falling into the awe of His incomprehensible majesty and seeking Him with all your heart?

The Sixth Argument: Human Intellect Cannot Grasp the Infinite
A proof that human intellects cannot grasp the infinite, using the example of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that finite human minds cannot comprehend God's infinite essence, using the example of eternity.

Core Idea
Finite minds cannot encompass the infinite — even eternity escapes our grasp.

This lesson presents a classical kalam argument for divine transcendence. The predecessors argued that because God is infinite and our minds are finite, we cannot fully know His essence. The argument is illustrated by the attribute of eternity: no matter how many years we imagine, we never capture eternity itself. This reasoning underscores the limits of human reason when faced with the divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's premise
Begin by reading the opening premise: God is infinite, human intellects are finite, and the finite cannot encompass the infinite. This sets the logical foundation.
Notice how the argument starts with a general rule before applying it.
2
Consider the eternity example
Read the elaboration where the author uses the attribute of eternity. Try to mentally perform the exercise: imagine vast numbers of years and then subtract them from eternity.
The point is that even after subtracting all finite durations, eternity remains unchanged.
3
Understand the conclusion
Reflect on the final conclusion: since every mental construct is finite and outside eternity, the intellect cannot even grasp one divine attribute, let alone the essence.
This reinforces the doctrine of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : قال المتقدمون : إنه تعالى غير متناهي ، والعقول البشرية متناهية ، والمتناهي يمتنع أن يحيط بغير المتناهي . وتفسير هذا الكلام : إن من جملة صفات الله تعالى كونه قديما أزليا ، فإذا أردنا أن يحيط عقلنا بالأزل . ففرضنا مائة ألف ألف سنة ، ونحسب كل لحظة ولمحة من هذه المدة الطويلة ، وفرضنا مائة ألف ألف سنة ، وبالغنا في استحضار هذه الأعداد في عقولنا وأفكارنا وإن كانت مع كثرتها متناهية محدودة ثم أسقطناها من معنى الأزل بقى الأزل كما كان ، من غير أن ينتقص منه شيء ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ ظهر أن كل ما تصل إليه عقولنا وأفكارنا فإنه متناهي . وكل متناهي فإنه خارج عن الأزل . وهذا يفيد أن عقلنا لا يصل البتة إلى تصور معنى الأزل ، بل كل ما يتصوره فإنه يكون خارجا عن معنى الأزلية . وإذا ظهر بهذا البيان [ عجز عقول الخلق ، عن معرفة هذه الصفة الواحدة ، وهي معنى الأزلية ٣ ] فبأن تكون العقول عاجزة عن معرفة الموصوف ، كان ذلك أليق وأحق
The sixth proof: The predecessors said: He, exalted, is infinite, and human intellects are finite, and the finite cannot encompass the infinite. Explanation of this statement: Among the attributes of God, may He be exalted, is that He is eternal (qadim) and everlasting (azali). So if we want our intellect to encompass eternity, we suppose a hundred thousand thousand years, and we count every moment and glance of this long period, and we suppose a hundred thousand thousand years, and we exaggerate in bringing these numbers to mind and thought, even though with their multitude they are finite and limited. Then we subtract them from the meaning of eternity, eternity remains as it was, without any decrease. And if this is the case, then it becomes clear that everything our intellects and thoughts reach is finite, and everything finite is outside of eternity. This indicates that our intellect does not at all attain conceptualization of the meaning of eternity; rather, everything it conceptualizes is outside the meaning of eternality. And if it becomes clear by this explanation [that the intellects of creatures are incapable of knowing this single attribute, which is the meaning of eternity (3)], then that they are incapable of knowing the one described is more fitting and appropriate.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This is a proof (الحجة السادسة) that human intellects cannot comprehend God's essence. It begins with a premise: God is infinite, human intellects are finite, and the finite cannot encompass the infinite. Then it elaborates: among God's attributes is eternity (الأزل). The intellect tries to grasp eternity by imagining immense durations—hundreds of thousands of years—and stretching the mind, but these durations are finite. Even after subtracting all finite numbers, eternity remains unchanged. Thus, everything the mind reaches is finite and outside of eternity. Hence, the mind cannot even conceptualize a single divine attribute (eternity), so it is even less able to know the one described (the essence). The conclusion is that human minds are incapable of knowing God's essence.

Key Terms
متناهي
غير متناهي
أزل
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mind can grasp infinity through abstraction or analogy.
Correction: The argument proves that all mental constructs are finite, so even abstraction cannot capture true infinity like eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, why can't the human intellect grasp God's essence?
A) Because God's essence is simple.
B) Because the intellect is too weak to think about God.
C) Because the finite cannot encompass the infinite; even the attribute of eternity is beyond finite conception.
D) Because God has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
Human minds, being finite, cannot fully grasp God's infinite essence; even a single attribute like eternity lies beyond complete conceptualization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think you understand God, do you realize that even a single attribute like His eternity escapes your mind? How does this truth shatter your intellectual pride?
?
If every mental picture you have of God is finite and therefore not truly Him, how does that change the way you approach Him in worship? Are you worshipping your own ideas of God, or the incomprehensible reality?
?
The argument forces you to admit that your intellect cannot grasp even a speck of God's essence. Does that admission terrify you, or does it free you from the illusion of control? What will you do with that freedom?

Knowledge as Domination: The Seventh Argument
An argument that if humans fully knew God, they would dominate Him, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that knowledge implies domination, and why full knowledge of God would imply human domination over God, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Knowledge is conquest; to fully know God would be to conquer Him.

The seventh argument in al-Matalib al-Aliyah draws a striking analogy: knowledge is a form of domination. If we could fully know God, we would, in a sense, dominate Him. But that is impossible, for only God has true power. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument uses a rhetorical question to make its point.
2
Examine the analogy
The argument compares ignorance to being subdued and knowledge to dominating. Identify how this analogy is built and why it is effective.
The analogy relies on common human experience of learning.
3
Apply the conclusion
Understand the logical step: if knowledge is domination, then knowing God fully would imply dominating God. Since that is impossible, intellects cannot reach His essence.
This conclusion reinforces divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : قالوا : العلم نوع استيلاء على المعلوم ، ألا ترى أن من لم يكن عالما بشيء كانت روحه بالنسبة إلى ذلك المعلوم ، كالعاجز المقهور ، فإذا علمه وأحاط به صار كالمستولى عليه ، والقاهر له ؟ إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو وصلت العقول إلى كنه حقيقته ، لحصل للخلق استيلاء على الحق من بعض الوجوه ، أما لما عجزت العقول عن معرفته ، كان الخلق أبدا في ذلك القهر وعجز المعرفة ، وكان الاستيلاء والقهر للحق ، وذلك هو الواجب
The seventh proof: They said: Knowledge is a type of domination over the known. Do you not see that one who is not knowledgeable of something, his soul in relation to that known is like an incapacitated, subdued person; then when he knows it and encompasses it, he becomes like one dominating it and controlling it? If this is established, then we say: If intellects reached the depth of His reality, then creatures would have domination over the Truth in some respects. But as for when intellects are incapable of knowing Him, creatures remain perpetually in that subjugation and incapacity of knowledge, and domination and subjugation belong to the Truth, and that is what is obligatory.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This is a proof that human intellects cannot fully know God. It uses the analogy that knowledge is a form of domination (استيلاء) over the known object. When one does not know something, his soul is like a subdued captive; when he knows it, he becomes like a conqueror. Therefore, if humans could fully know God's reality (كنه حقيقته), they would dominate God in some respect, which is inappropriate (since only God should have domination). Thus, intellects are necessarily incapable of such knowledge, so that domination and power remain solely with God. This argument ties the impossibility of fully knowing God to the theological principle of divine supremacy.

Key Terms
استيلاء
قهر
كنه الحقيقة
الموصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is purely passive and does not involve any element of power or control.
Correction: This argument shows that knowledge, in human experience, involves a form of domination over the known; therefore, fully knowing God would imply dominating Him, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the seventh argument use to show that full knowledge of God is impossible?
A) Knowledge is like light.
B) Knowledge is a type of domination; knowing fully would imply dominating God.
C) Knowledge is like a mirror.
D) Knowledge is like a vision.

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument concludes that human intellects cannot fully know God because such knowledge would imply domination over God, contradicting His absolute supremacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge is a form of domination, examine your own pursuit of religious knowledge. Are you seeking to submit to God or to 'conquer' Him by reducing Him to a concept you can fully control? What evidence do you see in your heart?
?
When you encounter aspects of God's nature that confuse or frustrate you because they don't fit your mental framework, do you accept that limitation as a sign of His transcendence, or do you feel a subtle irritation that He is not 'yielding' to your intellect? How can you cultivate contentment with unknowing?
?
Consider a recent moment when you felt proud of your theological understanding. Did that pride stem from a sense of having 'mastered' a truth, or from gratitude for being given a glimpse? How can you repent if the former is true?

The Intellect's Limitation: A Case for Divine Transcendence
An argument that since the mind cannot contemplate two objects at once, it cannot grasp the greatest object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mind's inability to handle multiple objects at once proves its incapacity to grasp the essence of the greatest reality.

Core Idea
The mind cannot grasp two objects at once, so it cannot grasp the infinite.

Sanusi presents a simple yet profound argument from human cognitive limitation. By observing that we cannot simultaneously hold two known objects in our mind, he argues that we are fundamentally incapable of comprehending the essence of the Supreme Being. This argument reminds us that our intellectual capacity is finite and cannot encompass the Infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear its rhythm, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to evidence to conclusion.
2
Identify the premise and evidence
The argument first states a general claim about the intellect's limitation. Then it provides experiential evidence: when focused on one object, it cannot turn to another.
The evidence is drawn from common human experience.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is a rhetorical question: if the intellect cannot handle even two finite objects, how could it possibly reach the depth of the greatest reality? Reflect on why this is a powerful theological point.
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this argument supports the idea that God's essence is beyond human comprehension. This is a key theme in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قالوا : العقل لا يقدر على استحضار معلومين دفعة واحدة . بدليل : أنه إذا اشتغل باستحضار معلوم ، امتنع عليه في تلك الساعة استحضار معلوم آخر ، فإذا كان العقل عاجزا عن استحضار معلومين دفعة واحدة فكيف يمكنه الوصول إلى كنه حقيقة أعظم المعلومات ، وأعلاها ؟
The eighth proof: They said: The intellect cannot bring to mind two known objects at once. Evidence: when it occupies itself with bringing to mind one known object, it is impossible for it at that moment to bring to mind another known object. If the intellect is incapable of bringing to mind two known objects at once, then how can it reach the depth of the reality of the greatest and highest of known objects?
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This proof argues that the human intellect is limited in capacity: it cannot focus on two distinct objects simultaneously. The evidence is common experience: when thinking about one thing, it cannot simultaneously think about another. If it cannot handle even two finite objects, how could it possibly grasp the essence of the greatest and most sublime reality, which is God? The argument emphasizes the inherent limitation of the intellect.

Key Terms
استحضار
معلوم
عجز
كنه الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mind can multitask sufficiently to comprehend complex realities, or that God's essence is simple enough to be grasped in a single thought.
Correction: The argument shows that even for finite objects, the mind cannot handle two at once, so it certainly cannot handle the infinite, transcendent reality of God.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the eighth argument?
A) The intellect can handle infinite objects.
B) The intellect cannot attend to two known objects at once.
C) The intellect is most powerful when focused on one object.
D) The intellect can reach God's essence through meditation.

Key Takeaway
The mind's inability to focus on two objects at once shows it cannot grasp the greatest reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sit down to reflect on Allah's greatness, do you feel the frustration of your mind's limitation? How does this passage transform that frustration into a reason for awe and submission?
?
In your daily life, do you try to 'figure out' Allah's essence through reasoning? What would it look like to accept that your mind cannot grasp Him and instead focus on His signs?
?
This argument says the mind cannot even hold two finite objects at once. Compare that to how often you try to multitask in worship. What needs to change so that your heart is fully present before the One who transcends all thought?

The Eighth Proof: The Mind Cannot Hold Two Known Things at Once
Al-Razi presents the eighth proof that the mind cannot grasp God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof that the human intellect cannot simultaneously grasp two known things, and why this implies it cannot comprehend God's essence.

Core Idea
The mind's inability to hold two thoughts at once proves it cannot grasp the greatest thought.

Al-Razi now presents a concise yet powerful argument for divine transcendence. The proof rests on a simple observation about how our minds work: we cannot think of two things at the same time. If that is true for ordinary objects, how could we ever hope to comprehend the highest reality of all?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
Notice the rhetorical question at the end – it's key.
2
Identify the main premise
The proof starts with a claim: the intellect cannot bring to mind two known things at once. Underline this in your mind.
3
Examine the evidence
The evidence is that when the mind is occupied with one known thing, it cannot at that moment bring another to mind. This is an empirical observation.
Al-Razi assumes this is self-evident.
4
Follow the conclusion
If the mind can't handle two ordinary known things, how could it possibly reach the core of the greatest known thing – God's essence? The implication is that God's essence is beyond direct intellectual comprehension.
5
Reflect on the overarching point
This proof supports divine transcendence (tanzih): God's essence is not accessible to human reason. Consider how this fits with other proofs in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قالوا : العقل لا يقدر على استحضار معلومين دفعة واحدة . بدليل : أنه إذا اشتغل باستحضار معلوم ، امتنع عليه في تلك الساعة استحضار معلوم آخر ، فإذا كان العقل عاجزا عن استحضار معلومين دفعة واحدة فكيف يمكنه الوصول إلى كنه حقيقة أعظم المعلومات ، وأعلاها ؟ .
The eighth proof: They said: The intellect is unable to bring to mind two known things at once. Evidence: if it is occupied with bringing a known thing to mind, it is prevented in that moment from bringing another known thing to mind. So if the intellect is incapable of bringing two known things to mind at once, how can it reach the core of the greatest and highest of known things?
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof from those who argue that the human intellect cannot arrive at knowledge of God's essence. The proof is by analogy: the mind can only attend to one thing at a time. If it cannot even hold two ordinary known things simultaneously, then it certainly cannot comprehend the highest and most immense of all known things, which is God's essence. The argument implies that the essence is so vast that understanding it would require the mind to hold multiple aspects at once, which it cannot do.

Key Terms
حقيقة
معلوم
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The human intellect can comprehend God's essence by focusing on multiple proofs at once.
Correction: The proof shows that the mind cannot even handle two ordinary known things simultaneously, so it is even less capable of grasping the greatest essence, which would require holding many aspects at once.

Checkpoint

A) Because it cannot hold two known things at once.
B) Because the intellect is too weak.
C) Because God's essence is not a known thing.
D) Because the intellect is not commanded to know God.

Key Takeaway
The intellect's inability to hold two known things at once demonstrates that it cannot comprehend the reality of God's essence.

How does this reality humble you when you try to comprehend the majesty of Allah? What practical step will you take this week to acknowledge your mental limits and turn your full attention to Him in worship?
?
This proof shows that the intellect cannot grasp the core of God's essence. Are there areas where you have tried to 'figure out' Allah or reduce Him to something your mind can control? What would it look like to surrender that intellectual pride and simply worship Him as He is, beyond your comprehension?
?
When you read the Quran or engage in dhikr, do you often find your mind split between multiple concerns? How does this lesson challenge you to approach Allah with a singular focus? Will you set aside five minutes tomorrow to practice bringing only your Lord to mind, trusting that He is greater than all your scattered thoughts?

Divine Transcendence

First Proof: The Proposition 'The Essence is Unknown' is Self-Contradictory
Al-Razi presents the first argument that the proposition 'the essence is unknown' is self-contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi demonstrates that the claim 'God's essence is unknown' is logically self-contradictory because every judgment requires prior conceptualization of its subject and predicate.

Core Idea
To deny knowledge of the essence, you must first know it.

Al-Razi opens his first proof with a fundamental logical principle: every judgment (tasdiq) depends on prior conceptualization (tasawwur) of its subject and predicate. He then applies this to the common claim that God's essence is unknown, showing that the very act of making such a claim presupposes knowledge of the subject, leading to a contradiction. This lesson walks through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical premise
Learn the foundational rule that tasdiq is preceded by tasawwur. This is the key to the argument.
2
Apply the premise to the claim
Identify the subject and predicate of the proposition 'His essence is unknown,' and see how the rule forces knowledge of both.
Notice that the subject 'His essence' must be conceived by the speaker.
3
Explore the counter-argument and its refutation
Consider the objection that the essence is known through attributes. Al-Razi examines two interpretations of the subject and shows both lead to contradiction.
This covers all possibilities, making the proof exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن كل تصديق ، فإنه يجب أن يكون مسبوقا بتصور الموضوع والمحمول. فإذا قال القائل : إن حقيقته غير معقولة للخلق ، كان موضوع هذه القضية : هو قولنا حقيقته . ومحمولها : هو قولنا : غير معقولة للخلق ، والحاكم باستناد هذا المحمول إلى هذا الموضوع ، يجب كونه عالما بهذا الموضوع، وبهذا المحمول ، حتى يمكنه هذا الاستناد . فيثبت : أن صدق قولنا : إن حقيقته غير معلومة للخلق ، يقتضي كون حقيقته معقولة للخلق من حيث إن موضوع القضية يجب أن يكون معلوما ، وما أدى نفيه إلى ثبوته كان نفيه باطلا . فوجب أن يكون قولنا : حقيقته غير معقوله للخلق : قولا باطلا . فإن قالوا : إن تلك الحقيقة معقولة بحسب بعض صفاتها وأحوالها . فنقول : إذا قلنا إن حقيقته غير معقولة فموضوع هذه القضية ، إما أن يكون تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي ، أو تلك الحقيقة بحسب بعض صفاتها وأحوالها . فإن كان الأول لزم كون تلك الحقيقة من حيث إنها هي معقولة للخلق . لما بينا أن موضوع القضية من الاعتبار الذي به صار موضوعاً للقضية ، يجب أن يكون متصورا. فإذا كان موضوع هذه القضية هو تلك الحقيقة من حيث إنها هي ، وجب كون تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي ، معلومة ، وحينئذ يلزم التناقض . وأما إن قيل : إن موضوع هذه القضية هو تلك الحقيقة بحسب بعض صفاتها المعلومة ، أو بحسب بعض أحوالها المعلومة ، فحينئذ قد حكم على الأمر المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم بأنه غير معلوم ، وذلك أيضا يوجب التناقض . فقد ظهر بما ذكرنا : أن قولنا : إن تلك الحقيقة ، غير معلومة : يوجب التناقض على جميع التقديرات .
The first proof: Every judgment (tasdiq) must be preceded by a conceptualization (tasawwur) of its subject and predicate. So if someone says, 'His essence is incomprehensible to creatures,' the subject of this proposition is our saying 'His essence,' and its predicate is our saying 'incomprehensible to creatures.' And the judge who attributes this predicate to this subject must be knowledgeable of this subject and this predicate so that he can make this attribution. Thus it is established that the truth of our saying 'His essence is unknown to creatures' necessitates that His essence be comprehensible to creatures, since the subject of the proposition must be known. And what leads from its negation to its affirmation renders the negation false. Therefore our saying 'His essence is incomprehensible to creatures' must be a false statement. If they say: That essence is comprehensible through some of its attributes and states, we say: When we say 'His essence is incomprehensible,' the subject of this proposition is either that essence itself (min haythu hiya hiya) or that essence through some of its attributes and states. If it is the first, then it would necessitate that that essence itself is comprehensible to creatures, because we have shown that the subject of the proposition, by the aspect by which it is subject, must be conceptualized. So if the subject of this proposition is that essence itself, then that essence itself must be known, and this entails a contradiction. If it is said that the subject of this proposition is that essence through some of its known attributes or known states, then one would have judged a known thing, insofar as it is known, to be unknown, and this also entails a contradiction. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that our saying 'that essence is unknown' entails contradiction in all suppositions.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction that it is impossible to coherently state that God's essence is unknown. The argument proceeds in two stages. First, it analyzes the proposition 'His essence is unknown' itself: every judgment requires prior conceptualization of both subject and predicate. The person making the claim must have a concept of what 'His essence' is in order to say it is unknown. Therefore, the very act of asserting that the essence is unknown implies that the essence is known as subject. This leads to contradiction: the assertion denies knowledge while presupposing it. Second, the argument considers a possible rejoinder: perhaps the essence is known only through some of its attributes, not in itself. The argument then splits the possibilities: if the subject is the essence in itself, then it must be known in itself (contradiction). If the subject is the essence under some known attribute, then the attribute is known, and one is judging a known thing to be unknown, which is also contradictory. Thus, under every interpretation, the statement 'the essence is unknown' is self-contradictory. This is a subtle dialectical argument that challenges the very coherence of claiming divine incomprehensibility in absolute terms.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
موضوع
محمول
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'God's essence is unknown' is a straightforward, coherent statement about divine transcendence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that this statement is logically self-contradictory because it requires the speaker to have knowledge of the essence as the subject, which the statement itself denies.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what happens if someone says 'God's essence is unknown'?
A) It is a valid statement.
B) It leads to a contradiction because the subject must be known.
C) It is true only if the attributes are unknown.
D) It is impossible to formulate such a sentence.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God's essence is unknown is logically incoherent because asserting it requires the very knowledge it denies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that denying knowledge of God is self-defeating. Have you ever used 'I can't know God' as an excuse not to seek Him? What concrete step will you take this week to actually learn about His attributes?
?
If the very act of saying 'God is unknown' proves that you do know something about Him, then what does your silence or ignorance say about your relationship with your Creator? Will you commit to studying one divine attribute each day?
?
Al-Razi forces you to admit that you cannot even deny God without admitting some knowledge. How does this change the way you approach doubts about God? Will you now stop using 'God is a mystery' as a comfortable shield and instead actively engage with what He has revealed?

Knowledge of Allah's Essence is Unattainable
The author concludes the discussion on the impossibility of knowing Allah's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human intellect cannot attain true knowledge of Allah's essence and how the author resolves objections while leaving some questions unanswered.

Core Idea
Human reason cannot comprehend Allah's essence; only divine bestowal grants true knowledge.

This passage concludes a deep discussion on the limits of human knowledge regarding Allah's essence. The author summarizes key arguments, addresses objections, and then raises the question of whether angels can attain such knowledge, leaving it undecided. Finally, he reminds us that when reason reaches its limits, we must turn to Allah for true understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the flow from summarizing an argument to answering objections, then to raising further questions and a final exhortation.
The passage begins mid-sentence, so pay attention to the logical connection.
2
Analyze the core argument
Identify the author's argument about the subject of the proposition. Understand why both possibilities (conceived or not conceived) lead to problems, and how the answer to the third objection resolves the issue by defining knowledge of the essence as a negative concept.
The concept of 'negative concept' (مفهوم سلبي) is key: it means we know what Allah is not, not what He is.
3
Reflect on the conclusion and final reflection
Consider the author's statement that this knowledge is unattainable for humans, and his hesitation about angels. Then read the final paragraph where he describes the bewilderment of intellect and the need to rely on Allah for true knowledge.
This reflects the Ash'ari emphasis on the limits of reason in divine matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متصوراً ، أو بكونه ممتازاً عن غيره .
فالحاصل : أن موضوع هذه القضية [ إن لم يكن متصوراً لزم التناقض وإن كان متصوراً لزم كون هذه القضية ] كاذبة ، وكلاهما أمران باطلان
،
والجواب من الثالث : إنا إذا علمنا الذات ، فالمراد منه كونه مستقلا
بنفسه ، غير محتاج إلى غيره، وهذا مفهوم سلبي ، والسلوب خارجة عن الماهيات . وهذا هو بعينه الجواب عن الوجه الرابع
·
فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . ثم نقول : هذا جملة الكلام في أن هذا العلم غير حاصل للبشر ، فأما أنه هل هو حاصل للملائكة أم لا ؟ وبتقدير أن يقال : إنه غير حاصل لهم. فهل يمكن حصول هذه المعرفة للملائكة أو
للخلق ؟ فهذا متوقف فيه
•
واعلم : أن المباحث في الإلهيات إذا انتهت إلى هذه المضائق ، فحينئذ تدهش العقول وتقف الأفكار ، وليس بعد ذلك إلا الالتجاء إلى الله تعالى في
إفاضة المعارف الحقيقية . [ وبالله التوفيق ] .
Conceived, or by being distinguished from others. So the conclusion: the subject of this proposition [if it is not conceived, contradiction follows, and if it is conceived, then this proposition would be false], and both are invalid. And the answer to the third: when we know the essence, what is meant by it is its being independent by itself, not needing others, and this is a negative concept, and negatives are outside of essences. And this is precisely the answer to the fourth aspect. So this is the end of the discussion in this chapter. Then we say: this is the summary of the discussion that this knowledge is not attained by humans. As for whether it is attained by angels or not? And if it is said that it is not attained by them, then is it possible for this knowledge to be attained by angels or by creation? This is a point of hesitation. And know: when discussions in divinity reach these straits, then minds are bewildered and thoughts halt, and there is nothing after that except seeking refuge in Allah the Exalted for the bestowal of true knowledges. [And success is from Allah.]
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage concludes a discussion on the impossibility of knowing Allah's essence. The author summarizes a previous argument: the subject of a proposition must be conceived; if not, contradiction arises; if conceived, the proposition is false. He then answers a third objection: knowledge of the essence means independence, a negative concept, and negatives are not part of quiddities. This also answers a fourth aspect. He states this concludes the chapter. He then raises further questions: is this knowledge attained by angels? This is uncertain. He ends by noting that when theological inquiries reach such depths, minds are bewildered, and one can only rely on Allah for true knowledge.

مفهوم سلبي
الماهيات
المعرفة الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human reason can fully comprehend Allah's essence through philosophical investigation.
Correction: The passage shows that such knowledge is unattainable for humans; true knowledge comes only through divine bestowal after reason reaches its limits.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's stance on whether angels have knowledge of Allah's essence?
A) They have full knowledge.
B) They have partial knowledge.
C) It is a point of hesitation (متوقف فيه).
D) They have no knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Human intellect cannot comprehend Allah's essence; true knowledge comes only through divine bestowal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter the limits of your own understanding about Allah, do you humbly turn to Him in desperate need, or do you cling to intellectual pride? Will you today make a sincere du'a asking Him to bestow true knowledge upon you?
?
Are you content to know Allah through His names and attributes, or do you secretly yearn to penetrate His essence? Is there a part of you that feels unsatisfied with negative knowledge? Confess that to Allah and ask for contentment.
?
The author says minds are bewildered and thoughts halt—have you ever let yourself be bewildered by the majesty of Allah, or do you rush to find easy answers? Commit to sitting with one theological puzzle this week, not to solve it, but to increase your awe and dependence on Allah.

The Necessity of Negating All Qualities from Allah
The author argues against attributing any physical qualities to Allah and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intellect negates all physical qualities from Allah, even those that might appear perfect, and how the author refutes an objection that different quiddities could share such qualities.

Core Idea
Allah must be absolutely free from all physical qualities to avoid rational inconsistency.

Having established that Allah is not a body or substance, the author now addresses the question of attributes like colors, tastes, and smells. Although some might argue that certain qualities could be perfections, the intellect sees them as properties of bodies. This lesson unpacks the author's argument, his response to an objection, and the necessity of negating all such qualities from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the main claim: Allah is free from qualities.
Notice the objection starts with 'إلا أن لقائل أن يقول'.
2
Identify the premise and its implications
The author begins by recalling that Allah is not a body or substance. This already negates quantity and some qualities (shape, form).
This is the foundation for the rest.
3
Understand the objection
An objector says: even if Allah is not a body, He could still have qualities like color because different things can share attributes.
The objection challenges the link between body and quality.
4
Follow the refutation
The author responds by noting that divine attributes (creation, etc.) do not depend on these qualities. Also, we cannot claim some qualities are perfections and others defects to pick and choose.
This is the key argument: either affirm all (leading to contradictions) or negate all.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The only consistent position is to negate all physical qualities from Allah. This is what the author intends.
Thus Allah's transcendence is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني عشر
تنزيه ذات الله تعالى عن الكيفيات
اعلم : أنا لما دللنا على أنه سبحانه ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر كان ذلك يفيد كونه منزها عن الكمية ، ويفيد أيضا كونه منزها عن بعض أقسام الكيفيات ، وهو الشكل والخلقة . وأما سائر الكيفيات مثل الألوان والطعوم والروائح ، فالقول بإثباتها الله تعالى يستبعده العقل ، لأن هذه صفات الأجسام ، فكان إثباتها للذات المنزهة عن الجسمية محالا إلا أن لقائل أن يقول : لا يلزم من ثبوت هذه الصفات للأجسام ، امتناع ثبوتها لما لا يكون جسما ، لأن الماهيات المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في بعض الصفات
·
ومن الناس من قال : إن صفات الإلهية وهي الخلق والتكوين لا تتوقف على حصول هذه الألوان والطعوم . وليس يمكن أن يقال : إن بعض أنواعها من صفات الكمال ، وأضدادها من صفات النقص، حتى يمكن القول بأن ما كان منها من صفات الكمال فهو ثابت الله تعالى وما كان من صفات النقص فهو منفي عن الله تعالى . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم يكن ثبوت بعض تلك الصفات الله ، أولى من ثبوت البواقي ، فإما أن يجب ثبوت كلها الله تعالى ، وحينئذ يلزم الجمع بين الضدين ، أو يجب تنزيه الله عن الكل . وذلك هو المطلوب .
Chapter Twelve: The Transcendence of the Essence of Allah, Exalted, from Qualities. Know: when we demonstrated that He, glorious, is not a body nor a substance, that indicated that He is free from quantity, and also indicated that He is free from some kinds of qualities, namely shape and form. As for other qualities such as colors, tastes, and smells, the intellect rejects affirming them of Allah, Exalted, because these are attributes of bodies, so affirming them for an essence free from corporeality is impossible. However, someone might say: it does not necessarily follow from these qualities being established for bodies that their establishment for what is not a body is impossible, because different quiddities do not preclude sharing some attributes. And some people said: divine attributes, namely creation and bringing into existence, do not depend on possessing these colors and tastes. And it cannot be said that some of their types are attributes of perfection and their opposites are attributes of deficiency, so that one could say that whatever of them is an attribute of perfection is affirmed for Allah, Exalted, and whatever is an attribute of deficiency is negated from Allah, Exalted. And since this is the case, then affirming some of those attributes for Allah is not more appropriate than affirming the rest. So either affirmation of all of them for Allah must be necessary, in which case the combination of opposites would follow, or Allah must be declared free from all. That is what is intended.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage, opening Chapter Twelve, argues for Allah's transcendence from qualities (kayfiyyat). The author begins by stating that since Allah is not a body (jism) or substance (jawhar), He is free from quantity and from some qualities like shape and form. For other qualities like colors, tastes, and smells, the intellect (aql) rejects attributing them to Allah because they are attributes of bodies. An objection is anticipated: different quiddities (mahiyyat) can share attributes, so a non-body could have these qualities. The author responds by appealing to a group who say that divine attributes like creation (khalq) and bringing into existence (takwin) do not depend on these qualities. Furthermore, one cannot claim that some of these qualities are perfections and others defects, because that would allow affirming only the perfections for Allah. But if one affirms some, there is no basis to stop at some rather than others; affirming all would combine opposites (like light and darkness), so the only consistent position is to negate all such qualities from Allah. Thus the intended conclusion is established.

Key Terms
الكيفيات (qualities)
الجسم (body)
الجوهر (substance)
الماهيات (quiddities)
الخلق والتكوين (creation and bringing into existence)
صفات الكمال (attributes of perfection)
صفات النقص (attributes of deficiency)
الجمع بين الضدين (combination of opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that certain physical qualities (e.g., beauty, brightness) are perfections and could be attributed to Allah if they are not inherently defective.
Correction: The author shows that there is no basis to classify some physical qualities as perfections and others as defects; thus, affirming any leads to a contradiction (affirming all or none). Hence, all such qualities must be negated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the intellect reject affirming colors, tastes, and smells for Allah?
A) Because they are attributes of bodies and Allah is not a body.
B) Because they are all defects.
C) Because they are not mentioned in revelation.
D) Because they imply multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
Allah must be absolutely free from all physical qualities because attributing any would inevitably lead to rational inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah in a specific form or shape, perhaps to feel closer to Him? How does this lesson challenge that tendency—and what concrete step can you take today to replace that image with pure tanzih?
?
The author argues that even claiming some physical qualities are perfections cannot justify attributing them to Allah. Does this make God feel more distant or more majestic in your heart? Are you willing to let go of comforting anthropomorphic ideas if they compromise His transcendence?
?
If you truly internalize that Allah is free from all physical qualities, how must your worship change? Think of a specific moment in your daily life—prayer, dua, or reflection—where this belief can transform your intention or focus.

Divine Transcendence

Allah's Transcendence from Corporeal Qualities
Argument for Allah's transcendence from all corporeal qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that Allah is transcendent from all corporeal qualities, including colors, tastes, and smells, and why affirming them would lead to contradiction.

Core Idea
Allah's transcendence from physical qualities is a logical necessity, not mere tradition.

In this passage, the author builds on the earlier proof that Allah is not a body or substance. Since corporeal qualities like shape, color, and taste are attributes of bodies, they cannot apply to Allah. But what about an objection that different essences can share attributes? The author responds with a powerful argument that affirming any such quality would force affirming all or none, and since some qualities are opposites, they cannot all be affirmed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the foundation
Recall that the author has already proven that Allah is neither a body nor a substance. This is the starting point for the argument.
This earlier proof is assumed; the passage builds on it.
2
See the immediate implications
From the denial of body and substance, we deduce that Allah is free from quantity and from certain qualities like shape and form.
Quantity and shape are directly linked to corporeality.
3
Extend to other qualities
The same logic applies to colors, tastes, and smells—they are attributes of bodies, so they cannot be affirmed for Allah. But an objection arises: different essences can share attributes.
The objection challenges the direct inference.
4
Grasp the decisive response
The author refutes the objection by noting that divine attributes (like creation) do not depend on such qualities. Moreover, these qualities cannot be selectively affirmed because they include opposites, so either all or none must be affirmed—affirming all is contradictory, so we must negate all.
This leads to the conclusion that Allah is absolutely transcendent from all corporeal qualities.

·
ومن الناس من قال : إن صفات الإلهية وهي الخلق والتكوين لا تتوقف على حصول هذه الألوان والطعوم . وليس يمكن أن يقال : إن بعض أنواعها من صفات الكمال ، وأضدادها من صفات النقص، حتى يمكن القول بأن ما كان منها من صفات الكمال فهو ثابت الله تعالى وما كان من صفات النقص فهو منفي عن الله تعالى . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم يكن ثبوت بعض تلك الصفات الله ، أولى من ثبوت البواقي ، فإما أن يجب ثبوت كلها الله تعالى ، وحينئذ يلزم الجمع بين الضدين ، أو يجب تنزيه الله عن الكل . وذلك هو المطلوب .
Chapter Twelve: The Transcendence of the Essence of Allah, the Exalted, from Qualities. Know: When we demonstrated that He, glorified is He, is not a body and not a substance, that implied that He is transcendent from quantity, and also implied that He is transcendent from some kinds of qualities, which are shape and form. As for the rest of the qualities such as colors, tastes, and smells, the intellect deems it remote to affirm them for Allah, the Exalted, because these are attributes of bodies. So affirming them for an essence that is transcendent from corporeality is impossible, except that someone may say: The fact that these qualities are established for bodies does not necessitate the impossibility of their being established for what is not a body, because different essences are not prevented from sharing some attributes. And some people said: The divine attributes—which are creation and bringing into existence—do not depend on the occurrence of these colors and tastes. And it cannot be said that some of their types are attributes of perfection and their opposites are attributes of deficiency, such that one could say that what among them is an attribute of perfection is affirmed for Allah, the Exalted, and what among them is an attribute of deficiency is negated from Allah, the Exalted. And if this is the case, then affirming some of those attributes for Allah is not more appropriate than affirming the rest. So either the affirmation of all of them must be necessary for Allah, the Exalted—and then it would necessitate combining opposites—or it is necessary to declare Allah transcendent from all of them. And that is what is intended.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on Allah's transcendence from qualities. It begins by stating the author's earlier proof that Allah is neither a body nor a substance, which implies freedom from quantity and some qualities like shape. The author then argues that other qualities—colors, tastes, smells—are attributes of bodies, and since Allah is not a body, affirming them for Him is impossible. An objection is raised: different essences can share attributes, so these qualities could still apply to a non-corporeal being. The author responds by citing a group of scholars who say that divine attributes like creation do not depend on such qualities. Moreover, these qualities cannot be divided into perfect and deficient attributes in a way that would allow affirming some and negating others. Therefore, either all must be affirmed—which leads to contradiction since some are opposites of others—or all must be negated. The passage concludes that negation is the correct position.

تنزيه (tanzih)
كيفيات (kayfiyyat)
جسم (jism)
جوهر (jawhar)
كمية (kamiyya)
ماهيات (mahiyyat)
التكوين (takwin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since Allah has attributes like knowledge and power, He could also have physical qualities like color or shape.
Correction: The passage clarifies that physical qualities are categorically different because they are tied to corporeality. Affirming any would lead to contradiction, so they must all be negated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for Allah to have qualities like colors and tastes?
A) Because Allah has no essence
B) Because these are attributes of bodies and Allah is not a body
C) Because the intellect cannot comprehend them
D) Because they are mentioned only metaphorically

Key Takeaway
Allah must be declared transcendent from all corporeal qualities because they are attributes of bodies, and affirming any would lead to contradiction.

?

Objection: Light as a Divine Attribute?
First objection: light as a perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that light is a perfection and thus attributable to Allah, and how it is refuted implicitly by the understanding of colors as corporeal qualities.

Core Idea
Light, as a bodily quality, cannot apply to a non-corporeal Allah.

The objector argues that light is a perfection, so Allah should have it, even though Allah is not a body. This objection assumes that light as we know it is a perfection independent of corporeality. The overall argument will show that such physical qualities are intrinsically tied to bodies and cannot be attributed to a non-corporeal being.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage of the first objection once, then read the translation. Notice how the objector uses the principle that Allah must have perfections.
The objector does not directly challenge Allah's transcendence; they argue that light could be a non-corporeal perfection.
2
Identify the underlying assumption
The objector assumes that light, as a quality known from bodies, can be a perfection for a non-corporeal being. Think about whether this assumption is valid.
3
Relate to the concluding refutation
The author does not answer this objection separately here, but the later argument about colors (lesson 3) refutes the possibility of any physical quality in Allah. Understand that the refutation is implicit.
This lesson sets up the need for a deeper understanding of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : هذا الدليل ضعيف من وجوه :
الأول : إن كيفية النور من صفات الكمال ، والمدح . وكيفية الظلمة من صفات النقص . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون ذات الله موصوفة بصفة النور مع كونه منزها عن الجسمية والجهة ؟ لما ثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى موصوفا بصفات الكمال والمدح، ويجب كونه منزها عن صفات النقص
والثاني : هب أنا سلمنا أنه ليس شيء منها صفة كمال ومدح ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون موصوفا ببعضها دون البعض ؟ قوله : « إنه ليس اتصاف ذاته بعضها أولى من اتصافها بالبواقي ، فنقول : قد بينا في علم المنطق أن قول القائل ليس هذا ، أولى من ذلك مقدمة ضعيفة . فإنه إن كان المراد عدم الأولوية في أذهاننا وفي عقولنا ، بمعنى أنا لا نعرف دليلا يدل على أن هذا أولى بالوقوع . فهذا مسلم ، إلا أن هذا لا يفيد إلا الوقف والشك ، وإن كان المراد بعدم الأولوية أنه يمتنع كون أحد الجانبين أولى في نفس الأمر ، فهذا ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز اتصاف ذاته ببعض هذه الأضداد أولى ، وإن كنا لا نهتدي ولا نعرف المعنى الموجب لتلك الأولوية ؟ .
والسؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : يحصل السواد والبياض فيه معاً ، ثم يحصل من اجتماعهما هيئة (۱) مركبة من اجتماعها ؟ ولا بد في إبطال هذا الاحتمال من دليل منفصل . واعلم أنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى منزه عن الجسمية والحصول في الحيز ، فحينئذ يمتنع أن يكون اللون القائم به ، أن يكون اللون القائم به ، لونا ساريا في ذاته منبسطا على سطحه ، فيبقى أن يكون ذلك اللون ماهية ، تخالف (۳) ما شاهدناه في الأجسام ، وحينئذ لا تكون تلك الصفة لونا ، بل صفة أخرى مخالفة لما يعقل من اسم اللون ، وذلك يفيد نفي الألوان على الوجه الذي عقلناه [ والله أعلم (٣) ] .
And someone may say: This proof is weak in several respects: First: The quality of light is among the attributes of perfection and praise. And the quality of darkness is among the attributes of deficiency. And if this is the case, then why is it not permissible for the essence of Allah to be described with the attribute of light, while He is transcendent from corporeality and direction? Because it is established that He, the Exalted, must be described with attributes of perfection and praise, and He must be transcendent from attributes of deficiency. Second: Suppose we concede that none of them is an attribute of perfection and praise, but why is it not permissible for Him to be described with some of them without others? His statement: 'The description of His essence with some of them is not more appropriate than the description with the rest.' So we say: We have already explained in the science of logic that the statement of one who says 'This is not more appropriate than that' is a weak premise. For if what is meant is the absence of priority in our minds and intellects, meaning that we do not know a proof indicating that this is more likely to occur, then this is conceded, but it only yields suspension and doubt. And if what is meant by the absence of priority is that it is impossible for one side to be more appropriate in reality, then this is denied. So why is it not permissible that His essence be described with some of these opposites as more appropriate, even if we are not guided to nor know the cause necessitating that priority? The third question: Why is it not permissible to say that blackness and whiteness occur in Him together, and then from their coming together a composite form arises from their combination? And it is necessary to refute this possibility with a separate proof. Know that when it is established that He, the Exalted, is transcendent from corporeality and existence in a spatial location, then it is impossible for the color subsisting in Him to be a color penetrating His essence and spread over His surface. So it remains that that color is an essence that differs from what we have observed in bodies. Then that attribute is not a color, but rather another attribute that is different from what is understood from the name 'color'. And that serves to negate colors in the sense that we have understood them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the earlier argument that Allah cannot have any qualities like color, taste, etc., and provides responses to each. The first objection argues that light is a perfection and darkness a deficiency, so Allah, who must possess all perfections, can be described with light even if He is not a body. The author does not give an immediate separate answer to this here, but the subsequent responses and the concluding argument about colors implicitly refute it. The second objection concedes that none of these qualities are perfections but asks: why can Allah not have some of them? The author responds by criticizing the logic of 'no priority', explaining that if we simply lack knowledge of priority, that leads only to doubt, not to a definitive conclusion, and if the objector claims impossibility of priority in reality, that is denied. The third objection suggests that black and white could combine in Allah to form a new composite quality. The author answers that since Allah is not a body, a color subsisting in Him cannot be a corporeal color spreading over a surface; if it exists, it would be a different kind of entity, not color as we understand it, so the common understanding of color is negated. The final phrase 'Allah knows best' indicates the author's deference to God's knowledge.

Key Terms
كيفية النور
صفات الكمال
صفات النقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since light is not a physical object, it could be a non-corporeal attribute of Allah.
Correction: Light, as a quality, is known only in connection with bodies; it cannot be abstracted from corporeality. Thus, attributing light to Allah would imply spatiality or corporeality, which is negated.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first objection?
A) Light is a deficiency, so Allah must be free from it
B) Light is a perfection, so Allah could have it despite being non-corporeal
C) Light is not a perfection, but Allah still could have it
D) The objection denies that Allah is transcendent

Key Takeaway
Light as a physical quality is not necessarily a perfection for a non-physical being, and any attribution of physical qualities to Allah requires careful theological reasoning.

?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly perfect quality like light cannot be attributed to Allah if it is physical. What other 'good' qualities do you mistakenly attach to Allah in a physical sense? What will you do to correct that?
