Objection: Why Not Some Qualities?
Second objection and its refutation via logic of priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that Allah could have some qualities without others, and the author's critique of the 'no priority' argument.

Core Idea
Claiming no priority between options does not prove impossibility; it only shows ignorance.

The objector concedes none of these qualities are perfections but asks why Allah cannot have some arbitrarily. The author responds by dissecting the logic of 'no priority', showing that lack of known priority is not the same as impossibility in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and response
Read the Arabic passage from 'والثاني' to 'المعنى الموجب لتلك الأولوية'. Notice that the author's response is embedded within the objection statement after 'فنقول'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'no priority'
The author identifies two possible meanings: 1) we don't know of any priority, 2) priority is impossible in reality. Understand the implications of each.
The first meaning leads only to doubt; the second meaning is denied without proof.
3
Assess the objection's strength
Reflect on why the author considers the 'no priority' argument weak. How does this refutation uphold the original proof?
The objection fails because it does not prove that priority is impossible, only that we lack evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : هذا الدليل ضعيف من وجوه :
الأول : إن كيفية النور من صفات الكمال ، والمدح . وكيفية الظلمة من صفات النقص . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن تكون ذات الله موصوفة بصفة النور مع كونه منزها عن الجسمية والجهة ؟ لما ثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى موصوفا بصفات الكمال والمدح، ويجب كونه منزها عن صفات النقص
والثاني : هب أنا سلمنا أنه ليس شيء منها صفة كمال ومدح ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون موصوفا ببعضها دون البعض ؟ قوله : « إنه ليس اتصاف ذاته بعضها أولى من اتصافها بالبواقي ، فنقول : قد بينا في علم المنطق أن قول القائل ليس هذا ، أولى من ذلك مقدمة ضعيفة . فإنه إن كان المراد عدم الأولوية في أذهاننا وفي عقولنا ، بمعنى أنا لا نعرف دليلا يدل على أن هذا أولى بالوقوع . فهذا مسلم ، إلا أن هذا لا يفيد إلا الوقف والشك ، وإن كان المراد بعدم الأولوية أنه يمتنع كون أحد الجانبين أولى في نفس الأمر ، فهذا ممنوع . فلم لا يجوز اتصاف ذاته ببعض هذه الأضداد أولى ، وإن كنا لا نهتدي ولا نعرف المعنى الموجب لتلك الأولوية ؟ .
والسؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : يحصل السواد والبياض فيه معاً ، ثم يحصل من اجتماعهما هيئة (۱) مركبة من اجتماعها ؟ ولا بد في إبطال هذا الاحتمال من دليل منفصل . واعلم أنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى منزه عن الجسمية والحصول في الحيز ، فحينئذ يمتنع أن يكون اللون القائم به ، أن يكون اللون القائم به ، لونا ساريا في ذاته منبسطا على سطحه ، فيبقى أن يكون ذلك اللون ماهية ، تخالف (۳) ما شاهدناه في الأجسام ، وحينئذ لا تكون تلك الصفة لونا ، بل صفة أخرى مخالفة لما يعقل من اسم اللون ، وذلك يفيد نفي الألوان على الوجه الذي عقلناه [ والله أعلم (٣) ] .
And someone may say: This proof is weak in several respects: First: The quality of light is among the attributes of perfection and praise. And the quality of darkness is among the attributes of deficiency. And if this is the case, then why is it not permissible for the essence of Allah to be described with the attribute of light, while He is transcendent from corporeality and direction? Because it is established that He, the Exalted, must be described with attributes of perfection and praise, and He must be transcendent from attributes of deficiency. Second: Suppose we concede that none of them is an attribute of perfection and praise, but why is it not permissible for Him to be described with some of them without others? His statement: 'The description of His essence with some of them is not more appropriate than the description with the rest.' So we say: We have already explained in the science of logic that the statement of one who says 'This is not more appropriate than that' is a weak premise. For if what is meant is the absence of priority in our minds and intellects, meaning that we do not know a proof indicating that this is more likely to occur, then this is conceded, but it only yields suspension and doubt. And if what is meant by the absence of priority is that it is impossible for one side to be more appropriate in reality, then this is denied. So why is it not permissible that His essence be described with some of these opposites as more appropriate, even if we are not guided to nor know the cause necessitating that priority? The third question: Why is it not permissible to say that blackness and whiteness occur in Him together, and then from their coming together a composite form arises from their combination? And it is necessary to refute this possibility with a separate proof. Know that when it is established that He, the Exalted, is transcendent from corporeality and existence in a spatial location, then it is impossible for the color subsisting in Him to be a color penetrating His essence and spread over His surface. So it remains that that color is an essence that differs from what we have observed in bodies. Then that attribute is not a color, but rather another attribute that is different from what is understood from the name 'color'. And that serves to negate colors in the sense that we have understood them. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the earlier argument that Allah cannot have any qualities like color, taste, etc., and provides responses to each. The first objection argues that light is a perfection and darkness a deficiency, so Allah, who must possess all perfections, can be described with light even if He is not a body. The author does not give an immediate separate answer to this here, but the subsequent responses and the concluding argument about colors implicitly refute it. The second objection concedes that none of these qualities are perfections but asks: why can Allah not have some of them? The author responds by criticizing the logic of 'no priority', explaining that if we simply lack knowledge of priority, that leads only to doubt, not to a definitive conclusion, and if the objector claims impossibility of priority in reality, that is denied. The third objection suggests that black and white could combine in Allah to form a new composite quality. The author answers that since Allah is not a body, a color subsisting in Him cannot be a corporeal color spreading over a surface; if it exists, it would be a different kind of entity, not color as we understand it, so the common understanding of color is negated. The final phrase 'Allah knows best' indicates the author's deference to God's knowledge.

Key Terms
الأولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the 'no priority' argument in the second objection?
A) He concedes the argument and accepts the objection
B) He shows that the premise is logically weak and can mean either ignorance or impossibility
C) He provides scriptural evidence for priority
D) He redefines the qualities as perfections

Key Takeaway
Lack of known priority does not negate the possibility of actual priority; the objection fails to prove its case.

?
The author distinguishes between 'I don't know a reason' and 'there is no reason.' In your own life, how often do you confuse your lack of knowledge with a proof of impossibility? Will you commit to saying 'I don't know' instead of assuming something cannot be true?
?
This passage shows that doubt arises from not knowing a proof. Do you rest in doubt about matters of faith, or do you actively pursue certainty? What specific step will you take this week to replace one area of doubt with sound knowledge from revelation and reason?

Objection: Composite Colors in Allah?
Third objection and final refutation of colors in Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that black and white could combine in Allah, and the refutation that such a color would not be color as we know it.

Core Idea
Color cannot exist in a non-corporeal being as it does in bodies.

The objector speculates that blackness and whiteness could combine in Allah to form a composite attribute. The author responds by reiterating Allah's transcendence from corporeality, which makes any supposed color fundamentally different from what we understand, thus negating the applicability of color concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic from 'والسؤال الثالث' to 'دليل منفصل'. Understand how the objector envisions black and white merging.
2
Read the author's response
Read from 'واعلم أنه لما ثبت' to the end. Notice the author's appeal to Allah's transcendence from body and space.
3
Understand the consequence
If Allah is non-corporeal, any color attributed would not spread over a surface; thus it would be a different kind of entity, not the color we know. So the objection fails because it assumes a physical understanding of color.

Refutation of the Color Combination Objection
Refutes the objection that black and white could coexist in God, producing a composite color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that black and white could combine in God fails, based on God's transcendence over corporeality.

Core Idea
Color, as we understand it, is a corporeal attribute; God, being incorporeal, cannot have color in that sense.

The third objection raised against God's transcendence is that black and white could coexist in Him, producing a composite color. This lesson follows the refutation: since God is not a body, any color attributed to Him must be fundamentally different from physical colors, nullifying the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the need for proof
Read the passage carefully. Notice the objection: why cannot black and white combine in God? Then see that the author states an independent proof is needed.
The objection is posed as a question, and the author immediately calls for a proof.
2
Identify the key premise
The author asserts that it is established that God is transcendent over corporeality and location. This is the foundation of the refutation.
This premise is not argued here; it is taken as given from earlier discussions.
3
Follow the deduction
From the premise, deduce that any color in God cannot be a bodily color that permeates or spreads over a surface. Therefore, it must be an entirely different essence.
Contrast this with colors in bodies, which are spread over surfaces.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the proposed attribute is not a color in the usual sense, the objection fails. The author concludes that colors in the common understanding are negated.
This refutes the objection without denying that God could have a unique attribute that is not color.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : يحصل السواد والبياض فيه معاً ، ثم يحصل من اجتماعهما هيئة مركبة من اجتماعها ؟ ولا بد في إبطال هذا الاحتمال من دليل منفصل . واعلم أنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى منزه عن الجسمية والحصول في الحيز ، فحينئذ يمتنع أن يكون اللون القائم به لونا ساريا في ذاته منبسطا على سطحه ، فيبقى أن يكون ذلك اللون ماهية تخالف ما شاهدناه في الأجسام ، وحينئذ لا تكون تلك الصفة لونا ، بل صفة أخرى مخالفة لما يعقل من اسم اللون ، وذلك يفيد نفي الألوان على الوجه الذي عقلناه
And the third question: Why is it not permissible to say: blackness and whiteness occur in Him together, then from their combination a composite form results from their conjunction? And there must be an independent proof to invalidate this possibility. And know that when it is established that He is transcendent over corporeality and location in space, then it is impossible that the color subsisting in Him be a color permeating His essence and spread over His surface. So it remains that that color be an essence (māhiyya) different from what we have observed in bodies. And then that attribute would not be a color, but another attribute contrary to what is understood from the name 'color'. And that results in the negation of colors in the sense we have understood.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes a third objection: Why cannot black and white coexist in God, forming a composite color? The author states that an independent proof is needed. He argues that since God is transcendent over corporeality and occupying space, any color attributed to Him cannot be a color that permeates His essence or spreads over a surface (as in bodies). Therefore, such an attribute would be an essence (ماهية) unlike the colors we know in bodies. Consequently, it would not be called 'color' in the usual sense. Thus the objection fails because the proposed attribute is not a color as commonly understood.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author argues that any color subsisting in an incorporeal being would be fundamentally different from the color we know, so it cannot be called color in the usual sense.

D) Because the objection is based on a logical fallacy.

Key Takeaway
The objection that black and white could combine in God is invalid because God's incorporeality means any attribute resembling color would be an entirely different kind of quality, not color as we understand it.

Divine Transcendence

The Impossibility of Divine Indwelling: Two Rational Proofs
Proves impossibility of divine indwelling via necessity/contingency division and two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments that demonstrate why God cannot indwell in another being, based on the concepts of necessity and contingency.

Core Idea
Divine indwelling would make God dependent, contradicting His necessary existence.

In this passage, the author presents the classical kalām argument against the belief that God could reside within creation. He first outlines the majority's approach: if God indwells, it must be either necessarily or contingently—both impossible. He then elaborates two specific proofs against necessary indwelling, showing that it leads to absurdities like multiple necessary beings. This lesson will walk through these proofs step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of حلول
The author refers to a prior definition. Indwelling means God residing in another being. This is rejected by mainstream Islamic theology.
This lesson builds on that definition.
2
Examine the majority's division
The majority argues: if indwelling occurs, it must be either necessary or contingent. Both are false. Identify why each is problematic.
The passage focuses on the first case (necessary indwelling) with two reasons.
3
Analyze the first reason against necessary indwelling
If God indwells necessarily, He depends on the locus. Dependence implies possibility, contradicting God's necessary existence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the second reason against necessary indwelling
If God indwells necessarily from eternity, the locus becomes eternal and necessary, leading to multiple necessary existents.
This violates tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث عشر في إثبات أنه يمتنع كونه تعالى حالك في غيره نقول : أما تفسير الحلول فقد سبق على الاستقصاء ونقول : أما الجمهور فقد عولوا في نفي الحلول على أن قالوا : لو حل لحل إما مع وجوب أن يحل أو مع جواز أن يحل والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بالحلول ، وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يحل مع وجوب أن يحل ، وذلك لوجهين : الأول : إنه لو حلّ مع وجوب أنه يحل ، لكان مفتقرا في ذاته إلى ذلك المحل ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته . هذا خلف . والثاني : إنه تعالى لما حلّ في المحل مع وجوب أن يحل فيه ، لزم من قدم الله قدم ذلك المحل ومن افتقار ذات الله إليه كونه واجبا لذاته . وإذا كان مفتقراً إلى ذلك المحل ، فمن المعلوم أن الذي يفتقر الواجب لذاته إليه يكون أولى بالوجوب الذاتي ، وحينئذ يصير واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد . وذلك محال
The thirteenth chapter on establishing that it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be an indweller in another. We say: As for the interpretation of indwelling (ḥulūl), it has preceded in detail. And we say: The majority (al-jumhūr) relied in negating indwelling on the following: they said: If He indwells, He would indwell either necessarily or contingently, and both divisions are false, so the claim of indwelling is false. And we say: It is not permissible that He indwell necessarily, and that is for two reasons: First: If He indwells necessarily, He would be dependent in His essence on that locus, and the dependent on another is possible in itself, so it would follow that the Necessary by essence (wājib lidhātih) would be [possible] – this is a contradiction. Second: Since He indwells the locus necessarily, from the eternity of God would follow the eternity of that locus, and from the dependence of God's essence on it would follow its being necessary by essence. And if He is dependent on that locus, it is known that that upon which the Necessary by essence depends is more deserving of essential necessity, and then the Necessary Existent would become more than one. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a proof (إثبات) that it is impossible for God to indwell (حلول) in another being. The author first references a prior detailed definition of حلول. He then presents the standard argument of the majority (الجمهور): If God indwells, He must do so either necessarily (مع وجوب أن يحل) or contingently (مع جواز أن يحل). Both are false: necessary indwelling would make God dependent on the locus, implying contingency (contradiction); contingent indwelling would make God's indwelling an accident, which is also impossible. The author elaborates two specific proofs against necessary indwelling: (1) Necessary indwelling implies that God's essence depends on the locus, but dependence on another is a mark of possibility, so God would not be necessary by essence – a contradiction. (2) If God indwells necessarily from eternity, then the locus would also be eternal, and since God's essence depends on it, the locus would become necessary by essence, leading to multiple necessary existents, which is impossible.

Key Terms
الحلول
وجوب الحلول
جواز الحلول
المفتقر إلى الغير
واجب الوجود
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine indwelling could be consistent with God's transcendence, but the proofs show it necessarily implies dependence and multiplicity.
Correction: The arguments demonstrate that indwelling, whether necessary or contingent, leads to contradictions with God's necessary existence and oneness.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given against necessary indwelling (الحلول مع الوجوب)?
A) It would make the locus eternal and necessary, implying multiple necessary existents.
B) It would make God contingent.
C) It would negate divine attributes.
D) It would imply that God is composite.

Key Takeaway
God cannot indwell in another being because it would either make Him dependent (contradicting His necessity) or imply multiple necessary beings (contradicting His oneness).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The second proof shows that divine indwelling would create another necessary being besides God. Is there any person, object, or idea in your life that you treat as essential alongside God—something you feel you cannot live without? How can you realign your heart to depend solely on the Self-Sufficient One?
?
If God’s independence means He does not need your worship, then why do you pray? Examine your motive: is it to fulfill a need in Him, or to acknowledge your own need? How will you change your next prayer to reflect the truth that God is free of all need?

Two Proofs Against Necessary Divine Incarnation
Author enumerates two reasons against necessary incarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine incarnation cannot be necessary, based on two logical proofs that safeguard God's transcendence.

Core Idea
If God were necessarily incarnate, He would depend on something else — impossible.

In this passage, the author presents the main argument against the possibility of divine incarnation (hulul). He first divides incarnation into necessary or contingent, then shows why necessary incarnation leads to absurdities. The two reasons are elegant logical refutations that protect God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of hulul
Read the opening lines where the author states that if God were to incarnate, it would be either necessarily or contingently. Both are false, but the focus is on necessary incarnation.
The word 'hulul' means incarnation or indwelling.
2
Understand the first proof: dependence implies contingency
Follow the first reason: necessary incarnation would make God dependent on the locus, but dependence implies contingency, contradicting His necessary essence.
This proof uses the principle that the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything.
3
Understand the second proof: multiplicity of necessary beings
Examine the second reason: if God necessarily incarnates, the locus must be pre-eternal and necessary, leading to more than one necessary being, which is impossible.
This argument relies on the uniqueness of the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث عشر في إثبات أنه يمتنع كونه تعالى حالك في غيره نقول : أما تفسير الحلول فقد سبق على الاستقصاء ونقول : أما الجمهور فقد عولوا في نفي الحلول على أن قالوا : لو حل لحل إما مع وجوب أن يحل أو مع جواز أن يحل والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بالحلول ، وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يحل مع وجوب أن يحل ، وذلك لوجهين : الأول : إنه لو حلّ مع وجوب أنه يحل ، لكان مفتقرا في ذاته إلى ذلك المحل ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته . هذا خلف والثاني : إنه تعالى لما حلّ في المحل مع وجوب أن يحل فيه ، لزم من قدم الله قدم ذلك المحل ومن افتقار ذات الله إليه كونه واجب لذاته لأن ذاته تعالى واجبة لذاته . وإذا كان مفتقراً إلى ذلك المحل ، فمن المعلوم أن الذي يفتقر الواجب لذاته إليه يكون أولى بالوجوب الذاتي ، وحينئذ يصير واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد . وذلك محال .
Chapter Thirteen: On Establishing that it is Impossible for Him, Exalted, to be Incarnate (hulul) in Another. We say: The explanation of incarnation (hulul) has already preceded in detail. And we say: The majority (al-jumhur) relied in negating incarnation on their saying: If He were to incarnate, He would incarnate either with the necessity of incarnation or with the possibility of incarnation, and both divisions are false, so the assertion of incarnation is invalid. And we say: It is not permissible that He incarnate with the necessity of incarnation, and that is for two reasons: First: If He were to incarnate with the necessity of incarnation, He would be in His essence dependent on that locus (al-mahal), and that which is dependent on another is possible in essence, so it would entail that the Necessary in essence (wajib al-dhat) be possible, and that is a contradiction (khalf). Second: Since He, Exalted, were to incarnate in the locus with the necessity of incarnation, it would follow from the pre-eternity (qidam) of God the pre-eternity of that locus, and from the dependence of God’s essence on it, its being necessary in essence, because His essence, Exalted, is necessary in essence. And if He were dependent on that locus, then it is known that that which the Necessary in essence depends on is more worthy of essential necessity, and thus the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) would become more than one. And that is impossible (muhal).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the necessity of divine incarnation. It begins by recalling that the definition of hulul has already been given. The author then presents the argument of the majority (al-jumhur): if God were to incarnate, it would be either necessarily or contingently. Both are false, so incarnation is impossible. The focus here is on the first case: necessary incarnation. Two reasons are given. The first reason: if incarnation were necessary, God would be dependent on the locus. But the Necessary Being cannot be dependent; dependence implies contingency (imkan), so it would contradict His necessary existence. The second reason: if God necessarily incarnates, then since God is pre-eternal, the locus must also be pre-eternal (otherwise God would be temporally prior). Moreover, God’s dependence on that locus would make the locus necessary in essence, because that which the Necessary Being depends on must itself be necessary. This would entail multiple necessary beings, which is impossible. Both reasons lead to contradiction, so necessary incarnation is impossible.

Key Terms
حلول
واجب الوجود
ممكن لذاته
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary incarnation would not imply dependence because God is independent in essence.
Correction: The proof shows that necessary incarnation logically entails dependence on the locus, which contradicts God's self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, why is necessary incarnation impossible?
A) Because it would make God dependent, implying contingency.
B) Because it would create multiple necessary beings.
C) Because the locus would be pre-eternal.
D) Because incarnation is a physical concept.

Key Takeaway
Necessary incarnation is impossible because it would imply either contingency or multiplicity of necessary beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You confess that God depends on nothing. But is there any created thing—a person, a plan, a possession—that you act as if your spiritual safety depends on? How does this lesson’s proof of God’s utter self-sufficiency rebuke that hidden idolatry?
?
The proof shows that if God depended on something, He would be contingent. Do you ever treat your worship or obedience as if God needs it, as if He is somehow incomplete without it? What would it mean to serve a God who needs nothing from you?
?
If God cannot be incarnate, then you cannot 'host' Him in your body through mere rituals. Are you content to be a dependent creature, always receiving from a self-sufficient Creator, or do you secretly wish to make God dependent on your efforts? What would change if you truly lived as the needy one today?

The Impossibility of Contingent Divine Incarnation
Sanusi disproves contingent incarnation through a reduction to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingent incarnation (the idea that God could sometimes incarnate and sometimes not) is rationally impossible because it would imply a change from absolute independence to dependence.

Core Idea
Contingent incarnation would make the independent God become dependent—an impossibility.

Sanusi presents a crisp logical argument against the notion that Allah could incarnate in a contingent manner. He shows that the very concept of incarnation entails need, and since Allah is absolutely independent, any incarnation—whether necessary or contingent—is impossible. This passage is a classic example of how kalam uses essential attributes to refute theological claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Let the rhythm of the argument sink in before analyzing it. Notice the structure: conditional statement, explanation, conclusion.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'hulul' (incarnation), 'muhtaj' (dependent), and 'ghani' (independent). Understanding these is crucial.
3
Follow the logical steps
The argument proceeds: incarnation requires dependence; God is independent; contingent incarnation would turn independence into dependence; that is impossible. Trace each step.
4
Consider the two options
Sanusi concludes that if incarnation were possible, it would be either necessary or contingent. Both are false. Reflect on why both fail.
5
Connect to divine transcendence
This argument protects the core belief that Allah is absolutely free of need and does not inhere in creation. How does this strengthen tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يحل في محل مع جواز أن يحل فيه . وذلك لأن الحال في الشيء يجب أن يكون محتاجا إليه ، والذي لا يحل في شيء هو الذي يكون غنيا عنه. فإذا قلنا في الشيء : إنه قد يحل في المحل مع جواز أن لا يحل فيه ، صار المعنى : أن الغني عن الشيء يجوز أن يصير محتاجا إليه وذلك محال . لأن الغني عن الشيء يمتنع أن ينقلب محتاجا إليه ، فيثبت : أنه تعالى لو حل في شيء لحل إما مع وجوب أن يحل أو مع جواز أن يحل ، والقسمان فاسدان ، فالقول بالحلول باطل .
And we said: It is impossible for Him to incarnate in a locus (mahal) with the possibility of incarnation in it. That is because the one who is incarnate in something must be in need of it, and the one who does not incarnate in something is the one who is independent (ghani) of it. So if we say concerning something: it may incarnate in the locus with the possibility of not incarnating in it, the meaning becomes: that which is independent of something may become in need of it, and that is impossible. Because that which is independent of something cannot turn into being in need of it. Thus it is established: if He, Exalted, were to incarnate in something, it would be either with the necessity of incarnation or with the possibility of incarnation, and both divisions are false, so the assertion of incarnation is invalid.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage proves the impossibility of contingent incarnation. The author argues that incarnation necessarily entails need (ihtiyaj) of the incarnate being for the locus. That which does not incarnate is independent (ghani) of the locus. Therefore, if incarnation is contingent (i.e., sometimes occurs and sometimes not), then the being that is independent would become dependent in the state of incarnation. But a change from independence to dependence is impossible for a being that is essentially independent. This argument covers both cases: necessary incarnation (already disproved) and contingent incarnation (now disproved). Since both are impossible, the claim of incarnation (hulul) is false.

Key Terms
حلول
محتاج
غني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that incarnation could be a temporary act of God without affecting His independence, like a king visiting a village without losing kingship.
Correction: The argument shows that incarnation inherently involves need of the locus (as a necessary condition), so it implies dependence. Even temporary dependence contradicts absolute independence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's argument, what does contingent incarnation imply about God?
A) That He would be limited to a place.
B) That He would become dependent on a locus.
C) That He would change His essence.
D) That He would be composite.

Key Takeaway
Contingent incarnation is impossible because it would require the absolutely independent Creator to become dependent on a created locus, which is a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah is absolutely independent (ghani). But when you sin, do you live as if Allah needs your repentance to fix His creation? Do you truly accept that your repentance benefits only you, not Him? How will you remind yourself of His ghina next time you feel your failure affects Him?
?
This passage says the Independent cannot become dependent. Have you ever tried to 'use' God for your own goals, as if He exists to serve your needs? Today, commit to one act of worship purely for His sake—asking nothing in return—and see if your heart feels the difference.
?
If Allah cannot incarnate, then He is utterly unlike you. Yet you often imagine He understands you because He is 'like' you. Will you let this truth strip away your comfortable, human-like image of God? What will you replace it with? How will you relate to a God who is altogether other?

Objection: Incarnation Does Not Imply Dependence
Objection using analogy of body in space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that incarnation does not necessarily imply dependence on the locus, using the analogy of a body in space.

Core Idea
The efficient cause does not depend on its effects.

The author has just argued that contingent incarnation would imply dependence and is therefore impossible for Allah. But an objector raises a powerful challenge: maybe incarnation does not require need? Using the example of a human and a body in space, the objector argues that the efficient cause can necessitate its own effects without being dependent on them. This lesson unpacks that objection in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage from 'فإن قيل' up to 'لم لا يعقل مثله في الحلول في المحل؟'. Then read the translation. Note how the objector frames the argument.
The objector uses the example of a human incarnating in something.
2
Identify the core reasoning
The objector says that the essence of the human could necessitate both the locus and the incarnation itself, conditioned on the locus's existence. This would not imply dependence because the cause does not depend on its effects.
Key terms: لازم (concomitant), أثر (effect), مؤثر (efficient cause).
3
Understand the spatial analogy
The objector draws an analogy: a body exists in a specific space after not being there, but it is not essentially dependent on that space. Similarly, incarnation in a locus need not imply need.
This analogy challenges the author's premise that being in a place requires dependence.
4
Reflect on the objection's strength
Consider why this objection is difficult to answer. It seems to show that the earlier argument against hulul is not airtight.
The author will acknowledge this difficulty in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الإنسان إنه لو حل في شيء مع أن يحل فيه ، لكان محتاجا إلى المحل ، وذلك لأنه لا يمتنع أن توجب ذاته ذات ذلك المحل . ثم إن ذلك المحل يوجب حلوله في نفسه ، أو يقال : ذاته توجب ذات ذلك المحل ، وتوجب حلول نفسه في ذلك المحل ، بشرط وجود ذلك المحل . وبهذا التقدير فإنه لا يلزم افتقار ذاته إلى ذلك المحل . لأن لوازم الشيء وآثاره واجبة الحصول عند حصول ذلك المؤثر ، مع أنه لا يلزم افتقار المؤثر إلى الأثر . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يحل في الشيء مع جواز حلوله فيه ؟ قوله : « لأن الحال في المحل مفتقر إلى ذلك المحل ، والذي لا يكون حالا فيه يكون غنيا عنه ، وكون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد غنيا عنه ومحتاجا إليه محال »، فنقول : لم قلتم : إن الحال في الشيء يكون محتاجا إليه ؟ ألا ترى أن الجسم المعين يحصل في الحيز المعين بعد أن كان غير حال فيه ، مع أنه لا يكون محتاجا لذاته إلى الحلول في ذلك الحيز المعين ؟ فإذا عقل هذا في الحصول في الحيز ، فلم لا يعقل مثله في الحلول في المحل ؟ واعلم أن هذه السؤالات واردة على هذه الطريقة ويصعب الجواب عنها . فالأولى : أن نقول : البحث عن أنه هل يجوز أن يصير حالا في شيء أم لا مسبوق بالبحث عن ماهية الحلول ؟ فنقول : المعقول من الحلول
For it might be said: As for a human, if he were to incarnate in something while having the possibility of incarnation in it, he would be in need of the locus, and that is because it is not impossible that his essence necessitates the essence of that locus. Then that locus necessitates his incarnation in itself, or it might be said: his essence necessitates the essence of that locus and necessitates his own incarnation in that locus, conditioned by the existence of that locus. According to this estimation, dependence of his essence on that locus does not necessarily follow, because the concomitants (lawazim) of a thing and its effects are necessarily obtained when that efficient cause is obtained, while there is no necessary dependence of the efficient cause on the effect. Then we say: Why is it not permissible for Him to incarnate in something with the possibility of His incarnation in it? Your statement: 'Because the one incarnate in the locus is dependent on that locus, and the one who is not incarnate in it is independent of it, and it is impossible for one and the same thing relative to one and the same thing to be both independent of it and in need of it' – so we say: Why do you claim that the one incarnate in something is in need of it? Do you not see that a particular body comes to be in a particular space after it was not incarnate in it, while it is not in its essence in need of incarnation in that particular space? If this is understood concerning coming to be in a space, why should the like not be understood concerning incarnation in a locus? Know that these questions are raised against this method and it is difficult to answer them. The best course is to say: Inquiry into whether it is possible for something to become incarnate in something or not is preceded by inquiry into the quiddity (mahiyya) of incarnation. So we say: What is understood of incarnation...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a objection to the previous argument against contingent incarnation. The objector uses the example of a human: if a human were to incarnate in something (which is possible), it does not necessarily imply dependence. He argues that the essence of the human could necessitate the essence of the locus and the incarnation itself, conditioned by the existence of the locus, without the human being dependent on the locus. This is because the efficient cause does not depend on its effects; rather, the effects depend on the cause. The objector then challenges the author's premise that incarnation implies need, using the analogy of a body occupying a space: a body can exist in a particular space without being essentially dependent on that space, as it can also be elsewhere. Therefore, the same could apply to incarnation in a locus. The author acknowledges that these objections are difficult to answer within this method. Hence, he proposes a more fundamental approach: first define what hulul (incarnation) means before discussing its possibility. The passage ends mid-sentence at 'what is understood of incarnation...', implying a new argument will follow based on the definition.

Key Terms
حلول
لازم
أثر
مؤثر
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that incarnation always implies dependence because the entity needs a place to exist.
Correction: The objector shows that a cause can necessitate its own effects without being dependent on them, similar to how a body can occupy a space without being essentially tied to it.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the objector use to challenge the necessity of dependence in incarnation?
A) A body in a space.
B) A soul in a body.
C) A cause and its effect.
D) A whole and its part.

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that incarnation does not imply dependence because a cause can necessitate its effects without being dependent on them.

Write down one attachment or habit you will actively release today because you know God is independent of it.
?
The objector uses an analogy of a body in space to show that being in a locus does not imply need. In what situation do you feel trapped by your circumstances, thinking you cannot be independent? How does the reality of God's absolute independence free you from that mindset? What is one step you will take today to break free?
?
This lesson argues that even hypothetical incarnation would not make God dependent. How does understanding God's complete self-sufficiency (ghina) transform your view of your own dependence on Him? Are there any 'idols' in your heart that you have made necessary for your well-being? Commit to one act of worship today that reminds you of your entire reliance on Him alone.

Difficulty and Methodological Shift: Define Hulul First
Author acknowledges difficulty and proposes defining hulul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author acknowledges the difficulty of answering the objection and why he proposes defining 'hulul' before discussing its possibility.

Core Idea
Clear definitions are prerequisite for sound reasoning.

After presenting the objection, the author admits that it is difficult to answer within the current method. Instead of offering a direct rebuttal, he proposes a more fundamental approach: first clarify what 'hulul' (incarnation) actually means. This lesson focuses on that methodological shift and its significance in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage of acknowledgment
Read from 'واعلم أن هذه السؤالات' to the end of the chunk. Note the author's frank admission of difficulty.
The passage is short but pivotal; it marks a turning point in the argument.
2
Understand the methodological proposal
The author says that the inquiry into whether incarnation is possible must be preceded by an inquiry into the quiddity (mahiyya) of incarnation. This is a call for conceptual clarity.
Quiddity means the essence or 'whatness' of a thing.
3
Reflect on why definitions matter
Without a precise definition, arguments about possibility become ambiguous. By defining hulul, the author hopes to avoid the confusion that allowed the objection.
This is a common move in kalam: define terms before debating.
4
Consider the cut-off
The passage ends with 'what is understood of incarnation...'. This suggests that the definition will be provided next, but the lesson stops here.
This is a natural break; the definition will be covered in a subsequent lesson.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt or objection to your faith, do you automatically try to defend your position, or do you first ask: 'Do I even understand what I'm defending?' The author models intellectual honesty by admitting difficulty and redefining terms. Will you pause and define your terms before rushing to answer? What is one theological term you use often but have never clearly defined?
?
Think of a theological term you frequently use (e.g., 'tawheed', 'shirk', 'iman'). Could you clearly define its quiddity? If not, what does that say about the strength of your arguments? The author asks us to define before debating. What is one term you can commit to defining clearly this week?
?
The author admits difficulty and shifts to a more rigorous approach. Is there an area in your life—not just theology—where you are avoiding a hard question by not defining your terms? What one step will you take today to clarify a key concept?

The Initial Argument Against Inherence and Its Objection
The author presents and critiques an initial argument against inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial rational argument against God inhering in a locus and the objection that challenges it.

Core Idea
If God could inhere, He would be dependent—yet objections show the argument needs refinement.

The author begins by presenting a straightforward argument: if God could inhere in a created thing, He would either do so necessarily (making Him dependent) or possibly (changing from independent to dependent). Both options are impossible. However, an objector raises a powerful counterexample: a man can be in a place without being dependent on that place. This objection exposes a weakness in the initial argument, forcing the author to reconsider his approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage has two main parts: the initial argument and the objection.
2
Identify the two options in the initial argument
The author presents a dichotomy: if God inheres, it must be either necessary or possible. He then shows both lead to impossibility because of dependence.
Notice the terms 'وجوب' (necessity) and 'جواز' (possibility).
3
Analyze the objection
The objector uses the example of a body occurring in a space. The body does not become dependent on the space in its essence, challenging the premise that inherence implies need.
The objection questions the core link between inherence and dependence.
4
Reflect on the author's response
The author admits the objections are valid and difficult to answer, indicating that the argument is not conclusive.
This shows the author's intellectual honesty and sets up a better argument later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يحل في محل ، مع جواز أن يحل فيه . وذلك لأن الحال في الشيء ، يجب أن يكون محتاجا إليه ، والذي لا يحل في شيء ، هو الذي يكون غنيا عنه. فإذا قلنا في الشيء : إنه قد يحل في المحل مع جواز أن لا يحل فيه ، صار المعنى : أن الغني عن الشيء ، يجوز أن يصير محتاجا إليه وذلك محال . وجوب · لأن الغني عن الشيء يمتنع أن ينقلب محتاجا إليه ، فيثبت : أنه تعالى لو حل في شيء لحل إما مع وجوب أن يحل ، أو مع جواز أن يحل ، والقسمان فاسدان ، فالقول بالحلول باطل . فإن قيل : الإنسان إنه لو حل في شيء مع أن يحل فيه ، لكان محتاجا إلى المحل ، وذلك لأنه [ لا(۱) ] يمتنع أن توجب ذاته ، ذات ذلك المحل . ثم إن ذلك المحل يوجب حلوله في نفسه ، أو يقال : ذاته توجب ذات ذلك المحل ، وتوجب حلول نفسه في ذلك المحل ، بشرط وجود ذلك المحل ، وتوجب حلول نفسه في ذلك المحل ، بشرط وجود ذلك المحل . وبهذا التقدير فإنه لا يلزم افتقار [ ذاته إلى ذلك المحل . لأن لوازم الشيء وآثاره واجبة الحصول عند حصول ذلك المؤثر ، مع أنه لا يلزم افتقار المؤثر إلى (۲) ] الأثر . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يحل في الشيء مع جواز حلوله فيه ؟ قوله : « لأن الحال في المحل ، مفتقر إلى ذلك المحل ، والذي لا يكون حالا فيه ، يكون غنيا عنه ، وكون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد غنيا عنه ، ومحتاجا إليه : محال ، فنقول : لم قلتم : إن الحال في الشيء يكون محتاجا إليه ؟ ألا ترى أن الجسم المعين، يحصل في الحيز المعين ، بعد أن كان غير حال فيه ، مع أنه لا يكون محتاجا لذاته إلى الحلول في ذلك الحيز المعين ؟ فإذا عقل هذا في الحصول في الحيز ، فلم لا يعقل مثله في الحلول في المحل ؟ واعلم أن هذه السؤالات واردة على هذه الطريقة ويصعب الجواب عنها .
And we said: He is prevented from inhering in a locus, while it is possible that He inheres in it. That is because the inheren in a thing must be in need of it, and that which does not inhere in anything is that which is independent of it. So when we say of a thing: it may inhere in the locus while it is possible that it does not inhere in it, the meaning becomes: that the independent of a thing may become in need of it, and that is impossible. Because the independent of a thing cannot change into being in need of it. So it is established: that if the Exalted were to inhere in something, He would inhere either with necessity of inhering, or with possibility of inhering, and both divisions are corrupt. So the statement of inherence is false. If it is said: Man, if he inhers in something while it is possible for him to inhere in it, he would be in need of the locus, and that is because his essence necessitates the essence of that locus. Then that locus necessitates his inhering in itself, or it is said: his essence necessitates the essence of that locus and necessitates the inherence of himself in that locus, conditioned upon the existence of that locus, and necessitates the inherence of himself in that locus, conditioned upon the existence of that locus. And by this estimation, his essence is not [in need of that locus. Because the necessities of a thing and its effects are necessarily obtained upon the obtainment of that effect, while the effect is not in need of the effect.] Then we say: why is it not permissible for Him to inhere in a thing while it is possible for His inherence in it? His statement: 'because the inheren in the locus is in need of that locus, and that which is not inheren in it is independent of it, and for one thing with respect to one thing to be both independent of it and in need of it is impossible.' So we say: Why did you say that the inheren in a thing is in need of it? Do you not see that a particular body occurs in a particular space after having not been in it, while it is not in need in its essence of inhering in that particular space? So if this is understood regarding occurrence in space, why is not the like of it understood regarding inherence in a locus? And know that these questions are valid against this method and it is difficult to answer them.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents an initial argument against the doctrine that God can inhere in a created locus, followed by a strong objection. The author first states the argument: if God could inhere in something, He would either do so necessarily or possibly. If necessarily, then He would be dependent on that locus; if possibly, then He would change from being independent to being dependent—both impossible. Therefore, inherence is false. An objector then raises a counterexample: man inhering in a place does not imply need, because his essence may necessitate that inherence only when the place exists, just as a cause necessitates its effect without being dependent on it. The objector further argues that the initial reasoning is flawed because a body can occur in a space without needing that space in its essence. The author acknowledges that these objections are valid against this method and hard to answer, setting the stage for a better approach.

Key Terms
الحلول (al-ḥulūl)
المحل (al-maḥall)
الغني (al-ghanī)
المحتاج (al-muḥtāj)
الوجوب والجواز (al-wujūb wa-l-jawāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the initial argument against inherence is conclusive and airtight.
Correction: The objection using the example of a body in space shows that inherence does not necessarily entail dependence, thus the argument is not conclusive.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author initially gives for the impossibility of God inhering in a locus?
A) Because God is eternal and the locus is temporal.
B) Because the one who inheres must be in need of the locus, while God is independent.
C) Because a locus must be created and God cannot be created.
D) Because inherence implies spatial location, which God transcends.

Key Takeaway
The initial argument against inherence based on dependency is challenged by a counterexample, showing that the issue requires deeper analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could not exist without something else, would your trust in His absolute power be shaken? This lesson shows that even the thought of God being dependent is impossible. In your life, do you sometimes act as if God needs your efforts or your worship? Examine one area where you might be placing conditions on God's sufficiency, and resolve to repent from that mindset today.
?
The objection raised against the argument for God's transcendence is acknowledged as difficult to answer. Have you ever encountered an intellectual difficulty about God that made you question your faith? Instead of running from it, commit to seeking a scholar or a reliable source this week to discuss that doubt.
?
The author presents an argument and then honestly admits its weakness. How often do you, when you are wrong, readily acknowledge it? This lesson models intellectual humility. Think of a recent situation where you held a view strongly but were proven wrong. Did you admit it? If not, seek out the person and acknowledge your error, following the example of the author.

Two Meanings of Inherence and Their Impossibility for God
Definition of inherence in two senses and proof of its impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two intelligible meanings of inherence and why both are impossible for God.

Core Idea
Inherence requires spatiality or dependence—both impossible for Allah.

Before we can judge whether inherence is possible for God, we must first understand what inherence means. The author defines two clear meanings and shows that each leads to a contradiction with God’s transcendence and self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining inherence
Read the Arabic passage up to 'فهذا هو الكلام الملخص في هذا الباب' and its translation. Focus on the two meanings.
2
Identify the two meanings
List the two meanings: spatial inherence and inherence of an attribute. Note how each is defined.
3
Understand the impossibility for God
For each meaning, see why the author says it is impossible for God: spatial inherence requires spatiality, attribute-inherence requires dependence.
God is transcendent and self-sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأولى : أن نقول : البحث عن أنه هل يجوز أن يصير حالا في شيء ، أم لا : مسبوق بالبحث عن ماهية الحلول ؟ فنقول : المعقول من الحلول أمران : الأول : كون الصورة حاصلة في الحيز المعين، تبعا لحصول محلها فيه . فإذا قلنا اللون حال في الجسم، فإن معناه أن اللون حصل في ذلك الحيز المعين ، تبعا لحصول ذلك الجسم فيه . والحلول بهذا التفسير إنما يعقل في الشيء الذي يكون حاصلا في الجهة والحيز ، فإذا كان الباري تعالى منزها عن هذه الصفة ، كان إثبات الحلول في حقه محالا . والتفسير الثاني للحلول : كونه مختصا به ، مع كونه محتاجا إليه كقولنا : إن صفة العلم والقدرة حالة في ذات العالم القادر . والحلول بهذا الوجه مفسر باحتياج الصفة إلى الموصوف ، ولما كان الإله تعالى واجب الوجود لذاته ، ممتنع الافتقار إلى الغير ، كان حصول الحلول في حقه بهذا التفسير محالا . وهذا هو المعقول من لفظ الحلول ، وقد ثبت أن ذلك في حق الله تعالى ممتنع . فأما الحلول بتفسير ثالث فهو غير معقول ولا متصور ، فكان الكلام في إثباته ونفيه محالا . فهذا هو الكلام الملخص في هذا الباب ثم نقول : إذا جوزتم الحلول على ذات الله ، وجب أن تكونوا شاكين في أنه هل حل في هذه البقة ، وفي هذه النملة ، وفي هذه البعوضة ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : إنه لم يظهر من هذه النملة حال عظيمة مهيبة ، إلا أنا نقول : هذا إشارة إلى أنه لم يوجد ما يدل على حصول هذا الحلول ، ولا يلزم من عدم علمنا بحصول الدليل ، عدم المدلول . فيثبت : أن من جوز الحلول لزمه أن يبقى شاكا في كل واحد من هذه الأجسام الخسيسة ، أنه تعالى هل صار حالا فيها أم لا ؟ ولما كان ذلك باطلا ، كان القول بالحلول باطلا .
The better [way] is to say: The inquiry into whether it is possible for Him to become inheren in a thing or not is preceded by inquiry into the quiddity of inherence. So we say: The intelligible from inherence are two things: First: the form's occurrence in a particular space, following the occurrence of its locus therein. So when we say color is inherent in a body, its meaning is that color occurred in that particular space following the occurrence of that body in it. And inherence by this interpretation is only intelligible in a thing that occurs in direction and space. So if the Exalted Creator is transcendent above this attribute, then affirming inherence with respect to Him is impossible. The second interpretation of inherence: its being specific to it, while being in need of it, like our saying: the attribute of knowledge and power are in it in the essence of the knowing, powerful being. And inherence in this sense is interpreted by the attribute's need for the qualified. And since the Deity, Exalted, is necessary of existence in Himself, impossible of being in need of another, inherence with respect to Him by this interpretation is impossible. And this is the intelligible from the term 'inherence', and it has been established that this is impossible with respect to God, Exalted. As for inherence by a third interpretation, it is unintelligible and unconceivable, so speech about affirming or negating it is absurd. So this is the summarized speech on this topic. Then we say: If you permit inherence upon the essence of God, then you must be in doubt as to whether He inhered in this flea, and in this ant, and in this mosquito? The utmost that can be said is: that there has not appeared from this ant a great, awe-inspiring state, but we say: This indicates that there does not exist what indicates the occurrence of this inherence, and lack of our knowledge of the existence of the proof does not necessitate lack of the proven. So it is established: that whoever permits inherence must remain in doubt about each one of these vile bodies as to whether the Exalted became inheren in them or not. And since that is false, the statement of inherence is false.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage provides a refined approach to refuting inherence by first defining it clearly. The author states that the inquiry into inherence must begin with understanding what inherence means. Two intelligible meanings are given: (1) spatial inherence, like color in a body, which requires the subject to occupy physical space—impossible for God who transcends space. (2) inherence of an attribute in a subject, like knowledge in a knower, which requires the attribute to be dependent on the subject—impossible for God who is necessarily self-sufficient. Any other meaning is unintelligible. Then the author adds a reductio ad absurdum: if inherence were possible for God, one would have to remain in doubt whether He inheres in every vile creature (flea, ant, mosquito), since lack of evidence of such inherence does not disprove it. Such doubt is absurd, so inherence is false.

Key Terms
ماهية الحلول (māhiyyat al-ḥulūl)
الصورة (al-ṣūra)
الحيز (al-ḥayyiz)
الموصوف (al-mawṣūf)
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inherence is only about spatial indwelling (like God being inside creation).
Correction: The author identifies two meanings: spatial inherence and attribute-dependence. Both are impossible for God, but they are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the second interpretation of inherence (attribute in a subject) impossible for God?
A) Because God does not have attributes.
B) Because it requires God to be spatially located.
C) Because it implies God's attributes are in need of God, but God Himself cannot be in need of anything.
D) Because it is an unintelligible concept.

Key Takeaway
Inherence either requires spatial location or dependence, both of which contradict God's transcendence and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever subconsciously thought of God as being 'in' a particular place—like the sky, a mosque, or the Kaaba—as if He occupies that space? This lesson proves that such a thought reduces God to a physical object. What specific step will you take today to undo that mental image and truly internalize that He is beyond all places?
?
When you say 'God's mercy' or 'God's power,' do you treat these as separate things that God 'has' or 'gives,' almost like objects inhering in Him? This lesson shows that inherence implies dependence, which is impossible for God. How will you reshape your understanding of God's attributes from now on, knowing that they are not separate from Him but are Him?
?
The lesson ends by declaring any third meaning of inherence unintelligible and not worth discussing. Are there vague ideas about God that you hold that are actually meaningless when examined? Commit to stopping your mind from speculating about God in ways that have no basis in revelation—starting with one specific imagination you will abandon today.

The Reductio Argument Against Inherence
Reductio argument: if inherence possible for God, then absurd doubt follows, so inherence is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument that if inherence were possible for God, one would have to remain in doubt about whether He inheres in every lowly creature, an absurdity that disproves inherence.

Core Idea
Allowing inherence forces endless doubt about God's presence in fleas and ants.

Having defined inherence and shown its impossibility in two senses, the author adds a powerful reductio: if you still claim inherence is possible, you must accept that God could be inherent in any vile body, and you cannot rule it out. This leads to absurd skepticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio passage
Read the Arabic from 'ثم نقول' to the end and its translation. Notice the conditional structure.
2
Identify the assumption and consequence
The assumption: inherence is possible for God. The consequence: you must doubt whether He inheres in every lowly creature, because lack of evidence doesn't prove non-occurrence.
3
Understand why this is absurd
Such endless doubt is irrational. Therefore, the original assumption (that inherence is possible) must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage provides a refined approach to refuting inherence by first defining it clearly. The author states that the inquiry into inherence must begin with understanding what inherence means. Two intelligible meanings are given: (1) spatial inherence, like color in a body, which requires the subject to occupy physical space—impossible for God who transcends space. Key Terms
ماهية الحلول (māhiyyat al-ḥulūl)
الصورة (al-ṣūra)
الحيز (al-ḥayyiz)
الموصوف (al-mawṣūf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If there is no evidence of God inhering in a creature, then He does not inhere there.
Correction: The author points out that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence; one cannot rule out inherence simply because no sign appears. This forces the supporter into doubt.

Checkpoint
According to the author, if someone believes inherence is possible for God, what must they conclude about His relation to lowly creatures like fleas?
A) God definitely does not inhere in them because they are unworthy.
B) They must remain in doubt about whether God inheres in them.
C) God inheres only in noble creatures.
D) God cannot inhere in any creature because it would imply limitation.

Key Takeaway
If inherence were possible for God, we would have to be in perpetual doubt about His presence in every creature, an absurdity that disproves inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know God is not in a flea, but do you live as though He is present in the most insignificant parts of your life — your thoughts, your habits, your hidden sins? What would change if you really believed He is just as aware of the small as the great? Will you confess one area where you act as if God doesn't notice?
?
This passage shows that doubt about God's presence in lowly things is absurd. But think: when you are alone, or in a moment of weakness, do you ever doubt that God sees you — that He is with you in that very moment? If the people of inherence were wrong to doubt, how much more wrong is your doubt about God's all-encompassing knowledge? Will you repent of that doubt today and trust that He is with you even in your lowest moments?

Two Meanings of Hulul and Their Impossibility for God
The author defines two interpretations of hulul and proves their impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two intelligible interpretations of hulul (indwelling) and why both are impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
God transcends spatial location and needs—only two interpretations of hulul apply to creation, not to God.

The author begins by clarifying what hulul actually means, because the term is often misunderstood. He identifies two coherent interpretations and shows why each contradicts the divine nature. Only after this foundation can he proceed to a more dialectical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two interpretations
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Let the author's own definitions come through.
Notice the phrase 'أمران' which signals an enumeration.
2
Identify each interpretation and its refutation
The first interpretation is spatial indwelling; the second is metaphysical dependence. For each, note the reason why it cannot apply to God.
The refutation for the first is God's transcendence of space; for the second, God's self-sufficiency.
3
Understand the third interpretation
The author mentions a third interpretation but dismisses it as unintelligible. Ask yourself why he does this.
This shows the author's method: only meaningful definitions can be discussed.
4
Reflect on the author's method
Notice how the author first defines terms, then proves impossibility. This is a common kalam approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمران : الأول : كون الصورة حاصلة في الحيز المعين، تبعا لحصول محلها فيه . فإذا قلنا اللون حال في الجسم، فإن معناه أن اللون حصل في ذلك الحيز المعين ، تبعا لحصول ذلك الجسم فيه . والحلول بهذا التفسير إنما يعقل في الشيء الذي يكون حاصلا في الجهة والحيز ، فإذا كان الباري تعالى منزها عن هذه الصفة ، كان إثبات الحلول في حقه محالا . والتفسير الثاني للحلول : كونه مختصا به ، مع كونه محتاجا إليه كقولنا : إن صفة العلم والقدرة حالة في ذات العالم القادر . والحلول بهذا الوجه مفسر باحتياج الصفة إلى الموصوف ، ولما كان الإله تعالى واجب الوجود لذاته ، ممتنع الافتقار إلى الغير ، كان حصول الحلول في حقه بهذا التفسير محالا . وهذا هو المعقول من لفظ الحلول ، وقد ثبت أن ذلك في حق الله تعالى ممتنع . فأما الحلول بتفسير ثالث فهو غير معقول ولا متصور ، فكان الكلام في إثباته ونفيه محالا . فهذا هو الكلام الملخص في هذا الباب
ثم نقول : إذا جوزتم الحلول على ذات الله ، وجب أن تكونوا شاكين في أنه هل حل في هذه البقة ، وفي هذه النملة ، وفي هذه البعوضة ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : إنه لم يظهر من هذه النملة حال عظيمة مهيبة ، إلا أنا نقول : هذا إشارة إلى أنه لم يوجد ما يدل على حصول هذا الحلول ، ولا يلزم من عدم علمنا بحصول الدليل ، عدم المدلول . فيثبت : أن من جوز الحلول لزمه أن يبقى شاكا في كل واحد من هذه الأجسام الخسيسة ، أنه تعالى هل صار حالا فيها أم لا ؟ ولما كان ذلك باطلا ، كان القول بالحلول باطلا .
Two matters: The first: that the form is present in a particular location, following the presence of its substrate therein. So when we say that color is indwelling in the body, its meaning is that the color occurs in that particular location, following the presence of that body therein. Indwelling according to this interpretation is only conceivable for something that is located in a direction and place. Since the Most High Creator is transcendent of this attribute, affirming indwelling with respect to Him is impossible. The second interpretation of indwelling: that it is particular to it while being in need of it, like our statement: the attribute of knowledge and power is indwelling in the essence of the knowing, powerful one. Indwelling in this sense is interpreted as the attribute's need for the described. Since the Most High God is necessary of existence in Himself and cannot depend on another, indwelling with respect to Him in this interpretation is impossible. This is the intelligible meaning of the term indwelling, and it has been established that this is impossible with respect to God Most High. As for a third interpretation of indwelling, it is unintelligible and inconceivable, so discussion of affirming or denying it is impossible. This is the summarized discussion on this topic. Then we say: If you allow indwelling for the essence of God, you must be doubtful as to whether He indwells in this bedbug, in this ant, and in this mosquito. The most that can be said is that no great awe-inspiring state has appeared from this ant. But we say: This indicates that nothing has been found to indicate the occurrence of this indwelling, and our lack of knowledge of the existence of evidence does not necessitate the non-existence of what is indicated. So it is established that whoever allows indwelling must remain doubtful about every one of these base bodies as to whether the Most High has become indwelling in it or not. Since that is false, the statement of indwelling is false.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage refutes the doctrine of hulul (indwelling) in God. It begins by describing two distinct interpretations of hulul and showing each to be impossible for God. The first interpretation is spatial indwelling: a thing is present in a location because its substrate is there. Since God is not spatial, this is impossible. The second interpretation is the dependence of an attribute on its subject: an attribute needs the subject. Since God is self-sufficient and not in need, this too is impossible. A third interpretation is said to be unintelligible and therefore not worth discussing. The author then presents a second argument: if one allows the possibility of hulul in God, one would have to remain uncertain whether God indwells in every lowly creature like bedbugs, ants, and mosquitoes. The absence of evidence for such indwelling does not prove its non-existence, so the believer in hulul must remain in doubt. Since such doubt is absurd, the doctrine of hulul is false.

Key Terms
الحلول (hulul, indwelling)
الحيز (location)
المحل (substrate)
الافتقار (need/dependence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think hulul only refers to spatial indwelling.
Correction: The author shows a second interpretation: the dependence of attributes. Both are impossible for God, and any possible significant meaning is covered.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the first interpretation of hulul impossible for God?
A) Because God is not a body
B) Because God is not located in space
C) Because God does not have attributes
D) Because God is not dependent on anything

Key Takeaway
Hulul either means spatial presence (impossible for God) or attribute-dependence (impossible for God), so it cannot apply to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of God as being 'somewhere'—even if vaguely—when you pray or imagine His closeness? How does this lesson's first interpretation force you to strip away that spatial assumption? What concrete step can you take today to mentally purify your concept of God's presence?
?
When you say that God is 'knowing' or 'powerful,' do you unconsciously feel that these qualities depend on God the same way your own knowledge depends on you? How does the second interpretation of hulul challenge that analogy? Will you now actively re-frame how you speak of God's attributes?
?
The author dismisses a third interpretation as 'unintelligible' and not worth discussing. Are there thoughts or images you hold about God that are similarly vague or borrowed from created things? What will you do—starting today—to root them out and replace them with what God has revealed and reason confirms?

The Argument from Necessary Doubt Against Hulul
The author argues that allowing hulul leads to necessary doubt about God's indwelling in base bodies, which is absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why allowing the possibility of hulul leads to necessary doubt about God's indwelling in lowly creatures, which is absurd.

Core Idea
If hulul were possible, you couldn't be sure God doesn't indwell in a bedbug—an unacceptable doubt.

The author now shifts to a dialectical argument. He points out that those who say hulul is possible must also admit the possibility that God indwells in every lowly creature. Since there is no way to disprove that possibility, they would be doomed to perpetual doubt—and that is clearly false.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from necessary doubt
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow: the premise, the implication, and the conclusion.
Look for the conditional 'إذا' which sets up the logical implication.
2
Understand the logical implication
If one allows hulul for God, then it is possible for Him to indwell in any body, including lowly ones. The author forces this conclusion.
3
Analyze the counter-argument
A possible objection: 'We don't see any awe in lowly creatures.' The author rejects this: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.
This is a key epistemological point.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since the doctrine leads to absurd doubt (and certainty is required), the doctrine is false.
