Razi's Second Proof: The Dilemma of Eternal vs. Originated Pleasure
Razi presents a second argument showing that pleasure in Allah leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi's second proof shows that attributing sensory pleasure to Allah leads to the absurd conclusion that the world is eternal.

Core Idea
If Allah experienced pleasure, the world would be eternal—impossible.

Razi presents a powerful dilemma: any pleasure Allah experiences must involve an object. That object must either be eternal or created. He then shows both options lead to the impossibility of the world being co-eternal with Allah. This argument further establishes divine transcendence beyond physical attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two branches of the dilemma
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi sets up a clear 'either-or' structure: if Allah experiences pleasure, the pleasurable object is either pre-eternal or originated.
Pay attention to the logical flow: 'إما أن يكون حاصلا في الأزل وإما أن لا يكون'.
2
Understand the first horn: eternal pleasure implies eternal object
The first branch argues that if the pleasure is eternal, then the object causing it must also be eternal. This implies the world (the object) is pre-eternal, which contradicts Islamic doctrine.
Razi notes that the pre-eternity of the world has already been refuted elsewhere.
3
Understand the second horn: originated pleasure leads to infinite regress
The second branch argues that if the pleasure is originated, then Allah, knowing eternally that He can attain it and having no obstacle, must originate the object. But at any supposed time of origination, the motive and lack of obstacles existed earlier, forcing origination even earlier—leading to an infinite regress and ultimately the object's pre-eternity.
This regress shows that the 'originated' horn collapses into the first horn.
4
Recognize that both horns are impossible, so the premise is false
Since both branches lead to the absurd conclusion of the world's pre-eternity (which is unacceptable), the original assumption that Allah experiences sensory pleasure must be impossible.
The proof is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على امتناع حصول اللذة في حق الله تعالى : أن نقول : لو التذ الله تعالى بشيء ، لكان الملتذ به إما أن يكون حاصلا في الأزل وإما أن لا يكون . والقسمان باطلان فالقول بكونه ملتذاً باطل. بيان أنه يمتنع كونه ملتذا في الأزل : ذلك لأن الالتذاذ بالشيء مشروط بحصول الملتذ به ، فلو كانت اللذة أزلية لكان المتلذذ به أزلياً فيلزم قدم العالم، وقد أبطلناه. وبيان أنه يمتنع القول بأن كونه ملتذا صفة حادثة : وذلك لأن حصول الالتذاذ في حق من يصح عليه الالتذاذ أمر مطلوب الحصول . فإذا كان الله تعالى عالما في الأزل بأنه يمكنه تحصيل الالتذاذ ، ولا مانع له من تحصيل ذلك الشيء البتة ، وجب أن يحدث ذلك الملتذ به . وهذا محال ، لأنه لا وقت يفرض أن الله تعالى يحدثه فيه إلا والداعي إلى إحداثه كان حاصلا قبل ذلك ، وكانت الموانع المانعة عن إحداثه زائلة ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يحدثه قبل ذلك الوقت ، فلزم أن يقال : إنه لا وقت إلا والله تعالى قد أحدث ذلك الملتذ به قبل ذلك الوقت ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لزم أن يقال : إن الله أحدثه في الأزل ، وحينئذ يرجع الكلام إلى القسم الأول ، وهو أن الملتذ به كان حاصلا في الأزل ، ويلزم منه قدم العالم . وقد أبطلناه . فثبت أن القول بصحة كونه تعالى ملتذا أمر يفضي إلى أحد هذين القسمين، وثبت كونهما باطلين ، فكان القول بجواز اللذة على الله تعالى محالا . هذا هو الكلام في اللذات الحسية
The second proof for the impossibility of pleasure occurring in the case of Allah Almighty: That we say: If Allah Almighty experienced pleasure from something, then the thing by which pleasure is experienced would either be existent in pre-eternity or not. Both divisions are invalid, so the statement that He is experiencing pleasure is invalid. Explanation that it is impossible for Him to experience pleasure in pre-eternity: That is because experiencing pleasure by something is conditioned by the existence of the thing by which pleasure is experienced. So if the pleasure were pre-eternal, then the thing by which pleasure is experienced would be pre-eternal, leading to the pre-eternity of the world, which we have already refuted. And explanation that it is impossible to say that His being experiencing pleasure is an originated attribute: That is because the occurrence of experiencing pleasure in the case of one for whom experiencing pleasure is possible is something whose occurrence is desired. So if Allah Almighty knew in pre-eternity that He could obtain the experience of pleasure, and there was no obstacle at all preventing Him from obtaining that thing, then He must have originated that thing by which pleasure is experienced. But this is impossible, because there is no time in which you suppose that Allah Almighty originates it except that the motive for originating it existed before that, and the obstacles preventing its origination were removed. If this is the case, then He must have originated it before that time, so it would be necessary to say: There is no time except that Allah Almighty has originated that thing by which pleasure is experienced before that time. If this is the case, then it would be necessary to say that Allah originated it in pre-eternity, and then the discussion returns to the first division, namely that the thing by which pleasure is experienced existed in pre-eternity, and from that follows the pre-eternity of the world. And we have already refuted that. So it is established that the statement that it is valid for Him Almighty to experience pleasure leads to one of these two divisions, and both are established as invalid, so the statement that pleasure is permissible for Allah Almighty is impossible. This is the discussion concerning sensory pleasures.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained proof (burhan) for the impossibility of physical pleasure in Allah. Razi presents a dilemma: If Allah experienced pleasure, the pleasurable object must be either eternal or originated. Both are impossible. The first horn: If the pleasure is eternal, then the pleasurable object must also be eternal, implying the eternity of the world, which is already refuted (presumably by previous arguments). The second horn: If the pleasure is originated, then Allah, knowing in eternity that He could attain that pleasure and having no impediment, would have to originate the pleasurable object. But this leads to an infinite regress: at any supposed time of origination, the motive and lack of obstacles existed earlier, so He would have originated it earlier, ultimately leading back to pre-eternity, which again implies the eternity of the world. Since both horns lead to absurdity, the premise (that Allah experiences pleasure) is impossible. Razi concludes that sensory pleasure in Allah is categorically impossible.

Key Terms
التذاذ
ملتذ به
أزل
حادث
قدم العالم
داعي
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Allah could originate a pleasure without any regress, choosing a specific time freely.
Correction: The argument shows that because Allah's knowledge and power are eternal, and the motive and absence of obstacles are also eternal, there is no reason to prefer one time over another. This forces an infinite regress, ultimately making the object pre-eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is it impossible for Allah to have originated pleasure?
A) Because pleasure is always physical and Allah is not physical.
B) Because Allah's knowledge and power are eternal, so if He had the motive and no obstacle, He would have originated the pleasurable object earlier, leading to an infinite regress and ultimately the eternity of the world.
C) Because pleasure requires change and Allah does not change.
D) Because the pleasurable object must be eternal, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof decisively shows that sensory pleasure in Allah is impossible because it leads to the absurd consequence of the world being eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah experiencing human-like emotions, even if you know it's wrong? After studying Razi's proof, what concrete steps can you take to purge any subtle anthropomorphism from your heart?
?
Razi argues that attributing sensory pleasure to Allah leads to absurdity. Do you truly live as though Allah is absolutely transcendent, or do you sometimes act as if He shares your weaknesses? What would change in your worship if you fully internalized this?
?
If Allah cannot experience pleasure, what does that imply about your own pursuit of pleasure? Are you seeking after fleeting delights while neglecting the One who is independent of all wants? How can you reorient your desires to please Allah without projecting human needs onto Him?

Why 'Jawhar' Does Not Imply Genus for Allah
An objection and its answer regarding the term 'jawhar' and divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'jawhar' (substance), when applied to Allah as 'independence from a substrate,' does not imply genus and composition, and how negative concepts differ from positive attributes in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Negative concepts cannot be parts of a quiddity, so 'independence' is no genus.

In Islamic theology, a common objection to describing Allah as 'jawhar' (substance) is that it would make Him composed of genus and differentia, implying contingency. The author refutes this by clarifying that the term 'jawhar' here means independence from a substrate, which is a negative concept. A negative concept cannot be a part of an essence, so it cannot serve as a genus. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and shows how theological precision preserves divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the critic's argument: if 'jawhar' is a genus, then Allah would fall under it, necessitating composition and contingency.
2
Identify the author's key distinction
Notice that the author defines 'jawhar' differently: not as a genus but as a negative concept (independence from a substrate).
3
Understand why a negative concept cannot be a genus
The genus is a part of the quiddity. A negative concept cannot be a part of an existent quiddity, so the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : المفهوم من الجوهر : جنس لما تحته ، فلو صدق عليه تعالى كونه جوهرا لكان داخلا تحت الجنس ، وكل ما كان داخلا تحت الجنس كان امتيازه عن سائر الأنواع بالفصل فيلزم كون ذاته مركبة من الجنس والفصل ، وذلك محال ، لما ثبت أن كل مركب فهو ممكن ، ولا شيء من واجب الوجود بممكن . والجواب : إن الجنس عبارة عن الجزء المشترك ، فالجنس جزء من أجزاء الماهية ، والاستغناء عن المحل مفهوم عدمي ، والمفهوم العدمي يمتنع أن يكون جزءاً من أجزاء الماهية الموجودة ، فيثبت أن الجوهر بهذا المعنى ليس جزءاً من أجزاء الماهية ، والجنس جزء من أجزاء الماهية ، فيمتنع كون الجوهر بهذا المعنى جنساً . فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب والله أعلم
If someone says: The concept of 'jawhar' is a genus (jins) for what is under it. So if it is true of Him, exalted, that He is a jawhar, He would fall under the genus. And everything that falls under a genus is distinguished from other species by a differentia (fasl), necessitating that His essence is composed of genus and differentia, and that is impossible because it is established that every composite is possible (mumkin), and nothing of the Necessary Existence is possible. The answer: The genus is the common part; thus the genus is a part of the parts of the quiddity (mahiyya). But independence from a substrate is a negative concept (mafhum 'adami), and a negative concept cannot be a part of the parts of an existent quiddity. So it is established that jawhar in this sense is not a part of the parts of the quiddity, while the genus is a part of the parts of the quiddity. Therefore, it is impossible for jawhar in this sense to be a genus. This is the complete discussion on this topic, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if 'jawhar' is a genus, then applying it to Allah would make His essence composed of genus and differentia, implying contingency (since composites are possible beings, not necessary). The author replies by distinguishing the meaning of 'jawhar' used here—independence from a substrate—which is a negative concept (mafhum 'adami). A genus is a part of the quiddity, but a negative concept cannot be a part of an existent quiddity. Therefore, 'jawhar' in this sense is not a genus, and the objection fails. The phrase 'al-ghina' (independence) is a negation of need, not a positive attribute that could serve as a genus.

Key Terms
جنس (jins)
فصل (fasl)
مركب (murakkab)
ممكن (mumkin)
مفهوم عدمي (mafhum 'adami)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any term predicated of Allah, like 'jawhar', must be a genus or a universal, implying composition.
Correction: The term 'jawhar' when meaning independence from a substrate is a negative concept, and negative concepts cannot be parts of a quiddity, so they cannot be genera.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'jawhar' (as independence from a substrate) cannot be a genus?
A) Because a genus must be a positive concept, but independence is a negative concept.
B) Because a genus must apply to many things, but only Allah is independent.
C) Because the term 'jawhar' is ambiguous.
D) Because a genus implies temporal priority.

Key Takeaway
The term 'jawhar' when applied to Allah means independence from a substrate, a negative concept that cannot be a genus, thus preserving divine simplicity and necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is self-sufficient (al-Ghani),' is that merely a word you repeat, or do you truly live as if you need nothing but Him? What attachments in your heart still imply that you rely on others more than on Allah?
?
This lesson shows how easy it is to apply human logic to Allah and fall into error. Have you ever thought of Allah in a way that limits or composes Him, even subtly? What will you do to purify your understanding of His oneness?
?

The Metaphorical Nature of 'Qā'im bi al-Nafs' and the Derivation of 'Jawhar'
Sanusi explains the metaphorical usage of 'qā'im bi al-nafs' and the linguistic root of 'jawhar'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'qā'im bi al-nafs' is considered metaphorical when applied to God and how the linguistic root of 'jawhar' relates to independence.

Core Idea
Divine self-subsistence is understood metaphorically, not as literal substance.

Sanusi begins by clarifying that while God is necessarily self-subsisting, the phrase 'qā'im bi al-nafs' itself is a metaphor when applied to Him. He then explores the linguistic origin of 'jawhar' to show that its core meaning of brilliance and permanence actually points to God more than to creatures, though the term is still not literally applicable. This lesson unpacks these nuanced discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the metaphor of 'qā'im bi al-nafs'
Read the first part of the Arabic passage that explains why 'qā'im bi al-nafs' is metaphorical in two ways. Notice how the author uses the concept of shared terms (mushtarak).
2
Understand the derivation of 'jawhar'
Read the next part where the author traces 'jawhar' to 'jahāra' (brilliance) and argues that greater independence implies greater permanence, making God most deserving of the term in a certain sense.
This does not mean the term is applied literally; it is a linguistic point.
3
Reflect on the overall point
Consider why the author spends time on these linguistic points. They prepare the ground for the objection and answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجب الوجود لذاته كونه قائما بالنفس ولا يطلق عليه لفظ الجوهر، وهذا النزاع بعيد، لأن لفظ القائم بالنفس مجاز من وجهين: أحدهما: إن إطلاق لفظ القائم بالنفس لإفادة معنى الاستغناء مجاز. والثاني: إن إطلاق لفظ النفس لإرادة الذات والماهية إطلاق للفظ المشترك لإرادة أحد مفهوميه، لأن لفظ النفس قد يراد به الجسد والدم، وقد يراد به الذات والحقيقة. فيثبت: أن لفظ القائم بالنفس مجاز من وجهين. وأما لفظ الجوهر فهو مأخوذ من الجهارة والظهور، وكل ما كان أكمل في الاستغناء عن الغير، كان أبقى وأدوم، فكان معنى الجهارة فيه أكثر، فكان أولى بإطلاق هذا اللفظ عليه. فهذا هو الكلام في تفسير لفظ الجوهر.

فإن قال قائل: المفهوم من الجوهر: جنس لما تحته، فلو صدق عليه تعالى كونه جوهرا لكان داخلا تحت الجنس، وكل ما كان داخلا تحت الجنس كان امتيازه عن سائر الأنواع بالفصل فيلزم كون ذاته مركبة من الجنس والفصل، وذلك محال، لما ثبت أن كل مركب فهو ممكن، ولا شيء من واجب الوجود بممكن. والجواب: إن الجنس عبارة عن الجزء المشترك، فالجنس جزء من أجزاء الماهية، والاستغناء عن المحل مفهوم عدمي، والمفهوم العدمي يمتنع أن يكون جزءاً من أجزاء الماهية، فيثبت أن الجوهر بهذا المعنى ليس جزءاً من أجزاء الماهية، والجنس جزء من أجزاء الماهية، فيمتنع كون الجوهر بهذا المعنى جنساً. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب والله أعلم.
The Necessary Existence in itself being self-subsisting, and the term 'jawhar' (substance) is not applied to Him, and this dispute is far-fetched, because the term 'al-qā'im bi al-nafs' (self-subsisting) is metaphorical in two respects: First, applying the term 'al-qā'im bi al-nafs' to convey the meaning of independence is metaphorical. Second, applying the term 'al-nafs' (self) to intend the essence and quiddity is an application of a shared term (mushtarak) to intend one of its two meanings, because the term 'al-nafs' may be used to mean the body and blood, and may be used to mean the essence and reality. So it is established that the term 'al-qā'im bi al-nafs' is metaphorical in two respects. As for the term 'jawhar', it is derived from 'al-jahāra' (brilliance) and 'al-ẓuhūr' (manifestation), and whatever is more complete in independence from others is more lasting and enduring, so the meaning of brilliance is greater in it, so it is more deserving of applying this term to Him. This is the discourse on interpreting the term 'jawhar'.

If someone says: The concept of 'jawhar' is a genus (jins) for what is under it. So if it were true of Him the Exalted that He is a jawhar, He would fall under the genus, and whatever falls under a genus is distinguished from other species by a differentia (faṣl), leading to His essence being composed of genus and differentia, and that is impossible, because it is established that every composite is possible (mumkin), and none of the Necessary Existence is possible. The answer: 'Genus' is an expression for the common part; so the genus is a part of the parts of the quiddity (māhiyya), and independence from a substrate (al-istighnā' 'an al-maḥall) is a negative (ʿadamī) concept, and a negative concept cannot be part of the parts of the quiddity. So it is established that jawhar in this sense is not a part of the parts of the quiddity, and the genus is a part of the parts of the quiddity, so it is impossible for jawhar in this sense to be a genus. This is the complete discourse on this chapter, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the inapplicability of the term 'jawhar' (substance) to God. The author begins by stating that the Necessary Existence is self-subsisting (qā'im bi al-nafs) and that the term 'jawhar' is not applied to Him. He explains that the dispute over this is far-fetched because the term 'qā'im bi al-nafs' itself is metaphorical in two ways: first, using it to mean independence is metaphorical; second, the word 'nafs' is a homonym (mushtarak) that can mean both 'body and blood' or 'essence and reality'. Thus applying it to God is not literal. Next, the author discusses 'jawhar', noting its derivation from brilliance (jahāra) and manifestation (ẓuhūr). He argues that completeness in independence from others makes a thing more deserving of the term, but that does not mean it applies literally to God. The author then presents an objection: if 'jawhar' is a genus (jins), and God is called a jawhar, then He would fall under a genus and be distinguished by a differentia (faṣl), making His essence composed of genus and differentia. Composition implies contingency (imkān), which is impossible for the Necessary Existence. The author answers this by clarifying that 'genus' means a part of the quiddity (māhiyya), but independence from a substrate is a negative (ʿadamī) concept, and negative concepts cannot be parts of a quiddity. Therefore, 'jawhar' in the sense of independence is not a part of the quiddity, so it cannot be a genus. Thus, the objection fails, and the term 'jawhar' cannot be applied to God in a way that implies composition. The passage concludes the chapter.

جوهر (jawhar)
مجاز (majāz)
لفظ مشترك (lafẓ mushtarak)
استغناء (istighnā')
جهارة (jahāra)
ظهور (ẓuhūr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'qā'im bi al-nafs' is a literal description of God's essence.
Correction: The author shows it is metaphorical because it uses a term (nafs) that can mean body, and the concept of independence is negative.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'qā'im bi al-nafs' is a metaphor when applied to God, and the term 'jawhar' linguistically points to God's perfection but is not used as a theological predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as ‘self-subsisting’ (qā'im bi al-nafs), do you catch yourself imagining a literal being standing on its own? How does realizing this phrase is metaphorical reshape your mental image of Allah? Will you now actively remind yourself that your words fall short of His reality?
?
The root of ‘jawhar’ points to brilliance and manifestation. Where in your life are you seeking brilliance or permanence from created things rather than from the One who alone is truly independent? What concrete step will you take today to shift your reliance from creation to the Creator?
?
Sanusi shows that even our most refined theological language is metaphorical. Does this humble your certainty about how well you understand Allah? Will you allow this to make you more cautious and reverent when describing His attributes, or will you continue as before?

Refuting the Objection: Jawhar as Genus Would Imply Composition
Sanusi presents and refutes an objection based on genus-composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that calling God 'jawhar' would make Him composite and the author's refutation based on the negative nature of independence.

Core Idea
Independence from substrate is a negative notion that cannot be a part of quiddity.

An objector argues that if 'jawhar' is a genus, then calling God a 'jawhar' would place Him under a genus, requiring a differentia and resulting in composition. Since composition implies contingency, this would contradict God's necessary existence. The author responds by clarifying that 'jawhar' in the sense of independence from substrate is a negative concept (ʿadamī) and thus cannot be a part of quiddity, so the objection fails. This lesson walks through the logic of this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part beginning with 'فإن قال قائل'. Understand the step-by-step reasoning: if God is a jawhar, then He falls under a genus, requires a differentia, becomes composite, and composition implies possibility, which is impossible for the Necessary Existence.
2
Study the author's answer
Read the author's response. He distinguishes between two senses of 'jawhar': one as genus (part of quiddity) and one as independence (negative concept). He argues that independence from a substrate is a negative concept and therefore cannot be a part of quiddity, so 'jawhar' in that sense is not a genus.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the author's refutation is effective. It undermines the premise that 'jawhar' must be a genus in the philosophical sense when applied to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجب الوجود لذاته كونه قائما بالنفس ولا يطلق عليه لفظ الجوهر، وهذا النزاع بعيد، لأن لفظ القائم بالنفس مجاز من وجهين: أحدهما: إن إطلاق لفظ القائم بالنفس لإفادة معنى الاستغناء مجاز. والثاني: إن إطلاق لفظ النفس لإرادة الذات والماهية إطلاق للفظ المشترك لإرادة أحد مفهوميه، لأن لفظ النفس قد يراد به الجسد والدم، وقد يراد به الذات والحقيقة. فيثبت: أن لفظ القائم بالنفس مجاز من وجهين. وأما لفظ الجوهر فهو مأخوذ من الجهارة والظهور، وكل ما كان أكمل في الاستغناء عن الغير، كان أبقى وأدوم، فكان معنى الجهارة فيه أكثر، فكان أولى بإطلاق هذا اللفظ عليه. فهذا هو الكلام في تفسير لفظ الجوهر.

فإن قال قائل: المفهوم من الجوهر: جنس لما تحته، فلو صدق عليه تعالى كونه جوهرا لكان داخلا تحت الجنس، وكل ما كان داخلا تحت الجنس كان امتيازه عن سائر الأنواع بالفصل فيلزم كون ذاته مركبة من الجنس والفصل، وذلك محال، لما ثبت أن كل مركب فهو ممكن، ولا شيء من واجب الوجود بممكن. والجواب: إن الجنس عبارة عن الجزء المشترك، فالجنس جزء من أجزاء الماهية، والاستغناء عن المحل مفهوم عدمي، والمفهوم العدمي يمتنع أن يكون جزءاً من أجزاء الماهية، فيثبت أن الجوهر بهذا المعنى ليس جزءاً من أجزاء الماهية، والجنس جزء من أجزاء الماهية، فيمتنع كون الجوهر بهذا المعنى جنساً. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب والله أعلم.
The Necessary Existence in itself being self-subsisting, and the term 'jawhar' (substance) is not applied to Him, and this dispute is far-fetched, because the term 'al-qā'im bi al-nafs' (self-subsisting) is metaphorical in two respects: First, applying the term 'al-qā'im bi al-nafs' to convey the meaning of independence is metaphorical. Second, applying the term 'al-nafs' (self) to intend the essence and quiddity is an application of a shared term (mushtarak) to intend one of its two meanings, because the term 'al-nafs' may be used to mean the body and blood, and may be used to mean the essence and reality. So it is established that the term 'al-qā'im bi al-nafs' is metaphorical in two respects. As for the term 'jawhar', it is derived from 'al-jahāra' (brilliance) and 'al-ẓuhūr' (manifestation), and whatever is more complete in independence from others is more lasting and enduring, so the meaning of brilliance is greater in it, so it is more deserving of applying this term to Him. This is the discourse on interpreting the term 'jawhar'.

If someone says: The concept of 'jawhar' is a genus (jins) for what is under it. So if it were true of Him the Exalted that He is a jawhar, He would fall under the genus, and whatever falls under a genus is distinguished from other species by a differentia (faṣl), leading to His essence being composed of genus and differentia, and that is impossible, because it is established that every composite is possible (mumkin), and none of the Necessary Existence is possible. The answer: 'Genus' is an expression for the common part; so the genus is a part of the parts of the quiddity (māhiyya), and independence from a substrate (al-istighnā' 'an al-maḥall) is a negative (ʿadamī) concept, and a negative concept cannot be part of the parts of the quiddity. So it is established that jawhar in this sense is not a part of the parts of the quiddity, and the genus is a part of the parts of the quiddity, so it is impossible for jawhar in this sense to be a genus. This is the complete discourse on this chapter, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the inapplicability of the term 'jawhar' (substance) to God. The author begins by stating that the Necessary Existence is self-subsisting (qā'im bi al-nafs) and that the term 'jawhar' is not applied to Him. He explains that the dispute over this is far-fetched because the term 'qā'im bi al-nafs' itself is metaphorical in two ways: first, using it to mean independence is metaphorical; second, the word 'nafs' is a homonym (mushtarak) that can mean both 'body and blood' or 'essence and reality'. Thus applying it to God is not literal. Next, the author discusses 'jawhar', noting its derivation from brilliance (jahāra) and manifestation (ẓuhūr). He argues that completeness in independence from others makes a thing more deserving of the term, but that does not mean it applies literally to God. The author then presents an objection: if 'jawhar' is a genus (jins), and God is called a jawhar, then He would fall under a genus and be distinguished by a differentia (faṣl), making His essence composed of genus and differentia. Composition implies contingency (imkān), which is impossible for the Necessary Existence. The author answers this by clarifying that 'genus' means a part of the quiddity (māhiyya), but independence from a substrate is a negative (ʿadamī) concept, and negative concepts cannot be parts of a quiddity. Therefore, 'jawhar' in the sense of independence is not a part of the quiddity, so it cannot be a genus. Thus, the objection fails, and the term 'jawhar' cannot be applied to God in a way that implies composition. The passage concludes the chapter.

Key Terms
جنس (jins)
فصل (faṣl)
ماهية (māhiyya)
ممكن (mumkin)
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd)
استغناء عن المحل (istighnā' an al-maḥall)
عدم (ʿadamī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that calling God 'jawhar' would automatically place Him under the category of substance with its genus and differentia.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'jawhar' in the sense of independence is a negative concept, not a genus, so the objection does not apply.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'jawhar' cannot be a genus when applied to God?
A) Because God is not a substance.
B) Because independence from a substrate is a negative concept, and negative concepts cannot be parts of quiddity.
C) Because 'jawhar' is a metaphorical term.
D) Because the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The objection that calling God 'jawhar' implies composition fails because 'jawhar' in the relevant sense is a negative concept, not a genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously assumed that God must fit into categories you understand, like genus-species, because you couldn't conceive of His absolute uniqueness? What specific belief did that lead you to, and how does this lesson call you to repent of that intellectual pride?
?
The objector's error was thinking 'jawhar' means the same for Creator and creature. In your daily life, where do you tend to measure God by created standards – in your expectations of how He should act, or in your understanding of His attributes? What will you do today to actively remind yourself that His attributes are unlike anything in creation?
?
Independence from substrate is a negative concept – it tells us what God is not. Are you content with knowing God through what He is not, or do you secretly crave a positive grasp of His essence that would let you control Him? Surrender your need to fully comprehend Him, and commit to worship based on submission, not intellectual certainty.

God's Transcendence from Opponents and Rivals
The author introduces the second section focusing on God's transcendence from opponents and rivals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the Arabic terms ṣidd and nidd and their significance in the context of tawḥīd.

Core Idea
God has no opponent and no equal — that is the heart of tawḥīd.

This short heading marks the beginning of the second major section of the author's discussion on divine transcendence. The terms used here — ṣidd (opponent) and nidd (rival) — are central to Islamic theology, as they clarify what it means to affirm God's oneness. Let’s unpack what these words mean and why they matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the heading and translation
Read the Arabic passage once along with its English translation. Notice the two key terms.
This is a short section heading that sets the agenda for the coming discussion.
2
Understand the Arabic terms
Focus on the words 'ṣidd' and 'nidd'. Look up their meanings if needed. Ṣidd means opponent or adversary; nidd means rival or equal.
These terms appear together often in Quranic verses about tawḥīd.
3
Connect to the broader theme of tanzīh
Realize that this section is part of proving God's transcendence (tanzīh) from any partner or peer. Why is it important to deny both opponents and rivals?
This connects to the overall goal of establishing pure monotheism.

القسم الثاني في بيان أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن الصد والند
The second section: on explaining that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent from opponents and rivals.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a section heading. It introduces the second major part of the discussion, which focuses on God's transcendence (tanzīh) from opponents (ṣidd) and rivals (nidd). The term 'ṣidd' refers to an opponent or adversary, while 'nidd' means a rival or equal. The section will likely argue that God has no partner or equal in any sense.

Key Takeaway
The second section addresses God's transcendence from any opponent (ṣidd) or rival (nidd), reinforcing the core Islamic belief in tawḥīd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, what things or people do you unconsciously treat as opponents to God's will, or as rivals to His authority? How can you practically acknowledge His sole sovereignty today?
?
If God has no rival, why do you sometimes act as if your desires or fears have the final say over your choices? What would change if you truly lived as if He alone holds ultimate power?
?

Fourth Argument: Inability to Distinguish Two Gods
The author begins the fourth argument against two gods based on indistinguishability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the possibility of two gods, which is based on the impossibility of distinguishing between them, and the objection raised regarding distinction by essence.

Core Idea
Two eternal beings cannot be distinguished, so multiplicity is impossible.

This lesson presents a classic theological argument against polytheism. The argument claims that if there were two gods, they could not be distinguished by time or place, leaving no basis for multiplicity. However, an objector suggests that distinction could be by essence, like knowledge and power in a substance. We'll explore the argument and the beginning of its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument, objection, and answer.
The answer is incomplete; note where it cuts off.
2
Identify the argument
Understand the reasoning: two gods cannot be distinguished by time (both eternal) or place (transcendent of space), so no distinction, hence no multiplicity.
3
Understand the objection
The objector uses the example of knowledge and power inhering in one substance: they are distinguished by essence, not time/place. So why can't two gods be distinguished similarly?
This challenges the argument's premise.
4
Examine the answer
The answer begins by considering whether the distinguishing feature is essential to divinity or not. If essential, the one lacking it is not divine. The passage cuts off before concluding.
Think about how the argument might be completed.
5
Reflect on the incompleteness
Consider why the answer is important and what the full rebuttal might be. This shows how kalam arguments are built and refined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: قالوا: لو فرضنا إلهين، لأمتنع أن يمتاز أحدهما عن الآخر بالزمان، لأنهما قديمان، وبالمكان لما ثبت أن الإله يجب أن يكون منزهاً عن الحجمية والمكان، وإذا لم يحصل الامتياز بينهما، وجب أن لا يبقى التعدد. وقيل عليه: لم لا يجوز أن يتميز أحدهما على الآخر بالماهية والحقيقة؟ ألا ترى أنه إذا حلّ في الجوهر الواحد علم وقدرة، فإنه لم يحصل الامتياز بينهما لا بالزمان ولا بالمكان ولا بالمحل، ولكن حصل الامتياز بينهما بالماهية والحقيقة. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر ههنا كذلك؟ وأجيب عنه: بأنه لو وجد إلهان لامتنع أن يمتاز أحدهما عن الآخر بالزمان لأنهما قديمان وبالمكان لأنهما من الصفات، وأما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن حصل الامتياز بينهما بأمر من الأمور، فذاك الأمر إما أن يكون معتبراً في تحقق الإلهية، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، فإن كان ذلك الأمر معتبراً في الإلهية فالذي لم يحصل له ذلك الأمر، وجب أن لا
The fourth argument: They said: If we suppose two gods, it would be impossible for one to be distinguished from the other by time, because they are eternal, and by place because it is established that the god must be transcendent of corporeality and place. And if distinction between them does not occur, then multiplicity must not remain. And it was objected to it: Why is it not permissible for one to be distinguished from the other by essence (mahiyya) and reality (haqiqa)? Do you not see that when knowledge and power inhere in one substance, distinction between them does not occur by time, place, or location, but distinction occurs by essence and reality. So why is it not permissible for the matter to be likewise here? And it was answered: That if two gods existed, it would be impossible for one to be distinguished from the other by time because they are eternal, and by place because they are attributes?; but maybe it is not so. For if distinction occurs between them by some matter, that matter is either considered in the realization of divinity, or not. If that matter is considered in divinity, then the one for whom that matter does not occur must not...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth argument against two gods and an objection and partial answer. The argument states that if there were two gods, they could not be distinguished by time (since both are eternal) or by place (since God is not spatial). Without any distinction, multiplicity would be impossible. An objector raises the point: distinction could be by essence and reality, just as knowledge and power are distinguished in a single substance by their essence, not by time or place. The answer begins: if two gods existed, they cannot be distinguished by time or place, but the answer is cut off. It seems to consider that the distinguishing feature, if it is essential to divinity, would make the other not divine, or if not essential, then it would be an accident, which cannot apply to God. The passage ends before concluding the argument.

Key Terms
امتياز
زمان
مكان
ماهية
حقيقة
جوهر
إلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could be distinguished by their essence or attributes, just as two different beings are distinct.
Correction: The argument challenges this by showing that any distinguishing feature that is essential to divinity would negate the divinity of the other, and if not essential, it would be an accident, impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument claim that two gods cannot be distinguished by time?
A) Because they are immaterial
B) Because they are both eternal (qadiman)
C) Because time does not exist
D) Because they are omnipresent

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of distinguishing two eternal, non-spatial beings shows that multiplicity among gods is rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument exposes that any distinction between two gods would either negate divinity or be an accident. In your own heart, is there any 'partner' or worldly concern that you implicitly treat as having a divine attribute—like control over your destiny or ultimate worth? How does this logic force you to confront that subtle shirk?
?
The objection uses the example of knowledge and power being distinct in a substance, yet the response shows such a distinction is impossible for God. Have you ever thought of God's attributes as separate parts? What does this argument demand you change about how you conceive of Allah's oneness and simplicity?
?
If you truly internalize that no being can share divinity, what is one relationship, attachment, or fear that you must now place entirely under Allah's sole authority? Be specific: what will you do differently today to apply this truth?
